Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Introduction to Sales: Definition, Proofs, and Placement
Opening of Kitab al-Bay' – placement, linguistic definition, and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Book of Sales follows the Book of Waqf in Hanafi texts, define 'bay‘' linguistically as a word with opposite meanings, and cite the Quranic and Sunnah proofs for the permissibility of sale.

Core Idea
Sale is fundamentally permissible; its term has two opposite meanings, and its legal proof is the Quranic permission.

This lesson opens the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Bay‘) from a classical Hanafi text. The author first explains the structural placement of this chapter after Waqf, then delves into the linguistic peculiarity of the word 'bay‘' – a verbal noun that can mean both selling and buying. Finally, he establishes the legality of sale through explicit Quranic permission and a Prophetic hadith. Understanding these foundations is essential before studying specific rulings of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural placement
Why does the author place the Book of Sales after the Book of Waqf? The passage explains that both involve transfer of ownership: Waqf is simpler (removal only), sale is compound (removal and acquisition). The simple is naturally prior.
This illustrates a common method in Hanafi fiqh: ordering chapters by conceptual simplicity.
2
Analyze the linguistic nature of 'bay‘'
The author states that 'bay‘' is a word with opposite meanings (al-didd). It can mean both selling (removal from ownership) and buying (entry into ownership). The hadith 'Do not sell over the sale of your brother' is interpreted as prohibiting buying over another's purchase, not selling.
This linguistic insight resolves apparent contradictions in texts.
3
Extract the two legal proofs
The author cites two proofs: (1) Quran: 'Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (2:275) – an explicit permission. (2) Sunnah: The hadith 'O merchants, sale is attended by vain speech and oaths, so repent by giving charity' – which acknowledges sale as occurring and advises expiation, not prohibition.
The Quranic proof is qati‘ (definitive), while the hadith is ahad; together they establish permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع
[۱]
كتاب البيع
[١] قوله : كتاب البيع ; لما فرغ المصنف له عن «كتاب الوقف» شرع في «كتاب البيع»; لمناسبة بينهما، وهي : أن الوقف إزالة الموقوفِ عـن مـلـك الواقف، أما على قولهما فظاهر، وأما على قول أبي حنيفة له فبعد حكم الحاكم. وفي البيع إزالة المبيع عن ملك البائع، مع إدخاله في ملك المشتري ، فنُزِّل الوقف في ذلك منزلة البسيط من المركب، والبسيط مقدم على المركب طبعاً، فقدمه وضعاً. ولما كان البيع مصدراً ، والأصل في المصدر أن لا يثنى ولا يجمع، فأورده بلفظ المفرد، ومن جمعه نظراً إلى ما تحته من الأنواع، والبيع من الأضداد، يقال علـى الإخراج عن الملك والإدخال فيه، ومن هذا قوله : «لا يخطبُ الرَّجلُ على خطبة أخيه، ولا يبيع على بيع أخيه ) : أي لا يشتري على شراء أخيه ; لأنَّ المنهي عنه هو الملك قصداً. الشّراءُ لا البيع، ويقع في الغالب على إخراج المبيع عن ملك البائع ويتعدى إلى المفعول الثاني تارة بنفسه، وتارة بحرف الجر، يقال: باعه الشيء، وباع منه، وربما دخلت اللام، فيقال : بعتك الشيء، وبعت لك الشيء، وباع عليه القاضي : أي من غير رضاه ، وكذا الشراء قال الله : وَشَرَوْهُ بِشَمَن بَخْسٍ دَرَاهِمَ مَعْدُودَة ) (۲) : أي باعوه، ويقع غالباً على إخراج الثَّمن عن الملك قصداً.
والبيع مشروع :
. بالكتاب : وهو قوله : ( وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرّبوا ) (۳)، وهذا إنكار لتسوية الكفار بينهما ، حيث قَالُوا إِنَّمَا الْبَيْعُ مِثْلُ الرِّبوا (٤) ، إذ الحل والحرمة ضـدان فـأنـي يتماثلان.

Book of Sales. [1] Book of Sales. [1] His statement: 'Book of Sales'; when its author finished with the Book of Waqf, he began the Book of Sales due to a suitability between them, which is that Waqf is removal of the endowed property from the ownership of the endower. As for according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is apparent; and according to the statement of Abu Hanifa, it is after the judge's ruling. And in sale is removal of the sold item from the ownership of the seller, along with its entry into the ownership of the buyer. So Waqf in that regard is placed at the level of the simple from the compound, and the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so he placed it first in arrangement. And since 'bay'' is a verbal noun, and the principle for a verbal noun is that it is not dualized nor pluralized, he brought it in the singular form. And whoever pluralized it did so considering its types. And 'bay'' is from the words with opposite meanings; it is used for removal from ownership and entry into it. From this is his statement: 'A man should not propose over the proposal of his brother, nor sell over the sale of his brother' meaning: he should not buy over the purchase of his brother, because what is prohibited is ownership intentionally; purchase, not sale. And it mostly occurs for removal of the sold item from the seller's ownership, and it takes a second object sometimes by itself, sometimes with a preposition. It is said: he sold him the thing, and he sold to him; and sometimes the letter lam enters, so it is said: I sold you the thing, and I sold to you the thing. And 'the judge sold upon him' means without his consent. And likewise purchase. Allah said: 'And they sold him for a cheap price, a few dirhams' i.e., they sold him. And it mostly occurs for removal of the price from ownership intentionally. And sale is legislated: By the Book: which is His statement: 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (2:275). And this is a rejection of the disbelievers' equating between them, when they said: 'Sale is only like usury' (2:275), since the permissible and the forbidden are opposites, so how can they be similar? And by the Sunnah: which is his statement: 'O assembly of merchants, indeed sale is attended by vain speech and oaths, so repent from it by giving charity' narrated by Abu Dawud.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement that opens the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Bay‘). The author first explains why the Book of Sales follows the Book of Waqf: because both involve transfer of ownership, with Waqf being the simpler form (removal from ownership) and sale being more complex (both removal and acquisition). The simple is naturally prior, so the author placed Waqf first. He then discusses the linguistic properties of the word 'bay‘', noting it is a verbal noun that can mean both selling and buying (a word with opposite meanings). He cites the hadith 'Do not sell over the sale of your brother' to illustrate that the prohibition actually applies to buying over another’s purchase, because the intended prohibition is on acquiring ownership (buying), not on selling. Finally, he establishes that sale is legislated (mashru‘) through two proofs: the Quranic verse 'Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (2:275), which refutes the disbelievers who equated sale with usury; and the Sunnah, a hadith instructing merchants to give charity because sales involve vain speech and oaths. The Hanafi school holds that sale is fundamentally permissible (mubah), as indicated by the explicit permission in the Quran. The authority weight of this ruling is from the later commentary literature (fatawa mutakhkhirin), but it aligns with the foundational texts (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay‘): A verbal noun meaning both selling and buying, used for the transfer of ownership in exchange for consideration.
الربا (al-riba): Usury, which is prohibited by Quran and Hadith; it is the opposite of halal sale.
الضد (al-didd): A word that has two opposite meanings; al-bay‘ is cited as an example.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author begin the Book of Sales after the Book of Waqf?
A) Because Waqf is mentioned before Sale in the Quran
B) Because both involve transfer of ownership, and Waqf is simpler
C) Because the Prophet ordered that order
D) Because Waqf is a type of sale

Key Takeaway
Sale is fundamentally permissible (mubah) by Quran and Sunnah; the word 'bay‘' has opposite meanings, which is crucial for correctly understanding related hadith.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Legitimacy of Sale: Quranic, Prophetic, Ijma', and Rational Proofs
Opens the Book of Sale with linguistic preliminaries and four proofs for permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four proofs for the legality of sale and explain how each proof demonstrates permissibility according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Sale is fundamentally permissible – Allah's explicit permission in the Quran settles the matter.

The Book of Sale begins by establishing that sale is a legitimate contract. The commentator explains its placement after Waqf due to shared ownership transfer, then clarifies the linguistic scope of 'bay'' as a word with opposite meanings. The core message is the permissibility of sale, supported by four categories of evidence: the Quranic verse 'Allah has permitted sale', Prophetic hadiths, consensus of the Ummah, and rational reasoning based on human need and mercy. This lesson unpacks each proof and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proofs
Read the passage and list the four proofs cited for the legislation of sale: the Quran, the Sunnah, the Ijma' of the Ummah, and rational reasoning (ma'qul).
These are the standard sources of law in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof
Focus on the verse 'Wa ahalla Allahu al-bay'a wa harrama al-riba' (2:275). The explicit permission ('ahalla') indicates the ruling of ibahah (permissibility), refuting those who equate sale with riba.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut (certain transmission) and qat'i al-dalalah (clear meaning) in establishing permissibility.
3
Examine the Prophetic proof
Two hadiths are cited: one advising merchants to give charity because sale involves idle talk and oaths, and the Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir) of people trading. Both establish the legality of sale through prophetic practice.
The hadith about charity does not prohibit sale; it encourages repentance for unavoidable minor sins.
4
Understand the rational proof
The rational argument: People need various goods, not all are available everywhere, and forced acquisition leads to corruption. Therefore, Allah legislated voluntary exchange of property as a mercy.
This is an istihsan argument based on public interest (maslahah).
5
Connect to Hanafi rulings
These proofs collectively establish that sale is mubah (permissible) by default, not obligatory or prohibited. Any specific prohibitions (e.g., riba, gharar) are exceptions that require separate evidence.
The mufta bihi position is that sale is halal unless a prohibited element is proven.

Book of Sale [1] Book of Sale [1] His saying: Book of Sale; when the author finished the Book of Waqf, he started the Book of Sale due to an appropriateness between them, which is: that waqf is removal of the endowed property from the ownership of the endower. As for their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view it is apparent, and as for the view of Abu Hanifa, it is after the judge's ruling. In sale, it is removal of the sold item from the seller's ownership while entering it into the buyer's ownership. So waqf in this regard was placed like the simple in relation to the compound, and the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so he presented it in arrangement. Since sale is a verbal noun, and originally verbal nouns are not dualized or pluralized, he presented it in the singular form. Whoever pluralized it considered the types under it. Sale is among words with opposite meanings, used for removal from ownership and entry into it. From this is his saying: 'A man should not propose upon the proposal of his brother, nor sell upon the sale of his brother' meaning: should not buy upon the purchase of his brother, because what is prohibited is ownership intentionally, purchase not sale. It commonly occurs for removal of the sold item from ownership and it sometimes takes two objects by itself, sometimes with a preposition. It is said: he sold him the thing, and he sold from him, and sometimes the article enters, so it is said: I sold you the thing, and I sold for you the thing. The judge sold against him: meaning without his consent. Similarly for purchase Allah said: 'And they sold him for a low price, a number of dirhams' (12:20): meaning they sold him. It commonly occurs for removal of the price from ownership intentionally. And sale is legislated: By the Book: He said: 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (2:275), and this is a denial of the disbelievers' equating between them when they said: 'Sale is only like usury' (2:275), since permissible and prohibited are opposites, so how can they be similar. By the Sunnah: He said: 'O merchants, sale is attended by idle talk and oaths, so repent with charity,' reported by Abu Dawud. And from the hadith of Qays ibn 'Uzarah (may Allah be pleased with him): likewise, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was sent while people were trading, and he approved them upon that, and approval is a type of Sunnah. And the Ummah has unanimously agreed on its legislation. By reason: That Allah made ownership a means for fulfilling the interests of creation, and prescribed trade as a way to earn, and everything that every person needs is not found permissible in every place, and taking by way of oppression leads to corruption, so Allah prescribed taking wealth for wealth by mutual consent as a kindness to creation, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) encouraged it as reported from Rafi' ibn Khadij who said: 'It was said, O Messenger of Allah, which earning is best? He said: The work of a man with his hand and every sale that is approved' reported by Ahmad.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, opening the 'Kitab al-Bay'' (Book of Sale). The commentator, after completing the Book of Waqf, explains why the author placed the Book of Sale next: both involve transfer of ownership, with waqf being simpler (removal only, or with court order on Abu Hanifa’s view) and sale being a compound (removal plus entry). The word 'bay'' is a verbal noun, thus singular, though some pluralize it by considering its types. Interestingly, 'bay'' is an 'addad' (word with opposite meanings), used for both selling (removal from ownership) and buying (entry into ownership). The commentator proves this with the hadith 'do not sell upon the sale of your brother' meaning do not buy upon his purchase, as the prohibition targets acquiring ownership. The principal proof for the legality of sale is Quran 2:275, which explicitly permits sale and prohibits riba, refuting those who equate them. Additional proofs: the hadith advising merchants to give charity because sale involves idle talk and oaths, the Prophet’s tacit approval (taqrir) of people trading, Ijma' (consensus of the Ummah), and a rational argument: Allah made property essential for human welfare and trade as a means of lawful acquisition; since not everything is available everywhere, forced extraction leads to harm, so Allah legislated voluntary exchange of property for property out of mercy.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): A contract of exchange involving transfer of ownership; used for both selling (removal from one's ownership) and buying (entry into one's ownership) as it is an 'addad (word with opposite meanings).
الأضداد (al-addad): Words that have opposite meanings; here, 'bay'' is such a word, used for both sale and purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sale is fundamentally permissible (halal) as established by the Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', and rational reasoning.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Definition of Sale: Exchange of Property for Property
Defines the sale contract and explains its elements through commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sale and apply it to distinguish sale from pawn and gift with compensation.

Core Idea
Sale is an exchange of property for property on the basis of ownership transfer, not a donation.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the concise definition of sale given in the base text. Understanding this definition is essential because it determines when a transaction qualifies as a sale. By analyzing the words 'mubadala' and the implied 'tamlik', you will learn why certain transactions like pawn (rahn) and gift with compensation (hiba bi shart al-'iwad) are excluded from the definition of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verbal phrase and its grammatical construction
The base text says 'huwa mubadala maal bi maal' – a verbal noun (masdar) with the subject omitted. The 'first object' is 'maal' and 'second object' is 'bi maal'. This indicates mutual exchange between two parties.
The commentator points out the grammatical structure: the verb is transitive with two objects.
2
Note the implied element of tamlik (ownership transfer)
The commentator states that the definition implies tamlik. This is crucial because it excludes transactions that do not transfer ownership, such as pawn (rahn) where the pawned property is not transferred in ownership.
Tamlik is the intended legal effect of a sale.
3
Understand the exclusion of gift with compensation
Even though a gift with condition of compensation (hiba bi shart al-'iwad) results in an exchange, at its inception it is pure donation, not an exchange. Therefore, it falls outside the definition of sale.
The commentator argues that adding conditions like 'for trade' would be unnecessary; the simple definition already excludes gift.
4
Contrast with definitions of other schools
Hanafis define sale as mubadala maal bi maal without adding 'aqd (contract) explicitly, while Shafi'is often add 'aqd tamlik. The Hanafi definition is broader, covering all exchanges of property.
This difference can affect what transactions are considered sales.
5
Apply the definition to real transactions
Consider a transaction: A gives B a watch and says 'Take this as a gift, but give me your phone in return.' This starts as a gift, not a sale, even though it ends with an exchange. The definition focuses on the inception.
Knowing the exact boundaries prevents misclassification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هُو مُبَادَلَةٌ مَال بِمَال
(هُو مُبَادَلَةٌ مَال بِمَال
[١] قوله : مبادلة مال بمال مصدر مضاف إلى مفعوله الأول، والفاعل محذوف، والتقدير أن تبادل المتبايعان مالاً ،بمال فمال مفعول أوّل، وبمال مفعول ثان . حرف الجر والمبادلة : باهم معاوضة كردن. كذا في «الصراح»(4).
فتكون على وجه التمليك فلا حاجة إلى تقديره ؛ ولإخراج الرهن كما فعله بعضهم، وهذا احتراز عن الهبة، ولو كانت بعوض، فإن الهبة بشرط العوض وإن كانت في حكم البيع بقاء لكنها في ابتداء العقد تبرّع محض لا مبادلة، فلا حاجة لإخراجه إلى قيد التجارة أو الاكتساب كما فعله بعضُهم.
It is an exchange of property for property. (It is an exchange of property for property.) [1] His saying: exchange of property for property is a verbal noun annexed to its first object, with the agent omitted, and the estimation is that the two transactors exchange property for property, so 'property' is first object and 'for property' is second object. The preposition and 'mubadalah' (exchange) means mutual compensation in Persian. Like this in 'al-Sirah'. So it is a form of ownership transfer (tamlik), so there is no need to estimate it; and to exclude pawn (rahn) as some have done, and this is a precaution against gift (hibah), even if it is with compensation, because the gift with a condition of compensation, though in the remaining rulings it is like a sale, in the beginning of the contract it is pure donation, not exchange, so there is no need to exclude it by adding the condition of commerce or earning as some have done.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the sale contract as a legal term. The base text states 'huwa mubadala maal bi maal' (It is an exchange of property for property). The commentator expands: this is a verbal noun (masdar) with the subject omitted, meaning the two parties exchange property for each other. The phrase 'on the basis of tamlik' (ownership transfer) is implicit, which excludes pawn (rahn) because pawn does not transfer ownership. It also excludes gift (hibah) even if conditional on compensation, because at the inception the gift is a pure donation, not an exchange. Therefore, the definition 'exchange of property for property' precisely captures sale. There is no need to add extra conditions like 'for the purpose of trade' as some later scholars did. The commentator also references a Persian dictionary for the meaning of mubadalah.

Key Terms
مبادلة مال بمال (mubadala maal bi maal): The definition of sale: a mutual exchange of property for property, implying a contract of ownership transfer (tamlik).
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership; in the context of sale, it is the intended effect of the exchange, distinguishing it from other transactions like pawn or gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A gift with condition of compensation
B) A cash purchase for immediate delivery
C) A barter trade of two goods
D) A credit sale with deferred payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sale as 'exchange of property for property' inherently excludes transactions that begin as donations, such as gift with compensation.

Definition of Sale and Its Legitimacy
Defines sale and provides scriptural and rational proofs of its legitimacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sale and identify the scriptural and rational proofs for its legitimacy.

Core Idea
Sale is an exchange of property for property, affirmed by the Prophet, consensus, and human need.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi definition of a sale (bay') and its foundational proofs. Understanding this definition is critical because it excludes transactions that appear similar but are legally distinct, such as gifts with compensation and pledges. We will also examine how the definition implies mutual consent and ownership transfer, and how the legitimacy of sale is established through Qur'anic practice, hadith, consensus, and rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Locate the statement 'huwa mubadalah mal bi mal' (It is an exchange of property for property). This is the core legal definition of sale in the Hanafi school.
The definition is concise and uses the verbal noun mubadalah to emphasize mutual exchange.
2
Analyze the Grammatical Structure
The phrase 'mubadalah mal bi mal' is a verbal noun annexed to its first object. The subject (the two contracting parties) is omitted, and the two objects are 'mal' (first) and 'bi mal' (second). This implies that each party gives property to the other.
This grammatical analysis shows that the definition inherently involves two parties and two properties.
3
Recognize the Exclusions
The commentator explains that the definition excludes pledges (rahn) because a pledge does not transfer ownership, and gifts with compensation (hibah bi-shart al-'iwad) because they begin as donations, not exchanges.
Thus, the definition is precise enough to distinguish sale from other property transfers.
4
Examine the Proofs of Legitimacy
The author cites four proofs: (1) Prophetic taqrir (tacit approval) – the Prophet affirmed the Companions' trading; (2) Ijma' (consensus) of the Ummah; (3) Rational proof – property is necessary for human welfare, and coercion leads to corruption, so mutual exchange is legislated; (4) Hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij encouraging 'every blessed sale.'
These proofs establish that sale is fundamentally permissible (mubah) in Islamic law.
5
Understand the Legal Implications
The definition automatically implies ownership transfer; thus no additional wording like 'tamlik' is needed. It also excludes contracts that are not exchanges at inception.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from carefully crafted definitions.

It is an exchange of property for property.
It is an exchange of property for property.
From the hadith of Qays ibn 'Azrah: 'Indeed, the Messenger of Allah ﷺ sent [emissaries] while people were trading, and he affirmed them upon that.' And affirmation is one of the types of Sunnah.
The ummah is in consensus that it is legislated.
And by rational proof: That Allah made property a means for fulfilling the interests of the servants, and He legislated trade as a means of earning. And everything that everyone needs is not available permissible in every place, and taking by way of coercion leads to corruption. So Allah legislated taking property for property with mutual consent as a kindness to the servants. And the Messenger of Allah ﷺ encouraged it, as narrated from Rafi' ibn Khadij who said: 'It was said: O Messenger of Allah, which earning is best? He said: A man's work with his hand and every sale that is blessed (mabrur).' Narrated by Ahmad.
His saying: 'exchange of property for property' is a verbal noun annexed to its first object, with the subject omitted. The estimation is that the two contracting parties exchange property for property. So 'property' is the first object, and 'with property' is the second object (with the preposition). Al-mubadalah means mutual exchange. As stated in al-Sirah.
Thus it is on the basis of ownership transfer, so there is no need for estimation; and to exclude pledge (rahn) as some have done. And this is a precaution against gift (hibah) even if it is with compensation, for hibah with the condition of compensation, although in the remaining effect it is in the ruling of sale, but at the initiation of the contract it is pure donation, not an exchange. So there is no need to exclude it by adding the qualification of trade or earning as some have done.

This passage presents the definition of sale (bay') as an exchange of property for property, a definition that is foundational in Hanafi fiqh. The author then provides evidence for its legislation: the practice of the Companions affirmed by the Prophet (تقرير), the consensus of the Ummah, and a rational argument based on human need and prevention of harm. The hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij encourages every blessed sale. Then the commentator explains the grammatical structure of the definition and its legal implications: it excludes gifts (even those with compensation) because they are not exchanges at inception, and it excludes pledges (rahn) because a pledge is not a transfer of ownership. The definition automatically implies ownership transfer, so no extra wording is needed.

Key Terms

مال (mal): Property or wealth that has value and is subject to ownership.
تراضي (taradi): Mutual consent, a condition for the validity of a contract.
تقرير (taqrir): Prophetic affirmation of a companion's action, considered a form of Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Sale is a fundamentally permissible contract in Islam, defined as an exchange of property for property with mutual consent, and its legitimacy is established by prophetic approval, consensus, and rational necessity.

The Hanafi Definition of Wealth (Mal) and Mutaqawwim
Definition of key terms for property and sale contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'mal' and 'mutaqawwim', and apply the conditions of tamawwul and ibahat al-intifa' to classify items as property or not.

Core Idea
For an item to be saleable property (mal mutaqawwim), it must be both customarily valued and Sharia-permissible.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi definition of 'mal' (wealth/property) as found in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. The passage explains that the term has a linguistic and a legal dimension. Legally, two conditions must be met for something to be considered property that can be sold: people must customarily consider it as property (tamawwul), and its benefit must be permissible in Sharia (ibahat al-intifa'). This distinction is crucial for understanding which items can be validly traded, especially in contracts like sale (buyu').

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Note the source's two-part approach: first a linguistic gloss ('that which is owned'), then a detailed legal definition from al-Kashf. The legal definition adds two conditions: natural inclination and storability, but the commentator expands these into the twin criteria of tamawwul and ibahat al-intifa'.
2
Understand the two establishing factors for maliyyah
Property value (maliyyah) is established either by the general public considering something as property (tamawwul al-nas kaffatan) or by some people valuing it (taqawwum al-ba'd). Recognize that the latter (taqawwum) also requires Sharia permissibility.
3
Apply the conditions to the examples given
Work through each example: carrion (no tamawwul, no ibaha) → neither mal nor mutaqawwim; a grain of wheat (ibaha present but no tamawwul) → not mal; wine (tamawwul present but no ibaha) → mal but not mutaqawwim for Muslims. Note: the pig is also in this category.
4
Resolve the definitional ambiguity for sale
The commentator notes that jurists customarily use 'mal' to mean 'mal mutaqawwim' when discussing sale. This is because the subject of a sale must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Custom (urf) serves as a contextual indicator (qarina) that restricts the general term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمال لغة : خواسته وایخه در ملك كسي باشد كذا في «المنتهى الأرب»، وقال صاحب «البحر» ناقلا عن «الكشف» : «المال : ما يميل إليه الطبع ويمكن ادخاره لوقت الحاجة، والمالية : إنما تثبت بتمول الناس كافة ، أو بتقوم البعض، والتقوم : يثبت بها وبإباحة الانتفاع به شرعاً.
فما يكون مباح الانتفاع بدون تمول الناس لا يكون مالاً كحبة حنطة، وما يكون مالاً بين الناس ولا يكون مباح الانتفاع لا يكون متقوماً كالخمر، وإذا عدم الأمران لم يثبت واحد منهما كالدم». انتهى.
فخرج به الميتة والدم وحبة الحنطة وشعيرة وكف تراب وشربة ماء مما هو ليس مال، وبقي الخمر والخنزير وغيرهما مما هو مال لكنه غير متقوم للمسلم، اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد بالمال ما هو محل البيع، ومحله مال متقوّم كما هو العُرف عند الفقهاء، والعرف يكون قرينة.
Mal linguistically: property and what is in someone's ownership. As stated in 'al-Muntaha al-Arab'. The author of 'al-Bahr' said quoting from 'al-Kashf': 'Mal: that which the nature inclines to and can be stored for the time of need. And property value is established either by the general public considering it as property, or by its being valuable to some. And value is established by that and by the permissibility of benefiting from it according to Sharia. So what is permissible to benefit from without people considering it property is not mal, like a grain of wheat. And what is considered property among people but is not permissible to benefit from is not valuable, like wine. And if both are absent, neither is established, like blood.' End.
Thus excluded are carrion, blood, a grain of wheat, a grain of barley, a handful of dust, a sip of water – things that are not property. And wine, pig, and other things that are property but not valuable for a Muslim remain, unless it is said that the meaning of mal is what is subject to sale, and its subject is valuable property as is the custom among jurists, and custom is a contextual indicator.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'مال' (wealth/property) from both linguistic and legal perspectives. Linguistically, it is what is owned. Legally, it requires two elements: (1) the natural inclination of people to desire it and its storability, and (2) customary valuation by society or some people. Additionally, for a thing to be legally valuable (متقوم) in Islamic law, its benefit must be permissible (مباح). The passage uses examples: a single grain of wheat is not considered 'mal' because people do not generally value it as property, even though it is permissible to benefit from it. Wine and pigs are considered 'mal' by people (they have material value) but are not 'متقوم' for Muslims because their use is prohibited. Blood lacks both conditions. The commentator then applies these principles to determine what can be subject to sale, concluding that only valuable property (مال متقوم) can be sold, and this is determined by custom (عرف) among jurists.

Key Terms
مال (mal): That which people naturally incline to and can be stored for future need.
مالية (maliyyah): Property value, established by the general public considering something as property or by some people valuing it.
تقوم (taqawwum): Legal value in Sharia, requiring permissibility of benefit.
تمول (tamawwul): People's consideration of something as property.
إباحة انتفاع (ibahat al-intifa'): The permissibility of benefiting from something according to Sharia.

Key Takeaway
For an item to be saleable property (mal mutaqawwim) in Hanafi law, it must be both customarily valued and its benefit permissible in Sharia.

Formation of Sale by Offer and Acceptance
States ruling and defines terms with commentary resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of in'iqqad, ijab, and qabul in Hanafi fiqh and explain why offer and acceptance are the material cause of a sale contract.

Core Idea
Offer and acceptance are the material cause of a sale; without them, no contract exists.

Every sale contract begins with two statements: one from each party. The Hanafi school teaches that these statements are not just formalities—they are the very substance of the contract. This passage defines these terms and resolves a subtle question: are offer and acceptance inside or outside the contract? Understanding this distinction is key to knowing when a sale is validly formed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that a sale (bay') is concluded (yan'iqid) by offer and acceptance. The ruling is that the contract is formed (mubah in its permissibility). First, note the act: the formation of a sale contract. The hukm level is mubah (permissible) when done voluntarily between competent parties.
2
Define the key terms
The passage defines three terms: in'iqqad (formation), ijab (offer), and qabul (acceptance). In'iqqad is the joining of the speech of one party to the other. Ijab is the first statement; qabul is the second. The offer is called 'ijab' because it makes the other party have the option to accept.
In Hanafi usul, ijab is binding on the offeror until the session ends or acceptance occurs.
3
Understand the material cause (illah madiyyah)
The author explicitly states that offer and acceptance are the material cause of the contract. This means they are essential components—like the bricks of a building. The passage notes a potential confusion: the definition of sale as 'exchange of property' might suggest offer/acceptance are external instruments. The commentary resolves this by explaining that the sale is the whole composed of offer and acceptance, and the phrase 'exchange of property' should be interpreted as 'exchange of some property' (ba' meaning min), thus incorporating them.
This distinction matters for validity: missing either element means no contract.
4
Apply to a simple scenario
If A says 'I sell you this book for $10' (ijab) and B says 'I accept' (qabul), the contract is formed. The offer and acceptance are the material cause. Without the second statement, there is no contract—only an offer.
The session must continue for the acceptance to be validly connected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينعقد بإيجاب وقبول
ينعقد بإيجاب وقبول
قوله : ينعقد بإيجاب وقبول ؛ الانعقاد : عبارة عن انضمام كلام أحد المتعاقدين إلى الآخر.
والإيجاب: كلام أول من يتكلم من المتعاقدين حال إنشاء البيع ، سمي بالإيجاب مبالغة لكونه موجباً ومثبتاً للآخر خيار القبول. والقبول : كلام ثاني من يتكلم منهما في تلك الحال، وظاهر الكلام مشعر بأن الإيجاب والقبول خارجان عن البيع وآلتان له مع أن المصنف له قد صرح هاهنا بكونهما علة مادية.
وفي «التوضيح»: «بأن البيع هو المجموع المركب من الإيجاب والقبول الموجودين حساً، والمرتبطين ارتباطاً حكمياً، فالتأويل أن الباء في قوله : «مال»، بمعنى: «من»، فلا حاجة إلى ما قرره أخي جلبي، وهاهنا أبحاث لطيفة مودعة في المبسوطات.
It is concluded by offer and acceptance.
It is concluded by offer and acceptance.
His saying: 'It is concluded by offer and acceptance.' Al-in'iqqad: a term for the joining of the speech of one contracting party to the other.
Al-ijab: the speech of the first speaker among the contracting parties at the moment of initiating the sale. It is called 'ijab' (making obligatory) as an exaggeration because it makes the other party have the option of acceptance. And al-qabul: the speech of the second speaker among them at that moment. The apparent wording suggests that the offer and acceptance are external to the contract and instruments for it, while the author has explicitly stated here that they are the material cause.
And in al-Tawdih: 'The sale is the composite whole consisting of the offer and acceptance existing sensibly and connected by a legal connection. So the interpretation is that the ba' in his word "mal" is in the meaning of "min", so there is no need for what Akhi Jilabi determined. And here are subtle investigations deposited in the extended works.'
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage explains that the contract of sale is formed (انعقاد) by an offer (إيجاب) and acceptance (قبول). The commentator defines these terms: in'iqqad is the joining of the speech of the two parties; ijab is the first statement (by whoever speaks first) that initiates the sale, and it is called 'ijab' because it fixes the other party's right of acceptance; qabul is the second statement. A subtle issue arises: the definition of sale as 'exchange of property' (مبادلة مال بمال) might suggest that offer and acceptance are external to the contract, but the author says they are the material cause (علة مادية) of the contract. The commentator resolves this by citing al-Tawdih, which states that the sale is the composite whole of offer and acceptance, so the preposition 'ba' in 'mal' should be interpreted as 'min' (partitive), meaning exchange of some property for some property, thus incorporating the offer and acceptance as essential components.

Key Terms
انعقاد (in'iqqad): The formation of a contract by the joining of the speech of the two parties.
إيجاب (ijab): The first statement made by one contracting party to initiate the contract.
قبول (qabul): The second statement made by the other party indicating agreement.
علة مادية (illah madiyyah): A material cause; in contract law, the essential elements that constitute the contract itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Offer and acceptance are the material cause of a sale contract; without both, no valid contract exists.

Requirement of Past Tense for Offer and Acceptance in Sale
Ruling on the formation of sale contract through offer and acceptance with past-tense verbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that offer and acceptance in a sale must be expressed in the past tense, explain the proof based on Shari'ah practice, and distinguish the position of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Past tense verbs are required for a valid sale because the Shari'ah used them to indicate decisive existence.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the Hanafi school requires both the offer and acceptance in a sale contract to be in the past tense. Understanding this requirement is crucial because it determines when a sale is validly concluded. The passage also addresses a common objection and presents the rationale behind the rule.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Understand what 'in'iqaad' (conclusion), 'ijab' (offer), and 'qabul' (acceptance) mean in the context of a sale contract.
In'iqaad is the joining of the two speeches; ijab is the first speech, qabul the second.
2
Identify the condition
Note that the sale is concluded by past-tense verbs. The passage states: 'It is concluded by two past-tense expressions.'
3
Examine the proof
Why does the Shari'ah require past tense? Because it indicates actual occurrence and is conclusive, unlike future tense which is a promise.
The language-giver did not assign specific words for sale; the Shari'ah used past tense.
4
Respond to the objection
If the seller says 'Take this for a thousand' (imperative), the sale is still concluded by implication, as the imperative implies a past-tense offer.
The conclusion is not by 'take' but by the implied past tense.
5
Note the divergent opinion
Imam al-Shafi'i does not require past tense; he accepts any expression of mutual consent.
This difference has practical consequences for contract validity.

Source Text

صاحب «البحر»

فخرج به الميتة والدم وحبة الحنطة وشعيرة وكف تراب وشربة ماء مما هو ليس مال، وبقي الخمر والخنزير وغيرهما مما هو مال لكنه غير متقوم للمسلم، اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد بالمال ما هو محل البيع، ومحله مال متقوّم كما هو العُرف عند الفقهاء، والعرف يكون قرينة.
[١] قوله : ينعقد بإيجاب وقبول ؛ الانعقاد : عبارة عن انضمام كــلام أحد المتعاقدين إلى الآخر.
والإيجاب: كلام أوَّلِ مَن يتكلم من المتعاقدين حالَ إنشاء البيع ، سُمِّي بالإيجاب مبالغة لكونه موجباً ومثبتاً للآخر خيار القبول. والقبولُ : كلام ثاني من يتكلم منهما في تلك الحال، وظاهر الكلام مشعر بأنَّ الإيجاب والقبول خارجان عن البيع وآلتان له مع أنَّ المصنف له قد صرح هاهنا بكونهما علة مادية. 6
وفي «التوضيح»: «بأنَّ البيع هو المجموع المركب من الإيجاب والقبول الموجودين حسا()، والمرتبطين ارتباطاً حكميّاً ، فالتأويل أنَّ الباء في قوله : «مال»، بمعنى: «من»، فلا حاجة إلى ما قرره أخي جلبي (۳) ، وهاهنا أبحاث لطيفة مودعة في المبسوطات.
[۱] قوله : بلفظي ماض ؛ ولا بُدَّ أن يكون أحدهما يدلُّ على الإيجاب : كأعطيتك وجعلت لك هذا بكذا، أو على القبول : كأجزتُ وأخذت، وإنما شرط المضي فيهما ؛ لأن البيع إنشاء تصرُّف، والإنشاء يعرفُ بالشَّرع ؛ لأنَّ الواضع لم يضع له لفظاً خاصاً. والشرع استعمل فيه اللفظ الذي وضع للإخبار عن الماضي ؛ لأنه يستدعي سبق المخبر عنه ؛ ليكون الكلام صحيحاً ، فكان أوّل من غيره على تحقق الوجود، فكان أنسب بالإنشاء المحصل للوجود ؛ ولأنَّ الماضي إيجاب وقطع، والمستقبلُ عِدَةٌ وأمر وتوكيل ؛ فلهذا انعقد بالماضي.
فإن قيل : لو قال : خذ هذا بألف ، فقبل المشتري ، انعقد البيع؟
أجيب : بأن انعقاده ليس بلفظ : «خذ»، بل بالماضي الثَّابِتِ اقتضاء ؛ لأنه لما أمرَهُ بالأخذ بألف ، وليس له ولاية ذلك إلا بالبيع، فصار كأنه قال : بعتك هذا بألف فخذه ؛ لذا قال في «الدراية»: ولا ينعقد إن كان أحد اللفظين مستقبلاً نحو أن يقول البائع : أبيعك ، اشترِ مني، فقال المشتري : اشتريت، أو يقول المشتري : بعني، فقال البائع : بعث. كذا في «الكافي». وفي «شرح الطحاوي»: إذا كان أحد اللفظين مضارعاً إن أراد بالمضارع الحال فيعتمد ، وإن أراد به الاستقبال والوعد لا ينعقد. وذكر البرْجَنْدِي ناقلاً عـــن «التحفة»(۱) : إنه باللفظين الماضيين ينعقد بدون نيّة الإيجاب الحالي ، وأما إذا كان أحدهما بلفظ المستقبل فلا ينعقد إلا بنية الإيجاب. وقال صاحب «القُنْية»(۲) : «هذا الفقه، وهو أنَّ الشَّرعَ جعل الإيجاب والقبول علامة الرضاء، والإخبار عن الحال أدل على الرّضاءِ وقـت العـقـد مـن الماضي». وإن شئت التحقيق فارجع إلى «البحر الرائق»(۳)، و«فتح القدير»(٤)
It is concluded by offer and acceptance. It is concluded by offer and acceptance. The author of al-Bahr: 'And wealth in language: property and anything that is owned by someone, as in al-Muntaha al-Arab. And he said, quoting from al-Kashf: 'Wealth: that which the nature inclines to and can be stored for time of need. And wealthiness is established only by the valuation of all people, or by the valuation of some. And valuation is established by it and by the permissibility of benefiting from it legally. So what is permissible to benefit from without valuation by people is not wealth, like a grain of wheat; and what is wealth among people but not permissible to benefit from is not valuable, like wine; and if both attributes are absent, neither is established, like blood.' End. So by this, carrion, blood, a grain of wheat, a barley grain, a handful of dust, and a drink of water are excluded because they are not wealth. And wine, pig, and others remain which are wealth but not valuable for a Muslim, unless it is said that the intended meaning of wealth is that which is the subject of sale, and its subject is valuable wealth as is the custom among jurists, and custom is a contextual indicator. [1] His statement: 'It is concluded by offer and acceptance'; al-in'iqaad: expression of the joining of the speech of one of the two contracting parties to the other. And offer: the speech of the first speaker among the contracting parties at the moment of initiating the sale; it is called offer (ijab) by way of exaggeration because it causes and establishes for the other the option of acceptance. And acceptance: the speech of the second speaker among them in that situation. And the apparent meaning of the speech indicates that offer and acceptance are external to the sale and are instruments for it, while the author has stated here that they are material cause. In al-Tawdih: 'The sale is the composite whole consisting of offer and acceptance existing physically and connected legally. So the interpretation is that the preposition 'ba' in his statement 'by offer and acceptance' is in the sense of 'from', so there is no need for the analysis of Akhi Jilabi.' And there are subtle investigations deposited in the detailed works. [1] His statement: 'By two past-tense expressions'; and it is necessary that one of them indicates the offer: like 'I give you' and 'I made this for you for such', or indicates acceptance: like 'I accept' and 'I took'. The condition of past tense in both is because sale is an initiation of a disposition, and initiation is known by the Shari'ah; because the language-giver did not assign a specific word for it. The Shari'ah used the word that was placed for reporting about the past, because it requires that the reported event precede the speech for the speech to be valid, so it was more appropriate for initiation that brings about existence; and because the past tense is conclusive and decisive, while the future is a promise, command, and delegation; therefore it is concluded by the past tense. If it is said: If he says, 'Take this for a thousand,' and the buyer accepts, is the sale concluded? The answer: Its conclusion is not by the word 'take', but by the past tense established by implication; because when he ordered him to take for a thousand, and he has no authority for that except by sale, it becomes as if he said, 'I sold you this for a thousand, so take it.' Hence it is stated in al-Diraya: 'It is not concluded if one of the two expressions is in the future tense, like when the seller says, 'I am selling you,' 'Buy from me,' and the buyer says, 'I bought,' or the buyer says, 'Sell to me,' and the seller says, 'I sold.' Likewise in al-Kafi. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If one of the two expressions is a present tense verb; if he intends by the present tense the present time, it is relied upon; but if he intends future and promise, it is not concluded.' And al-Birjandi mentioned, quoting from al-Tuhfah: 'The sale is concluded by two past-tense expressions without the intention of present offer, but if one of them is in the future tense, it is not concluded except with the intention of offer.' And the author of al-Qunyah said: 'This is the true jurisprudence: that the Shari'ah has made offer and acceptance a sign of consent, and informing about the present state is more indicative of consent at the time of the contract than the past.' If you want verification, refer to al-Bahr al-Raiq and Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the text of al-Wiqayah, expanding on the formation of a sale contract. The commentator begins by defining the key terms: 'in'iqaad' (conclusion) means the joining of the speech of two contracting parties; 'ijab' (offer) is the speech of the first speaker in the sale transaction, called 'ijab' because it creates the option of acceptance for the other; 'qabul' (acceptance) is the speech of the second speaker. The apparent wording suggests that offer and acceptance are external to the sale, but the author clarifies that they are the material cause (illah madaniyyah) of the sale—meaning they are integral parts of the contract, not separate instruments. The commentator then addresses the requirement that the verbs be in the past tense. He explains that sale is an initiation (insha') of a disposition, and initiation is defined by the Shari'ah. Since the language-giver did not assign specific words for sale, the Shari'ah used words originally for reporting past events because past-tense verbs imply that the action has occurred, making them more suitable for creating immediate legal effects. Past tense is decisive and final, while future tense is a promise, command, or delegation, which does not effect a present transfer. The passage then responds to a potential objection: if a seller says 'Take this for a thousand' (using an imperative), and the buyer accepts, the sale is still concluded, but the conclusion is not by the word 'take' itself; rather, by implication, the imperative implies a past-tense offer because the seller cannot command the buyer to take unless ownership is transferred, so it is as if he said, 'I sold you this for a thousand, so take it.' The commentator cites various authorities: al-Diraya, al-Kafi, Sharh al-Tahawi, al-Tuhfah, and al-Qunyah, all affirming the requirement of past tense or, in certain cases, that present tense may suffice if it indicates the present moment. The passage concludes by directing the reader to more detailed works for deeper investigation. This discussion is foundational for understanding how a valid sale contract is formed in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الإنعقاد (al-in'iqaad): The legal conclusion of a contract, achieved by the joining of the offer and acceptance.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The speech of the first contracting party in the sale transaction, which offers the item and creates for the other party the right of acceptance.
القبول (al-qabul): The speech of the second contracting party in the sale transaction, indicating acceptance of the offer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why must the offer and acceptance in a sale be expressed in the past tense?
A) Because the past tense is grammatically more correct.
B) Because the Shari'ah used past tense verbs for contracts, as they indicate decisive existence suitable for creating immediate legal effects.
C) Because the buyer must confirm the sale after it has taken place.
D) Because the past tense allows for more flexibility in contract terms.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a sale contract is validly concluded only when both offer and acceptance are expressed in past tense verbs (or their implied equivalents), based on the Shari'ah's usage and the need for decisive existence.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Past Tense Requirement for Offer and Acceptance in Sale
Explains the condition of past tense in sale contract formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for past tense verbs in sale contracts, identify the proofs for this requirement, and explain the intra-Hanafi differences regarding future tense usage.

Core Idea
Sale contracts require past tense verbs because they indicate finality and actual existence, while future tense is merely a promise.

In this lesson, we examine why the Hanafi school insists on past tense verbs for offer and acceptance in a sale contract. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides both the rational proof (istidlal) and addresses objections and internal differences within the school. You will learn the primary rule, the legal reasoning behind it, and the nuances when future tense is used with intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal rule
The author states that sale is concluded with two past-tense verbs. One for offer (ijab) and one for acceptance (qabul). Identify what constitutes valid offer and acceptance words.
Examples: 'I gave you' (أعطيتك) for offer, 'I accepted' (قبلت) for acceptance.
2
Understand the proof
The past tense is used because sale is a creation of a transaction (insha'). The Shariah used the past tense, which is originally for informing about the past, because it indicates actual existence and finality – suitable for creating a legal effect.
The proof is rational (istidlal): the past tense signifies obligation and cutting off (qat'), while future indicates promise and delegation.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised: if the seller says 'Take this for a thousand' and the buyer accepts, is the sale valid? The response: it is valid by implication – the imperative implies a past tense offer because ordering to take for a price only makes sense through sale.
This shows that the requirement of past tense can be satisfied by necessity (iqtiḍā').
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Birjandi narrates from al-Tuhfah that with two past tenses no intention is needed, but if one is future, intention of present state is required. The author of al-Qunyah argues that future tense can indicate present state and is more indicative of consent.
This is a legitimate difference within the Hanafi school.
5
Explore the exception: father's transaction
The author addresses that a father can conclude a sale for his minor child with a single expression. Because of his affection and guardianship, his statement is elevated to two expressions.
This is an exception for the father's role as guardian (wali).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : بلفظي ماض ؛ ولا بُدَّ أن يكون أحدهما يدلُّ على الإيجاب : كأعطيتك وجعلت لك هذا بكذا، أو على القبول : كأجزتُ وأخذت، وإنما شرط المضي فيهما ؛ لأن البيع إنشاء تصرُّف، والإنشاء يعرفُ بالشَّرع ؛ لأنَّ الواضع لم يضع له لفظاً خاصاً. والشرع استعمل فيه اللفظ الذي وضع للإخبار عن الماضي ؛ لأنه يستدعي سبق المخبر عنه ؛ ليكون الكلام صحيحاً ، فكان أوّل من غيره على تحقق الوجود، فكان أنسب بالإنشاء المحصل للوجود ؛ ولأنَّ الماضي إيجاب وقطع، والمستقبلُ عِدَةٌ وأمر وتوكيل ؛ فلهذا انعقد بالماضي.
فإن قيل : لو قال : خذ هذا بألف ، فقبل المشتري ، انعقد البيع؟
أجيب : بأن انعقاده ليس بلفظ : «خذ»، بل بالماضي الثَّابِتِ اقتضاء ؛ لأنه لما أمرَهُ بالأخذ بألف ، وليس له ولاية ذلك إلا بالبيع، فصار كأنه قال : بعتك هذا بألف فخذه ؛ لذا قال في «الدراية»: ولا ينعقد إن كان أحد اللفظين مستقبلاً نحو أن يقول البائع : أبيعك ، اشترِ مني، فقال المشتري : اشتريت، أو يقول المشتري : بعني، فقال البائع : بعث. كذا في «الكافي». وفي «شرح الطحاوي»: إذا كان أحد اللفظين مضارعاً إن أراد بالمضارع الحال فيعتمد ، وإن أراد به الاستقبال والوعد لا ينعقد. وذكر البرْجَنْدِي ناقلاً عـــن «التحفة»(۱) : إنه باللفظين الماضيين ينعقد بدون نيّة الإيجاب الحالي ، وأما إذا كان أحدهما بلفظ المستقبل فلا ينعقد إلا بنية الإيجاب. وقال صاحب «القُنْية»(۲) : «هذا الفقه، وهو أنَّ الشَّرعَ جعل الإيجاب والقبول علامة الرضاء، والإخبار عن الحال أدل على الرّضاءِ وقـت العـقـد مـن الماضي». وإن شئت التحقيق فارجع إلى «البحر الرائق»(۳)، و«فتح القدير»(۴)
فإن قلت : قد ينعقد البيع بلفظ واحد ، كما في بيع الأب من طفله وشرائه منه ، بأن يقول: بعث هذا منه بكذا، أو اشتريت هذا من ابني، فكيف يصح قول الماتن : ينعقد بإيجاب وقبول بلفظي ماض ؟
قلت : عبارة الأب ؛ لكمال شفقته ووفور محبته أقيمت مقام العبارتين، فلم يحتج القبول، فكان أصيلاً في حق نفسه، نائباً عن طفله حتى إذا بلغ عليه دون أبيه.
[1] His saying: 'with two past-tense verbs': It is necessary that one of them indicates the offer: like 'I gave you' and 'I made this for you for such-and-such', or indicates acceptance: like 'I accepted' and 'I took'. The past tense is stipulated in both because sale is a creation (insha') of a transaction, and creation is known by the Shariah, because the language inventor did not put a specific word for it. The Shariah used for it the word that was placed for informing about the past, because it requires that the reported event precede the speech for the statement to be correct. Thus, it was first (among meanings) due to its signifying the actual existence, so it was most suitable for creation that brings about existence. And because the past tense is obligation and finality, while the future is promise, command, and delegation. Therefore, it is concluded with the past tense.
If it is said: If he says 'Take this for a thousand' and the buyer accepts, is the sale concluded? The answer is that its conclusion is not through the word 'take', but through the past tense that is established by implication. Because when he ordered him to take for a thousand, and he has no authority for that except through sale, it becomes as if he said 'I sold you this for a thousand, so take it.' Hence it is said in al-Diraya: It is not concluded if one of the two expressions is future, such as if the seller says 'I am selling you', 'Buy from me', and the buyer says 'I bought', or if the buyer says 'Sell to me' and the seller says 'I sold'. Likewise in al-Kafi. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If one of the two expressions is in the imperfect tense, if he intends the present state with it, it is relied upon, but if he intends future and promise, it is not concluded. Al-Birjandi narrated from al-Tuhfah that: With two past-tense expressions it is concluded without intention of present offer, but if one of them is in the future tense, it is not concluded except with intention of offer. And the author of al-Qunyah said: 'This is the fiqh, and it is that the Shariah made offer and acceptance a sign of consent, and informing about the present state is more indicative of consent at the time of contract than the past.' And if you wish verification, refer to al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Fath al-Qadir.
If you say: The sale may be concluded with a single expression, as in the sale of a father from his child and his purchase from him, such that he says 'I sold this to him for such-and-such' or 'I bought this from my son.' So how can the author's statement be valid: 'It is concluded by offer and acceptance with two past-tense expressions?' I say: The father's expression, due to his complete affection and abundant love, is elevated to the status of two expressions, so acceptance is not needed. Thus, he is original in his own right, acting as a deputy for his child until he reaches maturity without his father.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the requirement of past tense verbs for offer and acceptance in a sale contract according to the Hanafi school. The author explains that the Shariah uses past tense verbs because they indicate actual occurrence, which is necessary for the creation (insha') of a contract. The future tense indicates promise or delegation and thus is insufficient. The text then addresses several objections and exceptions. First, if the seller says 'Take this for a thousand' and the buyer accepts, the sale is concluded by implication of the past tense due to the necessary context. Second, if one of the two expressions is in the future tense, most Hanafi scholars require that the future tense be intended for the present state, or else the contract is not concluded; a minority view (from al-Qunyah) holds that the future can indicate the present state and thus is valid. Third, the exception of a father transacting on behalf of his minor child is discussed: a single expression from the father suffices because of his guardianship and affection.

Key Terms
ماضي (madhi): Past tense verb used for offer/acceptance in sale
إيجاب (ijab): The offer in a contract, e.g., 'I sold' or 'I gave'
قبول (qabul): The acceptance in a contract, e.g., 'I accepted' or 'I bought'
إنشاء (insha'): Creation of a legal effect through speech, as opposed to khabar (information)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary reason the Shariah requires past tense verbs for offer and acceptance in sale?
A) Because past tense is easier to pronounce
B) Because past tense indicates actual occurrence and finality, suitable for creating a contract
C) Because the language inventor assigned past tense exclusively for contracts
D) Because future tense is not grammatically correct

Key Takeaway
The past tense is essential for contract formation in Hanafi fiqh because it indicates immediate creation, but with proper intention, future tense may also be accepted in some views.

Sale by Mutual Exchange: One-Sided or Two-Sided?
Explains the validity and conditions of sale by mutual exchange without verbal offer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bay' al-ta'ati and identify the more correct view regarding one-sided vs two-sided exchange.

Core Idea
A sale can be valid by mutual exchange alone if consent is clear, even without words.

This lesson examines a practical issue: is a sale valid if only one party hands over the price or commodity without spoken offer and acceptance? The Hanafi school has an internal difference of opinion, settled by later authorities. We explore the reasoning and the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is bay' al-ta'ati (sale by mutual exchange). The ruling per the Hanafi school is that it is valid (sahih) because consent (rida') is established by the exchange.
2
Locate the main dispute
Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute over how much exchange is needed. The majority say both parties must give (the buyer hands over price, the seller hands over commodity). Another view, attributed to Imam Muhammad, says one side suffices.
The majority view is reported by al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani, and is chosen in al-Bazzaziyyah.
3
Understand the reasoning for the minority view
Imam Muhammad argued that the contract is established by receipt (qabd) of one party. If one side gives, and the other does not object, consent is implied.
Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam authenticated this view in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The more correct view (asahh) according to al-Tanwir is that one side giving suffices, provided the other does not express dissatisfaction (by crying out). This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This is the position adopted by later Hanafi scholars.
5
Apply the ruling to a real scenario
In everyday transactions like buying groceries, if you put money on the counter and take the item without words, the sale is valid as long as the seller does not object. This follows the mufta bihi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١]قوله : وبتعاط ؛ لأنّ جوازه باعتبارِ الرّضاء وقد وجد، وحقيقته: وضعُ الثَّمن وأخذ المثمّن عن تراضي منهما في المجلس كما قالوا وهو يفيد أنه لا بُدَّ من الإعطاء من الجانبين، وعليه الأكثر كما ذكره الطرسوسي ، وأفتــى بــه الحَلْوَانِي، وفي «البزازيَّة) : إنه المختار. لكن في «التنوير) : ويكتفى بالإعطاء من أحد الجانبين على الأصح إذا لم يصرخ مع التعاطي بعدم الرضاء ، وفي «المنح ) : وهكذا صححه الكمال في «الفتح» ) ونص محمَّدٌ لله على أنَّ بيعَ التَّعاطي يثبت بقبض أحد المتعاقدين، وهذا ينتظم المبيع والثمن.
[1] His saying: 'And by mutual exchange (ta'ati)': Because its permissibility is on account of consent (rida'), and it exists. Its reality is: placing the price and taking the commodity with mutual consent (taradi) in the session, as they said. This indicates that giving from both sides is necessary, and this is the view of the majority (al-akthar) as mentioned by al-Tarsusi, and al-Halwani gave fatwa accordingly, and in al-Bazzaziyyah: it is the chosen view (al-mukhtar). However, in al-Tanwir: It is sufficient with giving from one side according to the more correct view (asahh), as long as he does not cry out with dissatisfaction during the exchange. And in al-Manh: Thus al-Kamal authenticated it in al-Fath, and Muhammad al-Shaybani explicitly stated that the sale by exchange is established by the receipt (qabd) of one of the two contracting parties, and this includes the commodity and the price.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding sale by mutual exchange (bay' al-ta'ati), which is a transaction conducted without spoken offer and acceptance, but by handing over the price and taking the commodity. The author explains that the basis for its validity is the presence of mutual consent (rida'), which is apparent from the actions. He then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: the majority of Hanafi scholars require that both parties give (i.e., the buyer hands over the price and the seller hands over the commodity). This is the view of al-Tarsusi, al-Halwani, and is chosen in al-Bazzaziyyah. However, in al-Tanwir, it is considered sufficient that only one side gives (e.g., the buyer pays the price) as long as the other side does not express dissatisfaction. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani explicitly states that the sale is established by the receipt (qabd) of one party, meaning once the price and commodity are exchanged, the contract is complete. This view is authenticated by al-Kamal in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
تعاطي (ta'āṭī): Sale by mutual exchange, without verbal offer/acceptance, by handing over price and commodity
تراض (tarāḍī): Mutual consent between parties, the basis for validity of ta'ati
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession/receipt, which according to Imam Muhammad establishes the contract of ta'ati

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view regarding sale by exchange (bay' al-ta'ati) is considered the more correct (asahh) in al-Tanwir?
A) Both parties must give (put price and take commodity)
B) It is sufficient if one party gives, provided the other does not express dissatisfaction
C) A verbal offer is still required
D) The sale is valid only if written

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-ta'ati is valid in the Hanafi school; the relied-upon view is that one-sided giving suffices if the other party does not object.

Sale by Mutual Delivery (Bayʿ al-Taʿāṭī): The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on One-Sided Giving
Exposition of the ruling and conditions of mutual delivery sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bayʿ al-taʿāṭī and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding one-sided versus two-sided giving.

Core Idea
In sale by mutual delivery, one-sided giving suffices if the other party does not express dissatisfaction.

This lesson examines the ruling of sale by mutual delivery (bayʿ al-taʿāṭī), a common transaction in markets where goods and price are exchanged without formal verbal offer and acceptance. We will explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether giving must occur from both sides or if one-sided giving with consent is sufficient. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the operative fatwa and the reliance on custom and linguistic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on bayʿ al-taʿāṭī
The act is valid (sahih) based on mutual consent. The dispute centers on whether giving must be from both sides.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
The majority (al-Ṭarsūsī, al-Ḥalwānī) require giving from both sides, as indicated by the definition of taʿāṭī as mutual exchange.
This is considered the initial view, but later scholars differed.
3
Examine the linguistic and legal argument for one-sided giving
The term taʿāṭī linguistically means 'taking/handing over' (from one side), not mutual exchange. This supports the minority view that one-sided giving suffices.
Reference to al-Qāmūs and lexical authority.
4
Analyse the authoritative tarjīḥ
Later scholars like al-Karkī, al-Kamāl, and the author of Majmaʿ al-Anhur prefer the one-sided giving view, making it the mufta bihi.
Al-Karkī explicitly states 'and this is the basis for fatwa'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence)
Under majority view, the sale is invalid if only one party gives; under mufta bihi, it is valid as long as the other does not object.
This affects daily transactions in markets and online exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبتعاط؛ لأنّ جوازه باعتبار الرضاء وقد وجد، وحقيقته: وضع الثمن وأخذ المثمن عن تراضي منهما في المجلس كما قالوا وهو يفيد أنه لا بد من الإعطاء من الجانبين، وعليه الأكثر كما ذكره الطرسوسي، وأفتى به الحلواني، وفي «البزازية»: إنه المختار. لكن في «التنوير»: ويكتفى بالإعطاء من أحد الجانبين على الأصح إذا لم يصرخ مع التعاطي بعدم الرضاء، وفي «المنح»: وهكذا صححه الكمال في «الفتح» ونص محمد لله على أن بيع التعاطي يثبت بقبض أحد المتعاقدين، وهذا ينتظم المبيع والثمن. وفي «القاموس» وغيره: «التعاطي: هو التناول». وهو إنما يقتضي الإعطاء من جانب لا الإعطاء من الجانبين كما فهم الطرسوسي، وفي «الكركي»: وبه يفتى، واكتفى الكرماني بتسليم المبيع مع بيان الثمن، أما إذا دفع الثمن ولم يقبض فلا يجوز. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر».
And with mutual giving (taʿāṭī) because its permissibility is based on mutual consent and it has occurred. Its reality is: placing the price and taking the priced item (commodity) based on mutual agreement from both parties in the session, as they said. And it indicates that there must be giving from both sides, and this is the view of the majority as mentioned by al-Ṭarsūsī, and al-Ḥalwānī gave a fatwa accordingly. In al-Bazzāziyya: it is the chosen view. However in al-Tanwīr: sufficiency of giving from one side is the correct view if he does not express with the mutual giving dissatisfaction. In al-Minah: likewise al-Kamāl in al-Fatḥ authenticated it, and Muḥammad stated: the sale by mutual giving is established by the taking possession of one of the contracting parties, and this covers both the sold commodity and the price. And in al-Qāmūs and others: 'al-taʿāṭī is taking/handing over.' It only requires giving from one side, not from both as understood by al-Ṭarsūsī. And in al-Karkī: this is the basis for fatwa. Al-Kirmānī sufficed with delivery of the sold item with statement of the price; if he pays the price and does not take possession, it is not permissible. This is in Majmaʿ al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of sale by mutual delivery (bayʿ al-taʿāṭī), where parties exchange goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance. The commentator explains that the basis for its validity is mutual consent (riḍā), which is found. He defines it as placing the price and taking the commodity by mutual agreement in the session. The text notes a difference: the majority require giving from both sides (as per al-Ṭarsūsī), but some, like al-Ḥalwānī, accept sufficiency from one side, provided the other does not express dissatisfaction. The author of al-Tanwīr and al-Kamāl in al-Fatḥ also adopt the latter view. Imam Muḥammad ash-Shaybānī stated that the sale is established by the receipt of one party, covering both goods and price. The lexical meaning of taʿāṭī is 'taking/handing over', which implies one-sided giving, not mutual as understood by al-Ṭarsūsī. Thus, al-Karkī and al-Kirmānī allowed one-sided giving with disclosure of the price, but if only price is given without taking goods, it is invalid. This is the view relied upon in Majmaʿ al-Anhur.

Key Terms
تعاطي (taʿāṭī): Mutual giving or taking; in fiqh, sale conducted by exchange of goods without verbal contract.
رضاء (riḍā): Mutual consent; the basis for validity of taʿāṭī.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One-sided giving suffices if the other does not express dissatisfaction.
C) Only the buyer must give payment.
D) Verbal offer and acceptance are always required.

Key Takeaway
Sale by mutual delivery (taʿāṭī) is valid in the Hanafi school with one-sided giving, provided the other party does not object, based on custom and linguistic interpretation.

Defining Valuable and Cheap Goods in Sales
Clarifying the meaning of valuable and cheap goods in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nafis and khasis, distinguish between the custom-based and nisab-based definitions, and explain why custom is the primary criterion.

Core Idea
Valuable and cheap are defined by custom, not by a fixed textual threshold.

In Hanafi sales law, the validity of a transaction does not hinge on the item's value, but understanding what counts as 'valuable' (nafis) or 'cheap' (khasis) helps apply certain rulings. This passage clarifies these terms, noting that while custom is the primary gauge, some scholars use the nisab of theft (a quarter dinar) as a convenient boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the Arabic terms نَفِيس (nafis) and خَسِيس (khasis) in the passage. Recognize that they are opposites in this context.
2
Understand the primary definition
Note that the passage defines nafis as 'that whose price is abundant' (e.g., slaves) and khasis as 'that whose price is little' (e.g., meat, bread). This is a custom-based (urf) definition.
Custom can vary by time and place.
3
Examine the alternative threshold
Some scholars define nafis as items reaching or exceeding the nisab of theft (a quarter dinar or equivalent), and khasis as below it. Understand that this is a practical guideline, not a binding textual ruling.
The nisab of theft is a fixed Shar'i measure, but its use here is a convention.
4
Recognize the legal implication
In sales, the classification affects issues like the obligation to deliver, warranty, or permissibility of barter. However, the primary ruling is that any item—valuable or cheap—can be sold validly as long as the contract elements are met.
Custom determines the category, not the validity of the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : في النفيس والخسيس ؛ والمراد بالنفيس ما يكثر ثمنه كالعبيد والإماء ، وبالخسيس : ما يقل ثمنه كاللحم والخبز وغيرهما، ومنهم من حد النفيس بنصاب السَّرقة فأكثر، والخسيس بما دونه. كذا في «المنح).
His saying: regarding the valuable and the cheap; and what is meant by valuable is that whose price is abundant, like slaves and slave-girls; and by cheap is that whose price is little, like meat, bread, and others. And among them are those who define valuable as the nisāb of theft or more, and cheap as less than that. Thus in al-Minaḥ.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines two terms: al-nafīs (valuable) and al-khasīs (cheap) as used in the context of valid sales. The commentator explains that al-nafīs refers to items with high value, such as slaves, while al-khasīs refers to low-value items like food. Some scholars define the boundary by the nisāb (minimum threshold) for theft (i.e., a quarter dinar or equivalent), considering items above that as valuable and below as cheap. This distinction affects rulings such as whether a sale is valid even if the item is very cheap or very expensive.

Key Terms
نفيس (nafīs): Valuable item with high price according to custom.
خسيس (khasīs): Cheap item with low price according to custom.

Key Takeaway
Valuable and cheap are defined by custom, with the nisab of theft being only a scholar's guideline, not a binding rule.
