The Four Causes in the Definition of Sale
Explaining the four causes in the definition of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four Aristotelian causes (material, formal, efficient, final) as applied to the Hanafi definition of sale.

Core Idea
The definition of sale includes all four causes: property (material), offer/acceptance (formal), parties (efficient), and ownership transfer (final).

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of sale using Aristotelian causal analysis. By examining the material, formal, efficient, and final causes, you will see how the classical definition 'exchange of property' implicitly contains all necessary elements. This framework deepens your understanding of contract formation in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes in general
Define each cause: material (substance), formal (form/structure), efficient (agent), final (purpose).
These are philosophical categories applied to fiqh.
2
Map each cause to elements of a sale
For a sale, material = property, formal = offer and acceptance, efficient = contracting parties, final = transfer of ownership.
The definition 'exchange of property' directly mentions material and final causes.
3
Distinguish internal vs external causes
Material and formal causes are internal to the effect (sale itself); efficient and final causes are external to the essence.
Internal causes constitute the act; external causes bring it about or motivate it.
4
Explain how the definition encompasses all causes
Show that the single phrase 'exchange of property' implies the property (material), the exchange action (formal and efficient), and the result (final).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فمبادلة المال ... الخ ؛ تفريع على ما سبق من تعريف البيع ، اعلم : أولاً : أنَّ العلة ما يحتاجُ إليه الشَّيء في الوجود ، وهي إما أن تكون جزء من المعلول أو خارجاً عنه : والأوَّل إما أن يحصل المعلول له بالقوة وهي العلة المادية، أو بالفعل وهي العلة الصورية، وهاتان داخلتان في المعلول. والثاني: إما أن يصدر عنه المعلول: وهي العلة الفاعلية، أو لا يصدر عنه بل لأجله، وهي العلة الغائية، وهاتان خارجتان عن الماهية. وثانياً : إنَّ المصنف قال في «التوضيح شرح التنقيح»: «المراد بالحسيّاتِ ما :
His saying: 'exchange of property...' is a branching from what preceded of the definition of sale. Know firstly: that the cause (ʿilla) is what a thing needs for existence, and it is either part of the effect (maʿlūl) or external to it. The first: either the effect occurs potentially (bi-l-quwwa) – this is the material cause; or actually (bi-l-fiʿl) – this is the formal cause. These two are internal to the effect. The second: either the effect originates from it – this is the efficient cause; or it does not originate from it but rather for its sake – this is the final cause. These two are external to the essence. Secondly: the author said in al-Tawḍīḥ Sharḥ al-Tanqīḥ: 'What is meant by sensory [matters]...'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage applies Aristotelian causal theory to the definition of sale. The definition of sale as 'exchange of property' is analyzed: the material cause is the property being exchanged; the formal cause is the offer and acceptance (or mutual delivery); the efficient cause is the two contracting parties; and the final cause is the transfer of ownership. The commentator links this to the earlier definition, showing that the definition includes all causes. The quote from al-Tawḍīḥ indicates that the sensory matters (ḥissiyyāt) are the observable elements of the contract.

Key Terms
علة مادية (ʿilla mādiyya): Material cause: the substance or matter from which a thing is made; in sale, the property being exchanged.
علة صورية (ʿilla ṣūriyya): Formal cause: the form or structure that makes a thing what it is; in sale, the offer and acceptance.
علة فاعلية (ʿilla fāʿiliyya): Efficient cause: the agent that brings about the effect; in sale, the two contracting parties.
علة غائية (ʿilla ghāʾiyya): Final cause: the purpose or end for which a thing exists; in sale, the transfer of ownership.
علة (ʿilla): Cause: that which a thing needs for its existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the causal analysis of sale, which cause refers to the offer and acceptance (ījāb wa qabūl)?
A) Material cause (ʿilla mādiyya).
B) Formal cause (ʿilla ṣūriyya).
C) Efficient cause (ʿilla fāʿiliyya).
D) Final cause (ʿilla ghāʾiyya).

Key Takeaway
The four-cause analysis (material, formal, efficient, final) provides a complete framework for understanding the definition of sale in Hanafi fiqh.

The Four Causes of Sale in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Causal analysis of sale definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of a sale (bay') and distinguish between material, formal, efficient, and final causes as used in Hanafi legal analysis.

Core Idea
A sale exists through four causes: material (offer/acceptance), formal (exchange), efficient (parties), and final (benefits).

In this passage, the author delves into the philosophical underpinnings of the contract of sale, moving beyond a simple definition to explain how a sale comes into existence. Using the framework of Aristotelian causality, he identifies the material, formal, efficient, and final causes of a valid sale. Understanding these causes helps clarify why certain elements—like offer and acceptance—are essential, and why the contracting parties and the purpose of the transaction are integral.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes listed
The passage explicitly names four causes: material (offering, acceptance, and mutual delivery), formal (the exchange of property), efficient (the two contracting parties), and final (the benefits sought). Locate each in the Arabic text.
The final cause is omitted in this chapter but mentioned in the book of marriage.
2
Distinguish internal vs. external causes
The author explains that material and formal causes are part of the essence of the sale (internal), while efficient and final causes are external to the essence. Understand this dichotomy.
Material cause: what the sale is 'made of' (offer/acceptance). Formal cause: what gives it its specific nature (exchange).
3
Analyze the material cause in detail
The material cause includes both verbal (ijab and qabul) and non-verbal mutual delivery (ta'ati). Note that ta'ati is valid for all goods according to the Hanafi school, a point of dispute with other schools.
The passage clarifies that ta'ati means delivery from both sides, not just one, contrary to al-Tarsusi's understanding.
4
Explain the omission of the final cause
The author deliberately omits the final cause (the benefits of the sale) here, saying it is obvious and already covered in the chapter on marriage. Reflect on why he might consider the other causes less obvious.
The final cause is the purpose (maslahah) for which the sale is made, e.g., fulfilling needs through trade.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ البيوع الصحيحة في النفيس والخسيس، فمبادلة المال بالمال علة صورية للبيع، والإيجاب والقبول والتعاطي علة مادية له، والمبادلة تكون بين اثنين فهما العلة الفاعلية. وفي «القاموس» وغيره: «التعاطي: هو التناول». وهو إنما يقتضي الإعطاء من جانب لا الإعطاء من الجانبين كما فهم الطرسوسي، وفي «الكركي»: وبه يفتى، واكتفى الكرماني بتسليم المبيع مع بيان الثمن، أما إذا دفع الثمن ولم يقبض فلا يجوز. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». [١] قوله: في النفيس والخسيس؛ والمراد بالنفيس ما يكثر ثمنه كالعبيد والإماء، وبالخسيس: ما يقل ثمنه كاللحم والخبز وغيرهما، ومنهم من حد النفيس بنصاب السرقة فأكثر، والخسيس بما دونه. كذا في «المنح». [٢] قوله: فمبادلة المال... الخ؛ تفريع على ما سبق من تعريف البيع، اعلم أولاً: أن العلة ما يحتاج إليه الشيء في الوجود، وهي إما أن تكون جزء من المعلول أو خارجاً عنه: والأول إما أن يحصل المعلول له بالقوة وهي العلة المادية، أو بالفعل وهي العلة الصورية، وهاتان داخلتان في المعلول. والثاني: إما أن يصدر عنه المعلول: وهي العلة الفاعلية، أو لا يصدر عنه بل لأجله، وهي العلة الغائية، وهاتان خارجتان عن الماهية. وثانياً: إن المصنف قال في «التوضيح شرح التنقيح»: «المراد بالحسيات ما لها وجود حسي فقط، والمراد من الشرعيات ما لها وجود شرعي مع الوجود الحسي: كالبيع؛ فإن له وجوداً حسياً، فإن الإيجاب والقبول موجودان حساً، الوجود الحسي له وجود شرعي». انتهى بقدر الضرورة. ومر سابقاً أنه هو المجموع المركب من الإيجاب والقبول، فالإيجاب والقبول والتعاطي قوام البيع، وبينهما وبين المبادلة افتقار والتزام، فلما كان الإيجاب والقبول والتعاطي بمنزلة المادة، والمبادلة بمنزلة الصورة، وصار الإيجاب والقبول أو التعاطي علة مادية، والمبادلة علة صورية. ولما كان البيع صادراً من المتعاقدين، وحاصلاً منهما، صار علة فاعلية، وبقيت العلة الغائية: وهي المصالح المترتبة على البيع التي صدر البيع عن المتعاقدين لأجلها، وتركها المصنف هاهنا اكتفاء بذكرها في كتاب النكاح، ونظراً إلى ظهورها بخلاف العلل المذكورة، فإن فيها إخفاءً بالنسبة إلى تلك العلة.
The book of sale / Valid sales concerning precious and base goods. The exchange of property for property is a formal cause for the sale, and the offer, acceptance, and mutual delivery are a material cause for it. The exchange occurs between two parties, so they are the efficient cause. In 'al-Qamus' and others: 'al-ta'ati: it is taking.' It only implies giving from one side, not giving from both sides as al-Tarsusi understood. In 'al-Kurki': and by it fatwa is given. Al-Kirmani was satisfied with delivery of the sold item with mention of the price; but if one pays the price and does not take delivery, it is not permissible. As in 'Majma' al-Anhur.' [1] His statement: 'in the precious and the base'; the intended meaning of 'precious' is that whose price is high, like male and female slaves, and 'base' is that whose price is low, like meat, bread, and others. Some defined 'precious' as the nisab of theft and above, and 'base' as below it. As in 'al-Minh.' [2] His statement: 'the exchange of property... etc.' is a branch of what preceded of the definition of sale. Know first that the cause is what the thing needs for existence, and it is either a part of the effect or external to it. The first is either that by which the effect obtains potentially, which is the material cause, or actually, which is the formal cause, and these two are internal to the effect. The second: either that from which the effect emanates, which is the efficient cause, or not from it but for its sake, which is the final cause, and these two are external to the essence. Secondly, the author said in 'al-Tawdih Sharh al-Tanqih': 'The intended meaning of 'sensory things' is that which has only sensory existence, and the intended meaning of 'shar'i things' is that which has shar'i existence along with sensory existence, like sale; for it has sensory existence, for offer and acceptance exist sensorily, [and] its sensory existence has a shar'i existence.' End to the extent needed. And it passed earlier that it is the composite set of offer and acceptance. So offer, acceptance, and mutual delivery are the subsistence of sale, and between them and exchange there is need and commitment. Since offer, acceptance, and mutual delivery are like the matter, and exchange is like the form, so offer and acceptance or mutual delivery become material cause, and exchange becomes formal cause. And since the sale emanates from the two contractors and results from them, it becomes efficient cause, and the final cause remains: which are the benefits that result from the sale for which the contractors make the sale, and the author omitted it here, content with mentioning it in the book of marriage, and due to its obviousness contrary to the mentioned causes, for in them there is hiddenness relative to that cause.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a definition and causal analysis of the contract of sale (bay'). The author first states the formal definition: 'exchange of property for property' (mubadalah al-mal bi al-mal) and then classifies the causes of sale into four Aristotelian categories. The material cause (material) is the immediate components: offer (ijab), acceptance (qabul), and mutual delivery (ta'ati). The formal cause (suriyyah) is the exchange itself, which gives the contract its specific nature. The efficient cause (fa'iliyyah) is the two parties (contracting parties). The final cause (gha'iyyah) is the purposes and benefits (masalih) for which the sale is made, which the author omits in this context but mentions in the book of marriage. The passage then clarifies definitions: 'precious' (nafis) refers to high-value items like slaves, 'base' (khassis) to low-value items like meat and bread. It also cites authorities: al-Qamus defines ta'ati as taking (tana'ul), al-Kurki gives fatwa based on it, al-Kirmani requires delivery of the sold item with price disclosure. The passage further cites from al-Tawdih that sale has both sensory existence (offer/acceptance) and shar'i existence (legal effect). The explanation emphasizes that the four causes are intrinsic to understanding the legal reality of sale.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): Exchange of property for property; a legally recognized contract transferring ownership.
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): Mutual delivery of goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance; a valid mode of sale.
النفيس والخسيس (al-nafis wa al-khassis): Precious goods (high-value, e.g., slaves) and base goods (low-value, e.g., food).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered the material cause (علة مادية) of a sale?
A) The exchange of property (مبادلة المال بالمال)
B) The offer, acceptance, and mutual delivery (الإيجاب والقبول والتعاطي)
C) The two contracting parties (المتعاقدان)
D) The benefits derived from the sale (المصالح المترتبة على البيع)

Key Takeaway
A sale is understood through four causes: material (offer/acceptance/mutual delivery), formal (exchange of property), efficient (the parties), and final (the benefits), jointly constituting its legal reality.

Why 'Mutual Consent' is Omitted from Sale Definition
Explaining why mutual consent is not part of the sale definition, using coerced sale as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school excludes mutual consent from the definition of sale and apply this distinction to coerced contracts.

Core Idea
Mutual consent is a condition for soundness, not for the existence of a sale.

In defining a sale, Hanafi jurists deliberately omitted the phrase 'by mutual consent.' Why? Because including it would exclude the coerced sale (bay' al-mukrah), which is a validly concluded contract according to Abu Hanifa. This passage explains that mutual consent is not part of the essence of sale; it is a condition for the contract's full legal effects. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating the Hanafi approach to contract formation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the omission
Note that the definition does not include 'by mutual consent'. Ask why the author chose to omit it.
2
Understand the inclusion
The author explains that omitting 'mutual consent' allows the definition to include the coerced sale, which is a binding (mun'aqid) sale even though it is defective (fasid).
3
Distinguish formation from soundness
In Hanafi law, mutual consent is a condition for the soundness (sihhah) of the contract, not for its existence (in'iqad). Thus the coerced sale is validly formed but defective.
This contrasts with the Shafi'i view, where consent is a condition for validity, making a coerced sale void (batil).
4
Address the precious/base goods nuance
The definition includes both 'precious and base' to counter a minority Hanafi view (e.g., al-Karkhi) that mutual delivery (ta'ati) only works for base goods. The relied-upon position affirms it works for both.
This inclusion reinforces that the contract is concluded by offer and acceptance (or ta'ati), regardless of the item's value.
5
Derive the legal principle
Mutual consent is not a constitutive element of sale; it is a condition for its legal effects (e.g., irrevocable ownership transfer).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولم يقل على سبيل التراضي ليشمل ما لا يكون بالتراضي : كبيع المكره؛ فإنه بيع منعقد، (هو الصحيح). إنما قال هذا لأن عند البعض أن البيع لا ينعقد إلا في الخسيس دون النفيس، فقيد بالنفيس والخسيس لبيان أنه ينعقد في النفيس والخسيس جميعاً عندنا.
His statement: And he did not say 'by mutual consent' to encompass what is not by mutual consent, such as the sale of the coerced person, for it is a binding sale, (it is correct). He only said this because according to some people, the sale is only concluded for base goods, not precious goods, so he restricted it with 'precious and base' to clarify that it is concluded for both precious and base goods according to us.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the definition of sale, explaining why the author omitted the phrase 'by mutual consent' (على سبيل التراضي) from the definition. The reasoning is that including 'consent' would exclude the sale of a coerced person (بيع المكره), which is a validly contracted sale (بيع منعقد) even if it is fasid (defective) due to coercion. The term 'sahih' (correct) here refers to the ruling that the contract is validly formed. The passage also addresses a difference among Hanafis: some (like al-Karkhi) held that ta'ati (mutual delivery) only works for base goods, not precious ones. Therefore, the author mentions 'precious and base' in the definition to affirm that sale is valid for both categories according to the relied-upon school position. The key point is that mutual consent is not part of the essential concept of sale, but rather a condition for its full legal effects (such as the transfer of ownership being irrevocable). The contract's existence is based on offer and acceptance (or ta'ati) alone.

Key Terms
بيع المكره (bay' al-mukrah): A sale made under duress; it is a validly concluded contract but fasid (defective) due to the absence of consent.
التراضي (al-taradi): Mutual consent of the contracting parties; it is a condition for the legal effects of the sale, but not part of its definition or constituent elements.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contract law, mutual consent is a condition for the soundness of a sale, not for its formation; thus a coerced sale is validly concluded but defective.

The Hanafi Definition of Sale: Exclusion of Mutual Consent
Commentary on the definition of sale and the exclusion of mutual consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sale and explain why mutual consent is excluded as a condition rather than part of the definition.

Core Idea
Sale is defined by its external indicators (offer/acceptance), not by internal consent—mutual consent is a condition for validity, not part of the essence.

In this lesson, we examine why the Hanafi school deliberately omits 'mutual consent' (taradi) from the definition of sale. The commentator explains that this inclusion would exclude valid contracts that lack consent initially, such as a coerced sale. Understanding this distinction between definition and condition is crucial for applying the correct ruling to various sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
The passage states that sale is the composite of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) or exchange by action (ta'ati). Note that ta'ati is included as a valid form of contract formation.
The definition is purely external and observable.
2
Locate the exclusion of mutual consent
The commentator explains why the author did not say 'by way of mutual consent': to include sales that occur without consent, like the sale of a coerced person, which is still a concluded contract (mun'aqid).
This shows the practical rationale for the definition.
3
Understand the four causes of sale
The passage discusses material cause (offer/acceptance or ta'ati), formal cause (the exchange itself), efficient cause (the two parties), and final cause (the interests). Note how each corresponds to Aristotelian causation.
This metaphysical framework underlines the structure of the contract.
4
Distinguish between definition and condition
Mutual consent is a condition for the shar'i effect (establishing ownership), not part of the definition. Offer and acceptance are external proxies for internal consent.
This distinction prevents conceptual confusion.
5
Apply the distinction to a coerced sale
The coerced sale is mun'aqid (contract exists) but fasid (invalid in ruling). Ownership is established upon possession, but the seller may reclaim.
This shows the practical consequence of separating definition and condition.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of sale. The author of the base text (matn) did not include the condition of mutual consent (taradi) in the definition of sale. The commentator explains why: to include sales that occur without mutual consent, such as the sale of a coerced person, which is still a valid contract (mun'aqid) though fasid (invalid). The passage then discusses the four causes of sale: material cause (offer/acceptance or ta'ati), formal cause (exchange), efficient cause (the parties), and final cause (the interests). The footnote [1] elaborates: mutual consent is not part of the essence of sale but a condition for its shar'i effect. Footnote [2] confirms that offer and acceptance (or ta'ati) are proxies for internal consent because they indicate it externally.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): A contract involving exchange of property, defined as the composite of offer and acceptance or exchange (ta'ati).
التراضي (al-taradi): Mutual consent of the contracting parties, which is internal and not part of the definition but a condition for validity.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ijab wa al-qabul): Offer and acceptance, the verbal components of sale that indicate consent.
منعقد (mun'aqid): A concluded contract that has been formed by the pillars (offer and acceptance/ta'ati), even if its ruling may be fasid.
فاسد (fasid): A transaction that is structurally complete but defective in a condition, rendering it invalid in ruling while still producing some effects (e.g., ownership upon possession).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of sale focuses on external indicators (offer/acceptance/ta'ati), while mutual consent is a condition for validity, not a component of the definition.

Validity of Sale by Exchange (Ta'ati) and Disputes
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi disputes on ta'ati sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'ati sale, identify the conditions for its validity, and compare it with the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali positions.

Core Idea
Sale by exchange (ta'ati) is valid in the Hanafi school for low-value items only, based on custom (urf), not on verbal formula.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, the requirement of verbal offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) for a valid sale is not absolute. The concept of sale by exchange (ta'ati) recognizes that actions can also convey consent, especially in everyday low-value transactions. This lesson explores the Hanafi position, the inter-madhhab disputes, and the intra-Hanafi nuance on whether both parties must actively give.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: ta'ati is valid only for low-value (khasis) items. Note the source of this ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify opposing schools and their proofs
State the Shafi'i/Hanbali view (ta'ati invalid) and their proof that actions do not indicate intentions by convention. Then state the Maliki view (ta'ati valid for anything customary) and their proof that the purpose of sale is exchange with consent, and the Lawgiver did not require verbal expression.
The Maliki view supports the Hanafi position but differs on the scope.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to Shafi'i/Hanbali
Explain the Hanafi rebuttal: custom (urf) gives actions meaning, so ta'ati is valid for common items. The Lawgiver's silence on verbal expression implies recourse to custom.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual giving
Distinguish between the condition of giving from both sides (mu'tamad) and one-sided giving. Identify the majority view that both parties must give, as held by most mashayikh, al-Halwani, and al-Bazzaziyyah.
This affects whether a transaction where only one party hands over goods/money is valid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
State the practical consequence: for Hanafis, buying low-value items (e.g., bread) by handing money and taking goods silently is valid, provided both sides give. For Shafi'is, such a transaction is not a valid sale. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the view requiring mutual giving for khasis items.

[ ١ ] قوله : من الجانبين ؛ لأنه من المعاطاة، وهي مفاعلة، فيقتضي حصولها من الجانبين كالمضاربة والمقاسمة والمخاصمة، وعليه أكثر المشايخ ، كما ذكره نجم الدين الطَّرْسُوسِي (٤) ، وأفتى به الحَلْوَانِي، وفي «البَزَّازِيّة ) : إنه المختار. كذا في «المنح )، ونقل القاضي أبو المكارم في شرحه للـ «نقاية ) عن «الفوائد المسموعة عن صاحب «المحيط»: إنَّ المختار عندي أنه يشترط الإعطاء من الجانبين. انتهى. [3] His saying: 'Because with some...' etc.; and it is because he (al-Karkhi) said: Sale is only concluded by exchange (ta'ati) in lowly (khasis) things. The school of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on them) is that sale is not concluded by exchange, because actions do not indicate the intentions of people by convention. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah (3). The answer is what has passed within the inference for our school. And Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It (ta'ati) is concluded by everything that people consider a sale, because the purpose is exchange with consent, so whenever it occurs, the ruling of sale is established, and because the Lawgiver did not establish a requirement of verbal expression, so recourse to custom is obligatory. [1] His saying: 'From both sides'; because it is mu'atah (exchange), which is a mutual action, so it requires occurrence from both sides like mudarabah, muqasamah, and mukhasamah, and upon this are most mashayikh, as mentioned by Najm al-Din al-Tarsusi (4), and al-Halwani gave fatwa by it, and in al-Bazzaziyyah: it is the chosen view. Commentary
This lesson focuses on the ruling of sale by exchange (ta'ati), i.e., a contract concluded by action rather than verbal offer and acceptance. The passage presents the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) that ta'ati is valid only for low-value (khasis) items, not precious ones. It contrasts this with the Shafi'i and Hanbali position (no ta'ati at all) and the Maliki view (ta'ati valid for anything customary). The Hanafi response to Shafi'i is that actions can indicate consent by custom. Within the Hanafi school, there is a further dispute on whether ta'ati requires action from both parties (mutual giving) or if one-sided action suffices. The relied-upon view (mu'tamad) is that both sides must give, as most mashayikh hold, including al-Halwani and the author of al-Bazzaziyyah. The passage also gives an example of bargaining and taking goods without immediate payment, which is considered valid if consistent with custom.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): Sale concluded by exchange of goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance.
الخسيس (al-khasis): Low-value or base items, as opposed to precious (nafis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) All items, regardless of value.
B) Only precious (nafis) items.
C) Only low-value (khasis) items.
D) None, because verbal offer and acceptance are always required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates sale by exchange (ta'ati) for low-value items based on custom, a position distinct from Shafi'i/Hanbali and nuanced within the school regarding mutual giving.

The Ruling on Mu'atah (Hand-to-Hand Exchange) in Sale
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute on mu'atah validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mu'atah, distinguish between common (khasis) and precious (nafis) items, and explain the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes regarding its validity.

Core Idea
Mu'atah is valid for common items by custom, but precious items require verbal offer and acceptance.

Mu'atah refers to a sale where buyer and seller exchange goods and payment without explicit verbal offer and acceptance, like buying bread by handing over money and taking the bread. The Hanafi school permits this for common, low-value items, but the ruling is nuanced: within the school there is disagreement on whether both parties must act, and other schools like the Shafi'i reject it entirely. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi ruling: mu'atah is valid for common (khasis) items but not for precious (nafis) items. Memorize this baseline.
Khasis items are everyday low-value goods like bread, vegetables, etc. Nafis items include gold, silver, large sums, etc.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad (require action from both sides) and Abu Yusuf (action from one side suffices). Determine which view is relied upon (mufta bihi).
Most mashayikh take the two-sided view, but some later scholars adopted the one-sided view for ease.
3
Understand the Shafi'i/Hanbali objection
Imam al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that mu'atah is invalid because actions do not have a linguistic convention (wad') to convey intent like words do.
Their evidence is based on the need for clarity in contracts.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond with general proofs (Quran 4:29, custom, absence of textual requirement for utterance) and note that the Maliki school also accepts mu'atah based on custom.
The Hanafi emphasis on 'urf (custom) as a source of law is key here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practically, Hanafis allow buying cheap items by just taking and paying, while Shafi'is require verbal contracts even for these. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that mu'atah is valid for common items and that mutual action is required.
This affects daily transactions in Hanafi communities.

Source Text

Book of Sale/Sound Sales (2) 13: Indeed, it (the sale) is concluded by mu'atah in base (common) items, not in precious items. Mu'atah, according to some, is giving from both sides. According to others, it is sufficient from one side, such as when one negotiates, takes the commodity, and has no container to put it in, so he weighs it and leaves, then comes with the container and gives the price – it is valid. (Sale) by mu'atah in base items. The madhhab of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad is that sale is not concluded by mu'atah because actions, by convention, do not indicate people's intentions. Thus in Kamal al-Diraya. The response is what has passed from within the evidence for our madhhab. And Malik said: It is concluded by whatever people consider a sale because the objective is exchange with mutual consent; whenever that occurs, the ruling of sale is established, and because the Lawgiver did not establish the requirement of utterance, so he must refer to custom. [1] His statement: 'from both sides' because it is from mu'atah, which is a mutual interaction, so it requires occurrence from both sides like mudarabah, muqasamah, and mukhasamah. Most mashayikh are upon this, as mentioned by Najm al-Din al-Tarsusi, and al-Halwani gave fatwa accordingly. In al-Bazzaziyyah, it is the chosen position. Thus in al-Minh. And al-Qadi Abu al-Makarim in his commentary on al-Niqayah narrated from al-Fawa'id al-Masmu'ah from sahib al-Muhit: 'The chosen view with me is that giving from both sides is required.' End. [2] His statement: 'as when one negotiates...' etc. And since mu'atah according to some is from both sides (1) Among them: al-Halwani, al-Sughdi, and sahib al-Bazzaziyyah (1:368), and al-Tarsusi in Anfa' al-Wasa'il (p.233), and he said: most are upon it. (2) Among them: Abu al-Fadl al-Kirmani, al-Sarakhsi, Qadi Khan, Abu al-Yusr, sahib al-Qunyah, Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath (5:460), al-Tamurtashi in al-Tanwir (p.124), and al-Minh (q2/2b). Al-Kirki in al-Fayd said: By it is given fatwa, and al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (4:11) supported it. And see: Sharh Abi al-Makarim (q 333) and Majma' al-Anhar (5:2). (3) Kamal al-Diraya fi Sharh al-Niqayah (q363). (4) In Anfa' al-Wasa'il (p.233). (5) Al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyyah (1:368). (6) Minh al-Ghaffar (q2:2b). (7) Sharh al-Niqayah (q333).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the validity of mu'atah (hand-to-hand exchange) in sale. The Hanafi position is that mu'atah is valid for common (khasis) items but not for precious (nafis) items. Within the Hanafi school, there is disagreement: most mashayikh require action from both sides (i.e., both buyer and seller physically exchange), while others accept it from one side. The text gives examples: a buyer who negotiates, takes the item, leaves to get a container, and later pays – this is valid according to those who allow mu'atah from one side (the buyer's action). The commentary then explains that the word 'mu'atah' implies mutual action (from the pattern mufa'alah), supporting the view that both sides must act. However, some scholars like al-Halwani and al-Tarsusi hold that it suffices from one side. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools reject mu'atah entirely because actions do not linguistically convey intention like words do. The Hanafi response is that the general evidence (like the verse 'trade by mutual consent' and hadiths) supports the validity, and custom (urf) determines what constitutes a sale. Malik's school accepts mu'atah based on custom because the objective is exchange with mutual consent, not specific wording.

Key Terms
التعاطي (mu'atah): Mutual hand-to-hand exchange without verbal offer and acceptance, used in the context of sale.
الخسيس (khasis): Common, low-value items (like bread, vegetables, etc.) for which mu'atah is permissible.
النفيس (nafis): Precious, high-value items for which mu'atah is not valid and verbal offer/acceptance is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, in which type of items is mu'atah (hand-to-hand exchange) valid for sale?
A) All items regardless of value
B) Precious (nafis) items only
C) Common (khasis) items only
D) No items; mu'atah is invalid

Key Takeaway
Mu'atah is valid for common items by custom, but precious items require explicit offer/acceptance; the relied-upon Hanafi position requires mutual action from both sides.

Khiyar al-Majlis and Prohibition of Splitting the Transaction
Explains acceptance conditions and prohibition of splitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-majlis and explain why splitting the transaction from one side is prohibited.

Core Idea
A sale is valid only if acceptance occurs in the same session for the entire item and price; one party cannot split the transaction unilaterally.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author explains the key conditions for a valid acceptance in a sale contract. The acceptance must be made in the same session (majlis) as the offer, and it must cover the entire item and price. Additionally, the text highlights the prohibition of one party splitting the transaction into parts without the other's consent, as it causes harm. Let's unpack these rules step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that when an offer (ijab) is made, the other party may accept in the same session for the entire item and price, or he may leave it. This establishes the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis) and the requirement of completeness.
The example of wheat and qafiz illustrates how the rule applies in practice.
2
Analyze the option to accept or reject
The commentary explains that the other party has the option (khiyar) to accept or reject; if he accepts, the contract is binding. The option is inherent because without it, the contract would bind without consent.
This option lasts only during the session, not indefinitely.
3
Understand the session requirement (majlis)
The acceptance must occur in the same session as the offer. The session is treated as one continuous period for deliberation. If the buyer takes a step or two before accepting, the sale is not valid in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), though some later scholars allow it if the response is directly connected.
This shows the Hanafi facilitation (taysir) while maintaining the session's unity.
4
Examine the prohibition of splitting the transaction
The transaction must be for all the sold item with all the price. One party cannot split it (e.g., buy part of the goods for part of the price) because that would harm the other party. This is a unilateral act; mutual consent would allow splitting.
The harm (darar) is the basis for the prohibition.

Source Text

Cream of the end for the care of the guard: (And when one makes an offer, the other accepts in the session for all the sold item with all the price, or he leaves it. Even if he says: How do you sell the wheat? He says: A qafiz for a dirham. He says: Measure for me five qafizes. So he measures, and takes it away. So this is a sale, and he owes five dirhams. Its form is that he places a fals for example, and takes the sold item valued thereby. According to some, from one side: i.e., from the side of the buyer or the seller. The second is an example of mu'atah from the side of the seller, and he mentioned the debt of five on the buyer so that it is determined that this mu'atah is not from his side but from the seller's side. [2] His statement: 'accepts... etc.'; i.e., the other has the option: if he wishes he accepts, and if he wishes he leaves it and returns it; because if the option were not established for him, the ruling of the contract would apply without his consent, and what we supposed to be a sale would not be a sale. Thus mentioned by al-Birjandi. [4] His statement: 'all the sold item with all the price'; not part of it with part of it; because that involves splitting the transaction, and one of the two contracting parties does not have the right to do that; because there is harm to one of them; therefore splitting the transaction from one of them is not permissible. (1) From al-Misbah al-Munir (p.511).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules of acceptance in a contract session (khiyar al-majlis) and the prohibition of splitting the transaction. The text states that when one party makes an offer (ijab), the other may accept in the same session for the entire item and entire price, or he may leave it (i.e., reject). An example is given: if the seller quotes a price per qafiz and the buyer asks for multiple qafizes, and the seller measures and hands over, it constitutes a valid sale, and the buyer owes the price. The commentary explains that the other party has the option (khiyar) to accept or reject; if he accepts, the contract is binding. The acceptance must be in the same session (majlis) because the buyer needs to deliberate; thus the session is treated as one continuous unit for the option. If the buyer walks away a step or two before accepting, the sale does not conclude according to the apparent narration, though some choose otherwise. Additionally, the transaction must be for the entire goods with the entire price; one party cannot split the transaction (e.g., buy part of the goods for part of the price) because that would harm the other party. The commentary also includes a linguistic note on the measurement 'qafiz'.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option for both parties to rescind the contract during the duration of the session in which the contract is made.
تفريق الصفقة (tasrif al-safqah): Splitting the transaction into parts, e.g., selling part of the item for part of the price, which is prohibited from one side without mutual consent.
القفيز (qafiz): A unit of dry measure equal to eight makayil (volumes), used for measuring grain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling if the buyer accepts after walking a step or two while the seller is still speaking?
A) The sale is valid because the session is still ongoing.
B) The sale is not valid in the apparent narration.
C) The sale is valid only if the price is paid immediately.
D) The sale is invalid because physical movement cancels the offer.

Key Takeaway
A valid sale requires acceptance in the same session for the entire item and price; one party cannot unilaterally split the transaction, as it causes harm.

Offer and Acceptance: Conditions and Termination of the Offer
Ruling with commentary on offer and acceptance, including revocation and standing up

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid offer and acceptance in a sale contract and explain when an offer becomes void before acceptance.

Core Idea
An offer becomes void if revoked or if either party stands up before acceptance.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary lays out the fundamental rules of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) in sale contracts. It emphasizes that for a sale to be valid, the acceptance must occur in the same session, for the entire merchandise at the full price. Crucially, the offeror may revoke the offer, or either party may terminate it by standing up from the session. Understanding these conditions prevents disputes over whether a contract was formed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on the validity of a sale contract when offer and acceptance occur. The ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih) only if the conditions are met.
The overall hukm level is sahih when conditions are satisfied.
2
Locate the condition of same session
The acceptance must occur in the same session (majlis) as the offer. The commentary provides an example of transacting while walking, where a step or two breaks the session and invalidates the sale.
The session is considered as one continuous time for ease, but departure (e.g., standing) terminates the offer.
3
Trace the effect of revocation and standing
The offer becomes void if the offeror revokes it before acceptance, or if either party stands up from the session. Standing is interpreted as a sign of disinterest or withdrawal.
Revocation is allowed because the offer does not bind until accepted; standing is explicitly mentioned as a cause of termination.
4
Note the exception for specifying individual prices
Generally, splitting a transaction (accepting part and rejecting part) is not allowed without the offeror's consent. However, if the offeror specifies a price for each item, it is treated as multiple offers, so the buyer may accept some and reject others.
This exception is linked to the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the word 'bai'' must be repeated.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that the word 'bai'' must be repeated for each item when offering individually priced goods; otherwise the sale is invalid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that detailing the price suffices, making the sale valid. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn.
This dispute shows how textual interpretation (repetition of lafz vs. meaning) affects practical rulings.

Source Text

(وما لم يقبل بطل الإيجابُ إِن رَجَعَ ) الموجب أو قام" أيهما عن مجلسه وإذا وُجِدَ أُلْزِمَ البيع )
[1] قوله : إلا إذا بَيَّن؛ أي الموجب ثمن كلِّ مما قبل الآخر ومما ترك ؛ لأن ذلك دليل على رضاه بالتفريق ؛ ولأنَّ الإيجاب حينئذ في معنى إيجاباتٍ متعدّدة ، أما إذا كرَّرَ في البيان لفظ البيع بأن قال : بعتك هذين بدرهمين، بعت هذا بدرهم ،
بدرهم، يجوز اتفاقاً.
وبعت هذا
وأما إذا لم يكرر بعتك هذين بدرهمين ، كلُّ واحدٍ بدرهم فيجوز عندها، خلافاً للإمام ؛ بناءً على أن البيع يتكرر بتكرر لفظ : بعتُ عنده، وبتفصيل الثمن عندهما ، كذا في أكثر المعتبرات. [٢]قوله : إن رجع ؛ لأنَّ المانع من الرجوع لزومُ إبطال حق الغير وهو منتف هاهنا ؛ لأنَّ الإيجاب لا يفيد الحكم بدون القبول فإن قيل : يلزم هاهنا إبطال حق الغير، فإنَّ الموجب إن كان المشتري ففي رجوعه إبطال حق البائع، وهو تملكه الثّمن، وإن كان البائع ففي رجوعه إبطال حق المشتري ، وهو تملكه المبيع. أجيب : بأن الحق للموجب ؛ لأنه أثبت ولاية للآخر؛ وبأن حق التملكِ لا يعارض حقيقة الملك للبائع ؛ لكونها أقوى منها. (۳) قوله : أو قام ؛ لأنَّ القيام دليل الإعراض والرجوع، ولهما ذلك قبل القبول، فإن قيل بعد القيام لا يفيد قبوله ؛ لأن الإيجاب بطل بما يدلُّ على الإعراض.
When one person makes an offer, the other may accept it in the session regarding the entire merchandise for the entire price, or he may leave it. And if he says, 'How do you sell the wheat?' So he says, 'A qafiz for a dirham.' So he says, 'Give me five qafizes, and he owes five.' So he measures it and takes it away. This is a sale, and he owes dirhams. (When one makes an offer, the other accepts in the session, the entire merchandise for the entire price, or he leaves it. And its example is not hidden, for its form is that he places a fils, for example, and takes the merchandise estimated by it. According to some, it is from one side: from the buyer or seller. He cited two examples: The first is an example of mutual dealing from the buyer's side; for this reason he mentioned bargaining, assumed the absence of a container and the buyer's departure to obtain a container, to verify that this mutual dealing is not from the seller's side. The second is an example of mutual dealing from the seller's side, and he mentioned the debt of five on the buyer to specify that this mutual dealing is not from his side but from the seller's side. [1] His statement: qafiz. Al-Misbah al-Munir says: 'Al-qafiz is a measure, it is eight measures, the plural is aqfizah and qifzan.' End. [2] His statement: accepted...etc.; i.e., the other has the option, if he wishes he may accept, and if he wishes he may leave it and reject it; because if he were not given the option, the ruling of the contract would bind him without his consent, and what we considered a sale would not be a sale. [3] His statement: in the session; because the buyer needs the doer in that he agrees with him or not, so considering the hours of the session as one hour is an ease. And in al-Khazanah: if two people transact while walking, and the seller says, 'I sold you such-and-such,' and the buyer says after taking one or two steps, 'I bought it,' the sale is not valid in the apparent narration; and it is said it is valid if the addressee responds connected to the address, and it is the chosen view according to some. Thus mentioned by al-Barjandi. [4] His statement: the entire merchandise for the entire price; not some for some; because that involves splitting the transaction, and one of the contracting parties does not have that right; because it causes harm to one of them; therefore splitting the transaction by one of them is not permissible. (1) From al-Misbah al-Munir (p.511). Except when he specifies the price for each, and what has not been accepted, the offer becomes void if the offeror revokes or if either of them stands up from his session; and when it is found, the sale is binding, except when he specifies the price for each, i.e., if he says, 'I sold this for a dirham and that for a dirham,' and he accepts one of them for a dirham, it is permissible. [2] (And what has not been accepted, the offer becomes void if the offeror revokes or if either of them stands up from his session; and when it is found, the sale is binding.) [1] His statement: except when he specifies; i.e., the offeror specifies the price for each of what is accepted and what is left; because that indicates his consent to splitting; and because the offer then is in the meaning of multiple offers. As for if he repeats the word 'sale' in the statement by saying, 'I sold you these two for two dirhams, I sold this for a dirham, and I sold this for a dirham,' it is valid by agreement. And if he does not repeat 'I sold you these two for two dirhams, each one for a dirham,' it is valid according to them, contrary to the Imam; based on the fact that the sale is repeated by the repetition of the word 'I sold' according to him, and by the detailing of the price according to them. Thus in most reliable works. [2] His statement: if he revokes; because the obstacle to revocation is the obligation to nullify the right of another, and it is absent here; because the offer does not effect a ruling without acceptance. If it is said: here it entails nullifying the right of another, for if the offeror is the buyer, in his revocation is nullification of the seller's right, which is his ownership of the price; and if he is the seller, in his revocation is nullification of the buyer's right, which is his ownership of the merchandise. It is answered: the right belongs to the offeror; because he established the authority for the other; and because the right of ownership does not oppose the actual ownership of the seller, because it is stronger. (3) His statement: or stands up; because standing up indicates disinterest and revocation, and they have that right before acceptance; if it is said that after standing up his acceptance is not effective, it is because the offer has been voided by what indicates disinterest.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of offer and acceptance in sales, specifically addressing when an offer is binding and when it becomes void. The commentator explains that when one party makes an offer, the other has the option to accept or reject it in the same session. The examples illustrate both buyer-initiated and seller-initiated transactions. The commentator clarifies that the acceptance must occur in the same session to give the other party a choice and avoid compulsion. He also discusses the rule that an offer for the entire merchandise must be for the entire price, as splitting the transaction without consent causes harm. The text then addresses the termination of an offer: it becomes void if the offeror revokes or if either party stands up from the session, as standing indicates withdrawal. An exception is made when the offeror specifies a price for each item, which is treated as multiple offers. The commentator notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the need to repeat the word 'sold' when specifying prices: according to Abu Hanifa, the word 'bai'' must be repeated, while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, detailing the price suffices. The relied-upon position is that detailing the price without repetition is sufficient.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ījāb): The offer made by one party to enter into a contract; it is the first statement that initiates the contract.
قبول (qabūl): The acceptance of the offer by the other party; it must occur in the same session as the offer to form a binding contract.
مجلس (majlis): The physical sitting or session where the contract negotiations take place; the offer and acceptance must occur in the same majlis for the contract to be binding.
تفريق الصفقة (tafrīq al-ṣafqah): Splitting the transaction by accepting part and rejecting part of the offer, which is generally not allowed unless the offeror specifies individual prices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to an offer in a sale contract if the offeror stands up from the session before acceptance?
A) The offer remains valid until the next session.
B) The offer becomes void, and acceptance after that is ineffective.
C) The offer is still valid if the offeree accepts immediately upon standing.
D) The sale is automatically concluded.

Key Takeaway
An offer in a sale contract is not binding until accepted; it becomes void if revoked or if either party stands up from the session, and acceptance must occur in the same session for the entire price.

Retraction and Standing in Offer: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Offers
Commentary on the rule of offer and acceptance in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on retraction and standing in sales offers and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding multiple offers when price is specified without repeating the word 'I sold'.

Core Idea
An offer may be retracted any time before acceptance; standing up from the session counts as retraction. Specifying price per item can create separate offers according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah delves into two key issues of offer and acceptance in sale contracts. First, it discusses when an offer becomes void, focusing on retraction and standing up from the session. Second, it examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement: whether a seller's statement that specifies a price for each item without repeating the word 'I sold' constitutes multiple offers, allowing the buyer to accept one item independently. Understanding these subtleties is critical for determining the validity of partial acceptances and the binding nature of offers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule on retraction and standing
The passage states that an offer (ijab) is void if the offeror retracts or either party leaves the session (qiyam) before acceptance. Note the rationale: no right of the other party is established yet.
Retraction is allowed because the offer does not become binding until acceptance. Standing is considered a clear indication of disinterest.
2
Analyze the exception for specifying prices per item
If the seller specifies a separate price for each item (e.g., 'this for a dirham, that for a dirham'), then accepting one item is valid. The reason is that this indicates consent to separate sales, making the offer effectively multiple offers.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) when the seller repeats the word 'bitu' (I sold) for each item – valid by agreement; (2) when he says 'I sell you these two for two dirhams, each for a dirham' without repeating 'bitu'. Abu Hanifa held that this is a single offer, so accepting only one item is not valid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad held that specifying the price makes them separate offers, so accepting one is valid.
The difference hinges on how multiple offers are indicated: repetition of the verb (Abu Hanifa) vs. specification of price (Sahibayn).
4
Understand the reasoning for retraction
The commentator addresses an objection: retraction might invalidate the other party's right. The rebuttal: the right belongs to the offeror because he initiated the permission; the other party's right to possess is weaker than the offeror's ownership.
This reasoning (talil) supports the general rule that an offer can be revoked before acceptance.
5
Apply the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentator notes that the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is accepted (yajuzu 'indahuma) and is relied upon (mufta bihi). Thus, in practice, if a buyer accepts one item from a bundle with specified prices, the sale is valid.
This is consistent with the principle of tas'hil (facilitation) in transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الا إذا بين ثمن كل، وما لم يقبل بطل الإيجاب إن رجع الموجب أو قام أيهما عن مجلسه وإذا وجد ألزم البيع إلا إذا بين ثمن كل أي إذا قال: بعت هذا بدرهم وذلك بدرهم، فقيل أحدهما بدرهم يجوز. [٢]- (وما لم يقبل بطل الإيجاب إن رجع الموجب أو قام أيهما عن مجلسه وإذا وجد ألزم البيع) [1] قوله: إلا إذا بين؛ أي الموجب ثمن كل مما قبل الآخر ومما ترك؛ لأن ذلك دليل على رضاه بالتفريق؛ ولأن الإيجاب حينئذ في معنى إيجابات متعددة، أما إذا كرر في البيان لفظ البيع بأن قال: بعتك هذين بدرهمين، بعت هذا بدرهم، وبعت هذا بدرهم، يجوز اتفاقاً. وأما إذا لم يكرر بعتك هذين بدرهمين، كل واحد بدرهم فيجوز عندهما، خلافاً للإمام؛ بناء على أن البيع يتكرر بتكرر لفظ: بعت عنده، وبتفصيل الثمن عندهما، كذا في أكثر المعتبرات. [٢] قوله: إن رجع؛ لأن المانع من الرجوع لزوم إبطال حق الغير وهو منتف هاهنا؛ لأن الإيجاب لا يفيد الحكم بدون القبول. فإن قيل: يلزم هاهنا إبطال حق الغير، فإن الموجب إن كان المشتري ففي رجوعه إبطال حق البائع، وهو تملكه الثمن، وإن كان البائع ففي رجوعه إبطال حق المشتري، وهو تملكه المبيع. أجيب: بأن الحق للموجب؛ لأنه أثبت ولاية للآخر؛ وبأن حق التملك لا يعارض حقيقة الملك للبائع؛ لكونها أقوى منها. (۳) قوله: أو قام؛ لأن القيام دليل الإعراض والرجوع، ولهما ذلك قبل القبول، فإن قيل بعد القيام لا يفيد قبوله؛ لأن الإيجاب بطل بما يدل على الإعراض.
Except when he specifies the price of each, and if the acceptance does not occur, the offer becomes void if the offeror retracts or either of them stands up from his session, and when the acceptance occurs, the sale becomes binding. Except when he specifies the price of each, i.e., if he says: I sold this for a dirham and that for a dirham, then accepting one of them for a dirham is permissible. [2] And if acceptance does not occur, the offer becomes void if the offeror retracts or either of them stands up from his session. [1] His statement: 'except when he specifies' means the offeror specifies the price of each of what is accepted and what is left; because that indicates his consent to separation; and because the offer in such case is in the meaning of multiple offers. As for if he repeats the word 'sale' by saying: I sold you these two for two dirhams, I sold this for a dirham, and I sold this for a dirham, it is permissible by agreement. As for if he does not repeat: I sold you these two for two dirhams, each one for a dirham, then it is permissible according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to the Imam (Abu Hanifa); based on the principle that the sale is repeated by the repetition of the word 'I sold' according to him, and by specifying the price according to them. This is as stated in most reliable works. [2] His statement: 'if he retracts' because the preventative from retraction is the necessity of invalidating the right of another, which is absent here; because the offer does not have effect without acceptance. If it is said: invalidating the right of another occurs here, for if the offeror is the buyer, his retraction invalidates the seller's right to possess the price; and if the offeror is the seller, his retraction invalidates the buyer's right to possess the sold item. The answer: the right belongs to the offeror because he established permission for the other; and because the right to possession does not oppose the actual ownership of the seller; as it is stronger. (3) His statement: 'or stands up' because standing indicates disinterest and retraction, and they have that right before acceptance. If it is said after standing acceptance is not effective because the offer became void by what indicates disinterest.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding offer and acceptance in a sale. The rule is that if a seller specifies the price for each item individually, such as saying 'I sell this for a dirham and that for a dirham,' then accepting only one of them is valid. However, if the offeror retracts his offer before acceptance, or if either party leaves the session (stands up), the offer becomes void. The commentator explains the reasoning: the offer does not create a binding obligation until accepted, so retraction is allowed because no one's right is established yet. He then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the seller says 'I sell you these two for two dirhams, each for a dirham' without repeating the word 'I sold,' is it considered a single offer or separate? According to Abu Hanifa, the offer is not considered separate because he did not repeat the word 'I sold'; thus accepting one item is not valid. But according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), specifying the price for each item makes it multiple offers, so accepting one is valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The commentator then discusses the issue of retraction: the offeror's right to retract is based on the fact that no acceptance has occurred, so no right of the other party is violated. If the offeror is the buyer, retraction affects the seller's right to the price, but the seller's claim is weaker than the buyer's actual ownership. Similarly, if the offeror is the seller, retraction affects the buyer's right to the item, but the buyer's right is weaker. Finally, standing up from the session is considered a clear indication of retraction, so the offer becomes void.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): Offer; the first statement in a contract expressing willingness to sell.
مجلس (majlis): The session of the contract; the place where the parties are negotiating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (mufta bihi), if a seller says, 'I sell you this ring for $100 and that watch for $200' without repeating 'I sold', and the buyer says, 'I accept the ring for $100', what is the ruling?
A) The sale is invalid because the seller did not repeat the word 'I sold'.
B) The sale is valid because specifying the price creates separate offers.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer also accepts the watch.
D) The sale is invalid because the offeror is required to repeat 'I sold'.

Key Takeaway
An offer can be retracted before acceptance or by leaving the session; when a seller specifies separate prices, each item is considered a separate offer, allowing partial acceptance per the mufta bihi view.

Sale by Indication and the Rejection of Khiyar al-Majlis
Commentary on sale by indication and refutation of khiyar al-majlis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a sale by indication without knowledge of quantity and state the argument against khiyar al-majlis using the Hanafi interpretation of the relevant hadith.

Core Idea
Pointing identifies the item so quantity need not be known; the contract binds immediately — no option to rescind after acceptance.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses two key Hanafi rules in sales: first, that a sale is valid when the object is physically present and pointed to, even if its exact quantity or description is unknown; second, that the contract becomes binding upon offer and acceptance, with no right to rescind during the session (khiyar al-majlis). The second rule directly contradicts the Shafi'i school. Understanding these points requires careful analysis of the hadith 'al-mutabayi'ani bil-khiyar ma lam yatafarraqa' and how Hanafis interpret 'tafarruq' (separation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on sale by indication
Find the statement that a sale of a specified object (al-mushar ilayh) is valid even without knowledge of its quantity or description. Note the exception for ribawi goods.
The passage says 'صَحَّ الْبَيْعُ فِي الْعِوَضِ الْمُشَارِ إِلَيْهِ بِلَا عِلْمٍ بِقَدْرِهِ وَوَصْفِهِ'.
2
Understand the reasoning
The reason given is that pointing is the strongest means of identification and prevents dispute when the object is present. Contrast with an unspecified object where description is needed.
3
Locate the dispute on khiyar al-majlis
The passage explicitly says 'لا يَثْبُتُ خِيَارُ الْمَجْلِسِ خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ'. This is the Hanafi position denying the option of the session.
4
Analyze the hadith interpretation
The Shafi'i proof is the hadith 'المتبايعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا'. The Hanafi response: the hadith refers to the negotiation stage (mubashara), not after the contract is complete. 'Tafarruq' means separation of speech (acceptance), not physical departure.
The text cites Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and al-Hidayah.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi fiqh, once the buyer says 'I accept', the sale is binding immediately; in Shafi'i fiqh, either party can rescind until they physically part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية وصح البيع في العوض المشار إليه بلا علم بقدره ووصفه، لا في غير المشار إليه أي لا يثبت خيار المجلس خلافاً للشافعي. ولما ذكر الإيجاب والقبول أراد أن يذكر الثمن والمبيع، وإنما قدم ذكر الثمن؛ لأنه وسيلة إلى حصول المبيع، وهو المقصود، والوسائل متقدمة على المقاصد، فقال: (وصح البيع في العوض المشار إليه بلا علم بقدره ووصفه، لا في غير المشار إليه) فإنه حينئذ لا بد أن يذكر قدره ووصفه. [1] قوله: خلافاً للشافعي، فإنه قال: لكل من العاقدين بعد تمام العقد، أن يرد العقد بدون رضاء صاحبه ما لم يتفرقا بالأبدان؛ لقوله: «المتبايعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا» أخرجه الأئمة الستة. ولنا: أن في الفسخ إبطال حق الغير فلا يجوز، والحديث محمول على خيار القبول كما نقل عن إبراهيم النخعي، وفيه إشارة إليه، فإنهما متبايعان حال المباشرة لا بعدها، أو يحتمله فيحمل عليه، والتفرق فيه تفرق الأقوال. كذا في «الهداية»، وزيادة التفصيل في «العناية»)، وغيرها من المبسوطات. [2] قوله: في العوض؛ مبيعاً كان أو ثمناً، فإن كل منهما عوض إلى الآخر، والحكم المذكور مشترك بينهما. [3] قوله: بلا علم... الخ؛ لأن الإشارة أقوى أسباب التعريف، وجهالة القدر والوصف معها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا تمنع الجواز؛ لأن العوضين حاضران والأموال الربوية مستثناة من هذا الحكم.
(From the summary of the commentary on the Wiqaya:) It is valid to sell a specified object without knowledge of its quantity and description, but not an unspecified object. That is, there is no option of the session, contrary to al-Shafi'i. When the author mentioned offer and acceptance, he wanted to mention the price and the item being sold. He presented the price first because it is a means to obtaining the item, which is the goal, and means precede ends. So he said: (It is valid to sell a specified object without knowledge of its quantity and description, but not an unspecified object) because in that case it is necessary to mention its quantity and description. [1] His statement: contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he said: Each of the two parties after the completion of the contract has the right to rescind the contract without the consent of the other as long as they have not physically separated, due to his statement: 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated' reported by the six imams. Our proof: In rescission there is invalidating the right of another, so it is not permissible. And the hadith is interpreted as referring to the option of acceptance, as narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, and there is an indication of that in the hadith, for they are 'two parties to a sale' during the negotiation, not after; or it may be interpreted that way, so we interpret it accordingly. And 'separation' in the hadith means separation of speech. This is as in al-Hidayah, with further detail in al-Inayah and other comprehensive works. [2] His statement: regarding the consideration, whether it is the sold item or the price, for each of them is a consideration for the other, and the mentioned ruling applies to both. [3] His statement: without knowledge... etc., because indicating (pointing) is the strongest means of identification, and ignorance of quantity and description with it does not lead to dispute, so it does not prevent validity; because the two considerations are present, and ribawi goods are excluded from this ruling.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage addresses two important points in Hanafi sales law. First, it establishes that the validity of a sale does not require exact knowledge of quantity or description if the item is physically present and pointed to. For example, if a person points to a pile of wheat and says 'I sell you this pile' without measuring it, the sale is valid according to the Hanafi school. This is because pointing is the clearest form of identification, and since the item is present, any potential dispute is avoided. However, this does not apply to ribawi (interest-based) goods such as wheat, barley, etc., where exact weight is required. Second, the passage negates the concept of khiyar al-majlis (option of the session), which is a feature in Shafi'i fiqh. According to al-Shafi'i, after the contract is concluded, both parties have the right to rescind the contract within the session until they physically separate. The Hanafi school rejects this because rescinding a completed contract invalidates the rights of the other party, which is impermissible. The hadith used by al-Shafi'i ('The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated') is interpreted by Hanafi scholars to refer to the option of acceptance during the negotiation stage, not after the contract is concluded. Moreover, the 'separation' in the hadith is understood as separation of speech (i.e., completing the acceptance), not physical departure. Thus, in Hanafi fiqh, once the offer is accepted, the contract is binding immediately, and there is no right to rescind without mutual consent.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option of the session; the right to rescind the contract while the parties are still together in the place of contracting.
عوض ('iwad): Countervalue; the item or price given in exchange in a sale.
مشار إليه (mushar ilayh): A specified object pointed to; the object of sale that is physically present and indicated.
مال ربوي (mal ribawi): Ribawi good; goods subject to riba such as gold, silver, wheat, barley, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a sale become binding?
A) Immediately upon offer and acceptance, with no right of rescission after.
B) Only after the parties physically separate.
C) After a cooling-off period of three days.
D) After payment is made.

Key Takeaway
A sale by indication is valid when the object is present, while the contract is binding immediately without the option of the session in the Hanafi school.

Validity of Sale with Pointed-to Countervalue and Rejection of Khiyar al-Majlis
Ruling on sale of pointed-to countervalue and dispute with Shafi'i on option of session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects khiyar al-majlis and state the conditions for a valid sale when the countervalue is identified by pointing.

Core Idea
A sale is valid when the item is pointed to, even if its measure is unknown; but once concluded, the contract is binding—no option of session.

When you buy something by pointing at it, the sale is valid even if you don't know its exact weight or description—because pointing is the clearest form of identification. But what happens after you both say 'I sell' and 'I buy'? Can you change your mind later? The Shafi'i school says yes, as long as you haven't left the meeting. The Hanafi school disagrees, and this passage explains why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-majlis
Recognise that the Hanafi position is that once offer and acceptance are exchanged, the sale is binding; there is no option period after the contract is concluded.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Review the hadith 'al-mutabay'ani bil-khiyar ma lam yatafarraqa' (The two transactors have the option as long as they have not separated) which the Shafi'i school uses to affirm khiyar al-majlis.
This hadith is narrated by the six major hadith collections.
3
Analyze the Hanafi criticism
Understand the Hanafi response: rescission invalidates the rights of the other party, and the hadith is interpreted as referring to the option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul) before finalization, not after.
The Hanafi interpretation reads 'separation' as separation of speech (tafarruq al-aqwal), not physical separation.
4
Note the practical consequence
Contrast the two schools: in Hanafi law, the contract is immediately binding; in Shafi'i, either party may rescind until they physically part.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (fruit of the difference).
5
Apply to pointed-to countervalue
The source also rules that pointing is sufficient identification, so ignorance of exact measure/description does not invalidate the sale, as long as the goods are present (except ribawi items).
Pointing is considered the strongest form of identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَصَح البيع في العوض المشار إليه بلا عِلْم بقَدْرِهِ ووَصفه، لا في غير المشار إليه أي لا يثبت خيار المجلس خلافاً للشافعي ولما ذكر الإيجاب والقبول أراد أن يذكر الثمن والمبيع وإنما قدم ذكر الثمن لأنه وسيلة إلى حصول المبيع وهو المقصود والوسائل متقدمة على المقاصد فقال: (وَصَح البيع في العوض المشار إليه بلا علم بقدره ووصفه لا في غير المشار إليه) فإنه حينئذ لا بد أن يذكر قدره ووصفه. قوله: خلافاً للشافعي، فإنه قال: لكل من العاقدين بعد تمام العقد أن يرد العقد بدون رضاء صاحبه ما لم يتفرقا بالأبدان لقوله: «المتبايعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا» أخرجه الأئمة الستة. ولنا: أن في الفسخ إبطال حق الغير فلا يجوز، والحديث محمول على خيار القبول كما نقل عن إبراهيم النخعي، وفيه إشارة إليه، فإنهما متبايعان حال المباشرة لا بعدها، أو يحتمله فيحمل عليه، والتفرق فيه تفرق الأقوال. كذا في الهداية وزيادة التفصيل في العناية وغيرها من المبسوطات. قوله: في العوض؛ مبيعاً كان أو ثمناً، فإن كل منهما عوض إلى الآخر، والحكم المذكور مشترك بينهما. قوله: بلا علم... الخ؛ لأن الإشارة أقوى أسباب التعريف، وجهالة القدر والوصف معها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا تمنع الجواز؛ لأن العوضين حاضران والأموال الربوية مستثناة من هذا الحكم.
The sale is valid for the countervalue that is pointed to without knowledge of its measure and description, not for that which is not pointed to, meaning the option of the session is not affirmed, contrary to al-Shafi'i. When he mentioned the offer and acceptance, he intended to mention the price and the sold item, and he presented the price because it is a means to obtain the sold item, and the means precede the ends. He said: (The sale is valid for the countervalue pointed to without knowledge of its measure and description, not for that which is not pointed to) because then it is necessary to mention its measure and description. His statement: contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he said: each of the two contracting parties, after completion of the contract, may rescind the contract without the consent of the other as long as they have not separated bodily, based on his saying: 'The two transactors have the option as long as they have not separated' – narrated by the six imams. Our proof: In rescission there is invalidation of the right of another, so it is not permissible. The hadith is interpreted as referring to the option of acceptance, as transmitted from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, and there is an indication to that in it, for they are transacting during the direct meeting, not after it, or it is possible so it is interpreted accordingly, and separation in it is separation of speech. Thus in al-Hidayah and with increased detail in al-'Inayah and other expansive works. His statement: regarding the countervalue, whether it is the sold item or the price, for each one is a countervalue to the other, and the mentioned ruling is common between them. His statement: without knowledge... etc., because pointing is the strongest cause of identification, and ignorance of measure and description does not lead to dispute, so it does not prevent validity, since both countervalues are present, and ribawi goods are excluded from this ruling.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) in sales. The matn states that a sale is valid when the countervalue (whether price or goods) is identified by pointing (ishara) even if its exact measure and description are unknown, but if it is not pointed to, then measure and description must be stated. The commentator clarifies that this ruling implies the Hanafi position that khiyar al-majlis does not apply after the contract is finalized. He then presents the Shafi'i view, which affirms khiyar al-majlis based on the hadith 'The two transactors have the option as long as they have not separated.' The Hanafi response is that rescinding a concluded contract invalidates the rights of the other party, and thus is impermissible. The hadith is interpreted as referring to the option of acceptance (khiyar al-qabul) before the contract is finalized, not after. The commentator also explains that 'separation' in the hadith means separation of speech (tafarruq al-aqwal), not physical separation. The commentary further notes that 'pointing' is the strongest form of identification, so ignorance of quantity/description when the items are physically present does not lead to dispute, except for ribawi goods which require exact equality. This ruling applies to both price and sold item as they are mutual countervalues.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyār al-majlis): The option to rescind a sale contract as long as the parties remain in the session (physical meeting).
العوض (al-'iwaḍ): The countervalue in a sale, encompassing both the price and the sold item.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing at the specific item being sold, considered the strongest form of identification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject khiyar al-majlis (option of the session) after the conclusion of a sale contract?
A) Because the hadith mentioning it is not authentic.
B) Because rescission would invalidate the right of the other party and the hadith is interpreted as referring to the option of acceptance during negotiation.
C) Because the Hanafi school does not recognize any options after a contract.
D) Because the price must be known exactly for the contract to be valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that khiyar al-majlis is not established; a sale becomes binding immediately upon conclusion of offer and acceptance, and pointing suffices to identify the countervalue.
