وإن باعَ المَدْرُوع هكذا أَخَذَ الأَقل بكلِّ الثَّمَنِ أو تَرَك ، والأكثر له بلا خيار للبائع (وإن باعَ المَدْرُوعَ هكذا أَخَذَ الأَقَلَّ بكلِّ الثَّمَنِ أَو تَرَك ، والأكثر له بلا خيار للبائع) : لأنَّ الذُّراعَ وصفٌ في الثَّوب، والمراد بالوصف : الأمر الذي إذا قام بالمحل يُوجِبُ في ذلك المحل حسناً أو قبحاً، فالكمية المحضة لا تكون من الأوصاف، بل هي أصل ؛ لأنَّ الكمية عبارة عن قلة الأجزاء أو كثرتها، والشَّيءُ إنما يوجد بالأجزاء، والوصفُ ما يَقومُ بالشَّيء فلا بُدَّ أن يكون مؤخرا عن وجودِ ذلك الشيء، فالكميَّةُ التي يَختلف بها الكيفيَّةُ كالدَّرْعِ فِي التَّوْبِ أَمرٌ يَختلفُ به حسن المزيد عليه. قوله : لأنّ ؛ حاصل الاستدلال أنَّ الذراع وصف، والثَّمن لا ينقسم على الأوصاف، فكان كلُّ الثَّمن مقابلاً لكل المبيع، إلا أنه ثبت الخيار للمشتري ؛ لأنه فاته وصف مرغوب فيه وقع عليه العقد، وما زاد فللمشتري، ولا خيار فيه للبائع ؛ لأنَّ الزائد هاهنا وصف، فكان هذا بمنزلة ما إذا باع بشرط أنه معيب، فإذا هو سليم، وقد عرفت مدار الاستدلال كونَ الذراع وصفاً. فيرد عليه : إن الذراع عبارة عن طول وعرض، فكما أنه وصف وعرض، كذلك القلة والكثرة، فكيف جعل الدراعَ الزَّائد وصفاً دون القفيز، ومن أين حكم في المسألة الأولى بأن الزائد للبائع، وفي هذه المسألة بأنَّ الأكثر للمشتري ؟ فأجاب بعضُهم : بأن ما يتعيَّبُ بالتَّبعيض والتنقيص بهما، فالزيادة والنقصان فيه وصف، وما لا يتعيّب بهما فالزيادة والنقصان فيه أصل، والثوب يتعيب بالتنقيص ، والحنطة لا تتعيب به. وبعضُهم : بأن الوصف ما لوجوده تأثير في تقويم غيره، ولعدمه تأثير في نقصان غيره، والأصلُ ما لا يكون كذلك، فالقدرُ في المكيلات والموزونات أصل، والدرع في المذروعات وصف. وبعضُهم : بأنّ ما لا ينتقص الباقي لفواته أصل، كما في الحنطة، وما ينتقص الباقي لفواتِهِ وصف كما في الثوب. وأجاب الشارح : بأن المراد بالوصف هاهنا هو الأمر الذي إذا قام بالمحل يوجبُ في ذلك المحل حسناً أو قبحاً كما في الثوب ، فإنَّ الدَّرعَ مثلاً في الثوب يوجب حسناً، فإنَّ التَّوْبَ إِذا كان عشرة أذرع يساوي عشرة دنانير، وإن كان تسعة أذرع لا يساوي تسعة دنانير ؛ لأنَّها لا تكفي جُبَّة ) ، والعشرة تكفي، فوجود الذراع الزائد على التسعة يزيد حسناً التّسعةَ فيصِيرُ كالأوصاف الزائدة، فلا يقابلها شيء من الثَّمَن : أَي الثَّمَنُ لا يَنْقَسِمُ على الأجزاء كما يَنْقَسِمُ في الحنطة ، فإِنَّهُ إذا كان عشرة أَقْفِرة بعشرة دراهم، كان قفيز واحدٌ بدرهم، ولا كذلك في الثوب ، فإذا باع عشرة أذرع بعشرة دراهم، فكان الثَّوْبُ تِسْعَةَ أذرع كما في مسألتنا لا يأخذه بتسعة، بل إن شَاءَ أخذ بعشرة وإنْ شاءَ ترك، وإن كان زائدا كان للمشتري ، فإنَّه باع هذا الثّوبَ فوجدَ المُشْتَرِي فيه أمراً مرغوباً فكان للمشتري، كما إذا اشترى عبدا فوجده كاتبا.
And if he sells the measured cloth in this manner, the buyer takes the lesser (amount) with the full price or leaves it, and the extra is for him without any option for the seller. (And if he sells the measured cloth in this manner, the buyer takes the lesser with the full price or leaves it, and the extra is for him without any option for the seller) because the cubit is a description in the cloth, and what is meant by description is: the attribute which, when it resides in a subject, necessitates in that subject beauty or ugliness. So mere quantity is not among the descriptions; rather it is an origin. Because quantity is an expression of the fewness or multitude of parts, and a thing only exists through its parts, and description is what subsists in the thing, so it must be posterior to the existence of that thing. So the quantity by which the quality varies, like the cubit in the cloth, is a matter by which the beauty of that which is increased differs. His statement: 'because'; the gist of the inference is that the cubit is a description, and the price is not divided over descriptions. So the entire price is in exchange for the entire sold item, except that the buyer is given an option because he missed a desired description which was the object of the contract. And what is extra belongs to the buyer, and there is no option for the seller, because the excess here is a description. So this is similar to when he sells with the condition that it is defective, but it turns out to be sound. And you have understood that the basis of the inference is that the cubit is a description. An objection is raised against it: the cubit is an expression of length and width; just as it is a description and an accident, similarly the fewness and multitude are also such. So how can the extra cubit be made a description without the (extra) qafiz? And from where is it judged in the first case that the excess belongs to the seller, and in this case that the majority belongs to the buyer? So some answered: that what is impaired by division and reduction in both, its increase and decrease are description, and what is not impaired by them, its increase and decrease are origin. And cloth is impaired by reduction, and wheat is not impaired by it. And some: that description is that whose existence has an effect on the valuation of other things, and whose non-existence has an effect on the reduction of other things, and the origin is not like that. So the capacity in measured and weighed items is origin, and the cubit in measured cloth items is a description. And some: that what the remainder does not diminish due to its loss is origin, like in wheat, and what the remainder does diminish due to its loss is description, like in cloth. And the commentator answered: that what is meant by description here is the matter which, when it resides in a subject, necessitates in that subject beauty or ugliness, as in cloth. For example, the cubit in cloth necessitates beauty (or ugliness). For if the cloth is ten cubits and equals ten dinars, and if it is nine cubits it does not equal nine dinars because it is insufficient for a cloak, while ten suffice. So the existence of the extra cubit beyond nine increases the beauty of the nine, so it becomes like extra descriptions, and none of the price is allocated to it. That is, the price is not divided over parts as it is divided in wheat. For if there are ten qafiz for ten dirhams, each qafiz is one dirham, but not so in cloth. So if he sells ten cubits for ten dirhams, and the cloth turns out to be nine cubits as in our case, he does not take it for nine; rather if he wishes he takes it for ten, and if he wishes he leaves it. And if it is extra, it belongs to the buyer, for he bought this cloth and found in it a desirable attribute, so it belongs to the buyer, just as if he bought a slave and found him literate.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the sale of measured cloth (المَدْرُوع). It addresses the legal outcome when the actual length of the cloth differs from the specified length in the contract. The ruling: If the cloth is less than specified, the buyer has the option to either take it at the full price or reject it. If it is more, the extra belongs to the buyer without giving the seller any option. The author then provides the reasoning: the cubit (ذراع) in cloth is considered a description (وصف), not a part of the essence (أصل). Since the price is paid for the entire sold item and not divided over descriptions, the full price corresponds to the whole cloth. Any deficiency in description gives the buyer an option, and any excess is a bonus for him. The passage then records an objection: if the cubit is a description, why is the qafiz (a measure for grain) not treated similarly? The answer is that cloth is impaired by reduction (a smaller piece loses functionality), while grain is not. Therefore, length in cloth is a description, while quantity in grain is part of the essence. The author follows the order: first states the ruling, then the proof, then the objection, then multiple responses, including that of the commentator (الشارح). The commentator emphasizes that the description is that which affects beauty or ugliness; a nine-cubit cloth may not suffice for a cloak and thus loses value, so the extra cubit enhances beauty and is like an extra description.

Key Terms
المَدْرُوع (al-madrū‘): Cloth sold by the cubit (ذراع), i.e., measured by length.
الوَصْف (al-waṣf): An attribute that, when present, adds beauty or ugliness to the subject; something that is not part of the essence.
الأَصْل (al-aṣl): The essence or substance of the item, such as quantity in grain; that which does not affect value or functionality when changed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must take it at a reduced price proportional to the shortage.

C) He can force the seller to provide the missing length.
D) He can take it at the full price only if he waives his rights.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a specification that is missing is a description (wasf) rather than part of the essence (asl), the buyer has an option to rescind or take at full price; any excess description is a bonus for the buyer.

Sale of Shares vs. Specific Measurements in a House
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of selling specified shares vs. measured portions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the sale of shares and the sale of measured portions in Hanafi law, and explain why the sale of specified cubits is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
A share is common and known; a measured portion is specific yet unknown — ignorance invalidates the sale.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of selling undivided shares versus specific measured portions of a house. While selling a fraction like ten shares out of a hundred is clearly valid, selling ten cubits out of a hundred cubits leads to disagreement between the founder of the school and his two chief disciples. Understanding the underlying reasoning sharpens your ability to identify when uncertainty in a sales contract renders it defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa (invalid for cubits, valid for shares) and the ruling of Sahibayn (valid in both cases).
This is the core ikhtilaf.
2
Understand the shared analogy
The Sahibayn argue that selling ten cubits from a hundred is equivalent to selling one-tenth of the house as a common share, so it should be valid by analogy to the share sale.
Their qiyas is based on the fraction being the same.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's distinction
Abu Hanifa points out that the object in the cubits sale is not a common share but the specific place occupied by those cubits. That place is a determined part, but its location in the house is unknown.
This makes the subject matter partially specified yet uncertain.
4
Apply the principle of jahalah
Uncertainty about the exact location of the sold portion amounts to jahalah (ignorance) in the subject matter, which invalidates the sale under Hanafi rules.
Compare to selling 'one of these two slaves' – also invalid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Despite the Sahibayn's respected view, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's: sale of specified measurements is invalid.
This is affirmed in Al-Hidayah and later works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بيع عشرة أسهم من مئةِ سَهم، لا بَيْعُ عشرة أذرع من مئة ذراع من دارٍ واعلم أنَّ المسألة فيما إذا باغ ثوباً على أنه عشرة أذرع بعشرة دراهم، كلُّ أَنَّهُ ذراع بدرهم، فإذا هو تسعة أذرع، أو أحد عشرة ذراعاً، حتى لو كان تسعة ونصفاً أو عشرة ونصفاً فحكمه ليس كذلك على ما سيأتي في هذه الصفحة. (وصح بيع عشرة أسهم من مئةِ سَهْم ، لا بَيْعُ عشرة أذرع من مئة ذراع من دار هذا عند أبي حنيفة الله ، وقالا : صح في الوجهين ؛ لأنه باع عشراً مشاعاً من الدار"، وله : أن في الثاني المبيع مَحَلُّ الذراع، وهو مُعيَّن مجهول لا مشاع : بخلاف السهم. [١] قوله : اعلم ؛ لما كان قول المصنف الله أخذ الأقل وكل الأكثر شاملاً للكسر أيضاً مع أن له حكم آخر بَيَّنَ الشارح الله بقوله : اعلم أنَّ المراد هاهنا ما عدا الكسر. [٢] قوله : لأنه باع عشرة مشاعاً من الدّار ؛ فإنَّ عشرة أذرع من مئة ذراع عشر الدار، فأشبه عشرة أسهم من مئة سهم في كونها عشراً، فتخصيص الجواز بأحدهما تَحكُم. (۳) قوله : له أن... الخ ؛ حاصله : أن المراد بقول البائع : عشرة أذرع محل الذراع لا نفسها ؛ لأنَّ الذراع وإن كان في الحقيقةِ آلة يذرعُ بها من الخشب وغيره لكن إرادتها هاهنا متعذرة، وهذا هو الظاهر ، فلا محال يصيرُ مجازاً لِمَا يَحِلُّه والمبيع المذروع. وهذا من قبيل إطلاق الحال وإرادة المحلّ، وهو أيّ محلّ الذراع جزء معين إلا أنه مجهول لا يعلم أن تلك الأذرع من الدار في الجانب الشرقي منها أو الغربي أو من صدر الدار أو أسفلها، والبيع إذا كان مجهولاً لا يصح البيع ، كما إذا قال البائع : بعت منك أحد هذين العبدين. فإن قلت : التعيين ينافي الجهالة؟ قلت : نعم في أمر واحد، وأما في الأمرين كما ترى في هذه الصورة فلا، ولما ثبت أنه معيّن مجهول فلا يكون مشاعاً ؛ لأنّ الشائع لا يتصور أن يذرع لشيوع ملكه في جميع أجزاء الشائع، فليس بمحل الدرع.
And the sale of ten shares from a hundred shares is valid, but the sale of ten cubits from a hundred cubits of a house is not valid. Know that the case is when he buys a cloth on the condition that it is ten cubits for ten dirhams, each cubit a dirham, and it turns out to be nine cubits or eleven cubits. Even if it is nine and a half or ten and a half, its ruling is not like that, as will come later on this page. (The sale of ten shares from a hundred shares is valid, but the sale of ten cubits from a hundred cubits of a house is not valid) – this is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is valid in both aspects, because he sold one-tenth common in the house. And he (Abu Hanifa) has the argument that in the second [sale of cubits], the sold item is the place of the cubit, which is a specific but unknown thing, not common, unlike the share. [1] He (the commentator) said: Know – since the author's statement 'take the lesser and cancel the greater' also includes fractions, but it has another ruling, the commentator explained by saying: Know that what is meant here excludes fractions. [2] He said: Because he sold ten cubits common in the house – for ten cubits from a hundred cubits are one-tenth of the house, so it resembles ten shares from a hundred shares in being a tenth. So specifying permissibility for one of them is arbitrary. [3] He said: For he has the argument that... the summary is that what the seller means by 'ten cubits' is the place of the cubit, not the cubit itself. Because the cubit, although in reality it is an instrument used for measuring made of wood or other material, intending it here is impossible. This is apparent, so it necessarily becomes a metaphor for what it occupies (the measured area). The sold item is the measured [space]. This is an instance of using the name of the container to mean the contained. That is, the place of the cubit is a specific part, but it is unknown, not known whether those cubits from the house are on the eastern side or western side, or from the front or back of the house. And when the sale is unknown, the sale is not valid, as if the seller said: I sold you one of these two slaves. If you say: Specificity negates ignorance? I say: Yes in one matter, but regarding two matters, as you see in this case, no. And since it is established that it is specific but unknown, it is not common, because the common cannot be measured because ownership is shared in all parts of the common, so it is not a place for measurement.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of selling a specific measurement of a house versus a share. The author first states the ruling: selling ten shares out of a hundred is valid, but selling ten cubits out of a hundred cubits of a house is invalid. The commentator clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ: they consider both sales valid because selling ten cubits out of a hundred is effectively selling one-tenth of the house as a common share, analogous to the share case. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that when a seller says 'ten cubits,' he does not mean the measuring tool but the place (the area) that those cubits occupy. That place is a specific part of the house, but it is unknown which part (front, back, etc.). This partial specification without full knowledge constitutes ignorance about the subject matter, rendering the sale invalid. He compares it to selling 'one of these two slaves' – that is invalid because the exact subject is unknown. The commentator anticipates an objection: if it is specific, how can it be unknown? He answers: specification here is in one sense (it is a specific area of ten cubits), but ignorance remains in another (its location). Since it is not wholly common property (which would be undifferentiated), it cannot be measured as a common share. Thus Abu Hanifa's position is that the sale of measured portions is invalid, while the sale of shares is valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as stated in the Hidayah and other works.

Key Terms
سهم (sahm): A share or fraction of common property, here specifically a share in the house.
ذراع (dhira'): A cubit, a unit of length. In this context, it means the place (area) that a cubit measures, not the measuring instrument.
مشاع (musha'): Common property, undivided shared ownership in every part of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the sale of ten cubits from a hundred cubits of a house invalid?
A) Because the seller does not own the cubits.
B) Because the exact location of the cubits is unknown, leading to jahalah.
C) Because the price is uncertain.
D) Because the buyer does not have the right to choose the location.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between common shares and specific measurements hinges on whether the subject matter is known in proportion or location; ignorance in location invalidates the sale.

Sale of a Bundle with Uncertain Contents
Ruling and reasoning for invalidity of selling a bundle with known count but actual amount differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a bundle described as having a specific count when the actual count differs, and explain the reasoning based on jahalah.

Core Idea
Selling a bundle described as ten pieces is invalid if the actual count differs — either price or object becomes unknown.

This passage addresses a common commercial scenario: a seller offers a bundle of cloth described as containing ten pieces for a fixed price, but the bundle actually contains nine or eleven. The Hanafi school rules the sale invalid (fasid) due to ignorance (jahalah) of either the price (if fewer) or the exact object (if more). Understanding this ruling is crucial for identifying when uncertainty in a contract renders it legally defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states the sale is invalid (fasid) when the bundle contains less or more than the stated ten pieces. Pinpoint the exact verdict for both scenarios.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the total area or number is known, as per the majority Hanafi position.
2
Analyze the reasoning for fewer pieces
If the bundle has fewer than ten (e.g., nine), the price of the missing piece is unknown because the total price was set for ten. The value of the absent piece is not known, so the price of the present nine also becomes unknown, leading to jahalah in the price.
This is the first type of jahalah: ignorance of the price.
3
Analyze the reasoning for more pieces
If the bundle has more than ten (e.g., eleven), one piece must be returned. Since the pieces are not identical, it is unclear which piece is to be returned—the seller will demand the best, the buyer will return the worst. This makes the object of sale (the returned piece) unknown, invalidating the contract.
This is the second type of jahalah: ignorance of the object.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Khassaf reported a view that if the total measurement (e.g., total area) is known, the sale is permissible, analogous to selling sheep from a flock with known total number. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the invalidity, as affirmed in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.
The majority holds that jahalah remains even if the total is known, because the specific items are not individually identified.
5
Apply the ruling to practice
In modern terms, if someone buys a box of '10 items' for a fixed price but finds 9 or 11, the sale is invalid. The buyer can return the entire bundle or renegotiate with clear terms.
This protects against disputes arising from uncertainty.

And there is no sale of a bundle (عِدْل) on the condition that it is ten pieces of cloth, and it is less or more. This is because if it is less, he does not know the price of what is not present, so the share of what is present becomes unknown. And if it is more, the sold item is not known. The author's statement is in accordance with what Abu Zayd al-Sharuti mentioned about this issue: that the sale is invalid according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) even if the total measurement is known. This is the answer in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and it is the correct view, because ignorance remains whether the total measurement is known or not. The buyer demands from the front of the house and the seller delivers from the back, leading to dispute. And al-Khassaf mentioned that the invalidity of the sale in this issue according to Abu Hanifa is due to ignorance of the total measurement. [1] He said: Because... the summary is that when the bundle is less than ten pieces, e.g., nine, then the price of one piece is deducted, and it is unknown. Because if he did not name a price for each piece, the total price is divided among all pieces according to value, and the absent piece is not known whether it was good, medium, or poor, so its value is unknown. When the share of the absent piece becomes unknown, the price of the remaining nine also becomes unknown, and ignorance of the price necessitates invalidity of the sale. When it is more than ten pieces, one piece is to be returned, and the pieces are different, so it is not known which piece must be returned – the seller demands the good one, and the buyer returns the poor one. Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and reasoning for the invalidity of selling a bundle (عدل) described as containing ten pieces of cloth when it actually contains less or more. The ruling is that such a sale is invalid (fasid). The commentator explains the reasoning: if the bundle has fewer pieces (e.g., nine), then the price of the missing piece is unknown because the total price was set for ten, and the value of the missing piece is not known (good, medium, or poor). This ignorance of part of the price makes the entire price unknown, invalidating the sale. If the bundle has more than ten, then one piece must be returned, but which piece? The seller wants a good one, the buyer returns a poor one, so the exact object of sale is unknown. Again, this ignorance invalidates the sale. The text also clarifies that this ruling is from Abu Hanifa: the sale is invalid even if the total measurement (of the house or bundle total) is known, because the specific parts are unknown. Some, like al-Khassaf, held a different view within the Hanafi school – that if the total area is known, it is permissible – but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the first (invalidity), as affirmed in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
عدل (‘idl): A bundle or set of similar items, here of cloth, sold together for a total price.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance about a material fact of the contract, such as price or object, that invalidates the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the seller misrepresented the goods.
D) Because the price becomes too low.

Key Takeaway
Selling a bundle described as having a specific count is invalid if the actual count differs, because jahalah in the price (if fewer) or the object (if more) renders the contract fasid.

Sale of a Bundle with Unknown Quantity: Jahalah Invalidates
Ruling on sale of bundle with quantity discrepancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the quantity differs from the description and evaluate the two reports from Abu Hanifa on this issue.

Core Idea
Uncertainty (jahalah) in the subject matter or price invalidates the sale even if the total measure is known.

This passage addresses a common scenario in transactions: a seller describes a bundle as containing ten garments, but the actual number is either less or more. The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is invalid (fasid) due to ignorance (jahalah) that leads to dispute. However, there is an intra-madhhab difference on whether knowing the total length of the fabric resolves the uncertainty. Let us examine the ruling, its proof, and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the ruling
The passage discusses a bundle (عدل) sold as ten garments but actually containing less or more. The ruling is that the sale is invalid (fasid). Note the two scenarios: less or more.
Both scenarios involve uncertainty.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
If the bundle is less, the price of the missing garment is unknown, making the price for the present garments uncertain. If it is more, the exact sold item is unidentified. Both cases lead to gharar and potential dispute.
Gharar is prohibited in transactions.
3
Distinguish the two reports from Abu Hanifa
According to the report in al-Jami' al-Saghir (the preferred view), the sale is invalid even if the total length (ذرعان) is known. Another report from al-Khassaf holds that if the total length is known, the sale is valid, analogizing to selling each sheep of a known flock for a fixed price.
The difference hinges on whether the specific items are identified.
4
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) from al-Khassaf
Al-Khassaf compares the bundle case to selling each sheep of a flock for ten dirhams. If the total number of sheep is known, the sale is valid because each sheep is individually defined by its context. Similarly, if the bundle's total length is known, the uncertainty is removed.
The preferred view rejects this analogy due to the persistent uncertainty of which garments are sold.
5
Identify the preferred view (mufta bihi)
The text confirms that the first report (invalid even if length is known) is the correct one (الصحيح), as stated in al-Hidayah, al-Kifayah, and others. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This view prioritizes avoiding any potential dispute arising from uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بيع عدل على أنه عشرة أثواب، وهو أقل أو أكثر: لأنه إذا كان أقل لا يدري ثمن ما ليس بموجود، فيكون حصة الموجود مجهولة، وإن كان أكثر لا يكون المبيع معلوماً. وعبارة المصنف له على وفق ما ذكره أبو زيد الشروطي له في هذه المسألة من أن البيع فاسد عند أبي حنيفة له وإن علم جملة الدرعان، وهو جواب «الجامع الصغير» وهو الصحيح؛ لبقاء الجهالة علم جملة الدرعان أو لم يعلم، فالمشتري يطالبه من مقدم الدار، والبائع يسلم من مؤخرها فيؤدي إلى النزاع. وذكر الخصاف له أن فساد البيع في هذه المسألة عند أبي حنيفة؛ لجهالة جملة الدرعان، أما إذا عرفت مساحتها يجوز عنده، وجعل هذه المسألة على قياس ما لو باع كل شاة من القطيع بعشرة، إن كان عدد جملة الشاة معلوماً يجوز عنده، والأول الصحيح، كذا في «الهداية» و«الكفاية» وغيرهما.
And there is no valid sale of a bundle (count) as ten garments while it is less or more: because if it is less, the price of what is not present is not known, so the share of what is present is unknown; and if it is more, the sold item is not known. The wording of the author follows what Abu Zaid al-Sharuti mentioned for him in this issue: that the sale is invalid according to Abu Hanifa even if the total measure is known, and this is the answer of al-Jami' al-Saghir, and it is the correct one because ignorance remains whether the total measure is known or not; the buyer demands from the front of the house and the seller gives from the back, leading to dispute. Al-Khassaf mentioned for him that the invalidity of the sale in this issue according to Abu Hanifa is due to ignorance of the total measure; however, if its area is known, it is permissible according to him. He made this issue analogous to selling each sheep of a flock for ten; if the total number of sheep is known, it is permissible according to him. The first is correct, as in al-Hidayah, al-Kifayah, and others.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It discusses the sale of a bundle (عدل) that is described as containing ten garments, but the actual number is either less or more. The ruling is that such a sale is invalid (فاسد) due to ignorance (جهالة) in the transaction. The reasoning is twofold: if the bundle contains fewer than ten, the price of the non-existent garment is unknown, making the price of the existing garments uncertain; if it contains more, the exact sold item is unknown because the extra garment is unidentified. The author then mentions two reports from Abu Hanifa. According to the report from Abu Zaid al-Sharuti and the answer in al-Jami' al-Saghir, the sale is invalid even if the total length (ذرعان) is known, because ignorance remains regarding which garments are sold. Another report from al-Khassaf states that the invalidity is specifically due to ignorance of the total length; if the total area is known, the sale is valid. This is analogized to selling each sheep of a flock for ten dirhams—if the total number is known, it is valid. The preferred view (الصحيح) is the first, as affirmed in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
عدل (‘idl): A bundle or package; a similar quantity or equivalent. Here it refers to a bundle of garments.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding a crucial element of the transaction, such as price or subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because of ignorance (jahalah) that leads to dispute
C) Because the seller is dishonest
D) Because the buyer does not inspect the goods

Key Takeaway
A sale where the quantity differs from the described amount is invalid due to jahalah, and knowing the total measure does not cure this uncertainty according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Sale of Bundle with Price per Piece: Validity and Option
Ruling when seller specifies price per piece in a bundle with unknown total quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a seller specifies a price per piece in a bundle with an unknown total quantity and explain the basis for the buyer's option (khiyar).

Core Idea
Specifying a price per piece saves the sale for existing items but gives the buyer a choice when missing; excess items void the sale.

Imagine you agree to buy a bundle of 10 shirts, each for $20, but later discover only 9 shirts inside. Is the sale valid? What if there are 11 shirts? This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on sales where the total quantity is unknown but the price per piece is fixed. The key is whether the uncertainty affects the validity of the contract for the actual existing items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two possible outcomes
The seller says 'I sell you this bundle containing 10 pieces, each for a dirham.' The actual count may be less (e.g., 9) or more (e.g., 11). Determine the ruling for each.
The price per piece is known; only the total count is uncertain.
2
Apply the ruling for the lesser quantity
If the actual count is less than stated, the sale is valid (sahih) for the existing pieces, and the buyer has an option (khiyar) to accept or cancel. This is because the price per piece is known, eliminating ignorance (jahalah) about the price, but the missing piece may have been superior, so the buyer is given a choice.
The buyer's option compensates for the potential loss of a better piece.
3
Apply the ruling for the greater quantity
If the actual count is greater than stated, the sale is invalid (fasid) for the entire contract. The reason is that the extra piece is unidentified, creating ignorance of the sold item (jahalah fi al-mabi').
The contract cannot be partially valid for some pieces and not others in this case.
4
Understand the legal basis (dalil)
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). For the lesser, the known price per piece makes the contract sound; for the greater, the unknown extra piece nullifies it due to jahalah.
Istihsan overrides the general principle that uncertainty in quantity invalidates the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بَيَّنَ لكلِّ ثَمَناً صَحَ في الأقل بقدرِه، وخُيِّر، وفسد في الأكثر: لأنَّ المبيع مجهول.
And if he specified a price for each, it is valid for the lesser to the extent of it, and he has the option, and invalid for the greater: because the sold item is unknown.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It follows the previous discussion: if a seller says 'I sell you this bundle on the condition it contains ten garments, each for a dirham,' but the actual count is less (e.g., nine), the sale is valid for the nine that exist, because the price per piece is known. The buyer has the option (خيار) to take them or not, since the missing piece may have been better. However, if the actual count is more (e.g., eleven), the sale is invalid because the extra garment is unidentified, causing ignorance of the sold item.

Key Terms
خُيِّر (khuyyir): The buyer is given the option (khiyar) to either accept the sale or cancel it.
جاهلة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about a material element of the contract (price, subject matter) that can render a sale invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a seller says 'this bundle of 10 garments, each for 1 dirham,' but there are actually 9 garments?
A) The sale is completely invalid because of the shortage
B) The sale is valid for 9 garments and the buyer has an option
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer pays for 10
D) The sale is valid for 10 garments based on the description

Key Takeaway
When a bundle is sold per piece with a known price, the sale is valid for the actual existing pieces (with buyer's option if fewer) but invalid if there are more pieces than stated due to harmful uncertainty.

Sale of Cloth with Discrepancy in Length
Rulings and intra-Hanafi differences when a cloth sold by the yard differs from the described length

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings and differences between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad when a cloth sold by the yard is longer or shorter than described, and apply the proportional pricing rule.

Core Idea
When cloth is longer, buyer benefits without option; when shorter, buyer has option to reduce price or reject.

This lesson deals with a common scenario in sales: what happens when the actual length of a cloth differs from what was stated in the contract? The Hanafi school has a default ruling based on the principle that each cubit is priced individually, but the two leading students of Abu Hanifa differed on the calculation. We will examine the positions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and default ruling
Identify the described sale: 10 cubits at 1 dirham each. If the actual length is 10.5 cubits, the buyer takes it at 10.5 dirhams with no option. If it is 9.5 cubits, the buyer may take it at 9.5 dirhams by choice.
The default ruling implies that each cubit is priced proportionally.
2
Identify the underlying principle
The text says: 'because it necessarily follows from matching the cubit with the dirham that half matches half.' This means the price is proportional to the actual length.
This principle protects both parties from unfair advantage.
3
Explain Abu Yusuf's alternative view
Abu Yusuf holds that if the cloth is longer (10.5), the buyer may take it at 11 dirhams (the price for 10 cubits plus an extra cubit at the same rate). If shorter (9.5), the buyer may take it at 10 dirhams (the full agreed price for 10 cubits, not reduced).
Abu Yusuf treats the description as binding for the total price, so extra length is added at the same per-unit rate and shortage is not deducted.
4
Explain Muhammad's alternative view
Muhammad holds that the price is exactly proportional to the actual length: 10.5 dirhams for 10.5 cubits, and 9.5 dirhams for 9.5 cubits. This matches the default ruling.
Muhammad's view is the most consistent with the principle that each cubit is priced individually.
5
Determine the practical difference and the relied-upon view
The practical difference is the amount the buyer pays. In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of Muhammad, i.e., proportional pricing. Abu Yusuf's view is considered less preferred.
The text initially presents the proportional view as the default, indicating its preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بيع ثوب على أنه عشرة أذرع، كلُّ ذراع بدرهم أخذ بعشرة في عشرة ونصف بلا خيار، وبتسعة في تسعة ونصف إن شاء، وقال أبو يوسف: إن شاء أخذ بأحد عشر في الأول وبعشرة في الثاني، وقال محمد له: إن شاء أخذ بعشرة ونصف في الأول وبتسعة ونصف في الثاني: لأن من ضرورة مقابلة الذراع بالدرهم مقابلة نصفه بنصفه.
And in the sale of a cloth on the basis that it is ten cubits, each cubit for a dirham: he takes it at ten and a half without option, and at nine and a half if he wishes. Abu Yusuf said: He may take it at eleven in the first and at ten in the second if he wishes. Muhammad said: He may take it at ten and a half in the first and at nine and a half in the second if he wishes, because it necessarily follows from matching the cubit with the dirham that half matches half.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It deals with selling a cloth described as ten cubits long, each cubit for one dirham. If the cloth actually measures ten and a half cubits, the buyer may take it at ten and a half dirhams without option (since he got extra length). If it measures nine and a half cubits, he may take it at nine and a half if he wishes, because the cloth is shorter. The text then presents differences among the Hanafi masters: Abu Yusuf holds that in the first case (extra length) the buyer may take it at eleven dirhams (i.e., the full price for ten cubits plus an extra cubit at the same rate), and in the second case at ten dirhams (i.e., the price for ten cubits, not reduced). Muhammad holds that in the first case the buyer may take it at ten and a half (matching the length), and in the second at nine and a half. The reasoning is that since each cubit is matched to a dirham, half a cubit corresponds to half a dirham. The text does not explicitly state which view is preferred, but the initial statement (without explicit attribution to a specific imam?) seems to align with Muhammad's view. In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon view is that of Muhammad (as per the text's phrasing 'أخذ بعشرة ونصف...' being the default).

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira‘): A unit of length, a cubit (approx. 45-50 cm).
خيار (khiyar): Option; here the buyer has the option to accept or reject the cloth when it is shorter than described.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the default (initial) view in the passage, if a cloth described as 10 cubits at 1 dirham each is actually 10.5 cubits, what is the price and option?
A) Buyer takes at 10.5 dirhams, no option
B) Buyer takes at 11 dirhams, with option
C) Buyer takes at 10 dirhams, no option
D) Buyer can choose any price

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, when cloth is sold by the yard, the price is proportional to the actual length, with the buyer having an option only if the cloth is shorter than described.

Selling a Garment by the Cubit: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Excess and Deficiency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of a garment by the cubit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the sale of a garment by the cubit when the actual length differs, and apply the mufta bihi position to a given scenario.

Core Idea
When a garment sold by the cubit deviates from the stipulated length, the buyer's rights depend on whether the deviation is an excess or deficiency.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of a garment measured by the cubit. The matn gives the basic ruling, and the commentary elaborates the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how a single condition—price per cubit—generates different rules on price adjustment and buyer's option.

Guided Path
1
State the sale scenario and the factual variation
A garment is sold as ten cubits at one dirham per cubit. The actual length may be exactly ten, ten and a half, or nine and a half cubits. Identify which variation triggers the dispute.
The matn specifically mentions half-cubit deviations.
2
Identify the matn's ruling (the relied-upon position)
According to the matn, for excess the buyer takes at the proportional price (10.5 dirhams) without a choice; for deficiency the buyer takes at the proportional price (9.5 dirhams) with a choice.
This is the mufta bihi position of Abu Hanifa as adopted in al-Hidayah.
3
Compare the three imams' positions
Abu Hanifa: proportional price, no choice in excess, choice in deficiency. Abu Yusuf: price unchanged (10 dirhams), choice in both. Muhammad: proportional price, choice in both.
The difference hinges on how each imam treats the cubit: as a description (Abu Hanifa), as an independent unit (Abu Yusuf), or as a mix (Muhammad).
4
Trace each imam's legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Abu Hanifa: the cubit is originally a description, but by conditioning price per cubit it takes the ruling of a quantity. For excess, the fraction reverts to a description (like extra quality) so no choice. For deficiency, the desired description is lost so buyer has a choice. Abu Yusuf: each cubit is like a separate garment, so price stays the same and buyer has choice due to variation. Muhammad: proportional price is necessary but buyer has choice because the condition was not fully met.
The Hanafi response favours Abu Hanifa's view as more consistent with the principle that descriptions do not command a portion of the price unless explicitly conditioned.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a new scenario
Given a garment sold as 10 cubits at 1 dirham each, found to be 9.8 cubits (deficiency), the buyer must pay 9.8 dirhams and has a choice to accept or reject. For an excess of 10.2 cubits, the buyer must pay 10.2 dirhams without a choice.
The mufta bihi applies proportionally even for fractional deviations beyond half a cubit.

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of a garment when specified as a certain number of cubits at a price per cubit, and the actual length is found to be more or less by half a cubit. The text first gives the basic ruling from the matn: if the garment is exactly ten cubits, the buyer takes it at ten dirhams; if it is ten and a half, he takes it at ten and a half dirhams without a choice; if it is nine and a half, he takes it at nine and a half dirhams with a choice. The commentary explains that in the excess case, since the contract covered ten cubits, the extra half cubit is considered a separate item that the buyer must return, making the sale potentially invalid due to unknown excess. In the deficiency case, the buyer has a choice because the stipulated length was not fulfilled. The dispute arises between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf treats each cubit as an independent unit (like a garment), so the price does not change; the buyer only has a choice. Muhammad holds that the price adjusts proportionally to the actual length, and the buyer has a choice in both excess and deficiency. Abu Hanifa's position, as elaborated in the commentary, is that the cubit is originally a description (not a priced unit), but it takes the ruling of a quantity by the condition 'each cubit for a dirham'. Therefore, if the condition is fully met (exactly ten cubits), the contract proceeds as is. If there is an excess (half cubit), that excess reverts to being a description (like extra quality), so the buyer must take the garment at the proportional price (ten and a half dirhams) without a choice — because the excess is not a separate sale but an enhancement. In the case of deficiency, the buyer has a choice because the desired description (length) is lacking. The commentary further notes that for coarse cloth that does not vary in quality, the buyer is not harmed by the excess, and selling a cubit of such cloth is permissible. The ultimate ruling relied upon (as per al-Hidayah) is that the buyer must take the garment at the proportional price for the excess case without choice, and has a choice only in the deficiency case.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A unit of length (cubit) used in measuring cloth.
ثوب (thawb): A garment; the object of sale in this case.
وصف (wasf): An attribute or description of the sold item (here, length is considered a description).
خيار (khiyar): The option to rescind the sale given to the buyer due to a defect or variation from what was stipulated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is void because of unknown excess.
B) The buyer must take the garment at ten and a half dirhams without a choice.
C) The buyer pays ten dirhams and has a choice to accept or reject.
D) The buyer pays ten dirhams and takes the garment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi position is that excess length is treated as a description enhancement (buyer pays proportionally without choice), while deficiency gives the buyer a choice.

Deficiency or Excess in Yardage Sale: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on price adjustment for cloth length shortage or excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on price adjustment for fractional excess or deficiency in a yardage sale and explain the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Fractional excess in a yardage sale is like improved quality—no extra charge; fractional deficiency gives option, not price reduction.

When a cloth is sold by the yard with a fixed price per cubit, what happens if the buyer finds a fractional excess or deficiency? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, two founding imams of the Hanafi school, reached different conclusions based on how they characterize the legal nature of the cubit in this transaction. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequences for buyers and sellers.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Consider a sale where the seller says 'each cubit for a dirham' and the total is stipulated as 10 cubits. The buyer finds exactly 10.5 or 9.5 cubits. What is the price and does the buyer have an option?
The dispute assumes the sale contract is valid; the issue is the legal consequence of the fractional difference.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's analogy
Abu Yusuf treats each cubit as an independent item (like a separate garment). If one item is deficient, the buyer has the option to return the whole but no price reduction, because length is a description.
He analogizes to selling a garment described as 5 cubits but found to be 4: no price reduction, only option.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's distinction
Abu Hanifa argues that the cubit is originally a description. It only becomes a quantity by the condition 'each cubit for a dirham'. The condition is limited to full cubits; a fraction is not a cubit. So for excess, the condition fails and the original description applies (no extra charge). For deficiency, the condition is partially unfulfilled, giving the buyer an option.
The key is that a fraction does not satisfy the condition; thus the price remains for the full stipulated cubits.
4
Compare the practical outcomes
Under Abu Hanifa, if the cloth is 10.5 cubits, the buyer pays 10 dirhams (no extra). Under Abu Yusuf, the buyer also pays 10 dirhams but has the option to return. For 9.5 cubits, both agree the buyer pays 10 dirhams and has the option, but Abu Yusuf denies any price reduction.
The difference is primarily in the treatment of excess: Abu Hanifa sees it as an uncompensated benefit; Abu Yusuf sees it as giving option.
5
Identify the preferred view
Later Hanafi authorities generally prefer Abu Hanifa's reasoning. The text notes that some follow Muhammad al-Shaybani's view (not detailed here). In practice, the mufta bihi is often Abu Hanifa's position, meaning no price adjustment for fractions.
The commentary in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah does not explicitly state a tarjih, but Al-Hidayah and later works lean towards Abu Hanifa.

Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale of cloth by the yard. The issue: if the seller says 'each cubit for a dirham' and the buyer finds the cloth to be ten and a half cubits or nine and a half cubits (instead of ten), what is the price? Abu Yusuf analogizes each cubit to a separate garment: if a garment is sold as five cubits but found to be four, no price reduction occurs because the length is a description, but the buyer has the option to rescind. Here, similarly, partial deficiency does not reduce price; the buyer may return the whole cloth or accept it at the full price. Abu Hanifa argues that the cubit is originally a description, but here it is treated as a quantity by the condition ('each cubit for a dirham'). The condition is tied to full cubits; a fraction is not a cubit. Thus, for an excess fraction, the original principle (description) applies, so the excess is like an improvement in quality (no extra charge). For a deficiency, the condition fails, so the buyer has an option due to loss of the desired description. In non-differential coarse cloth, the buyer may not have the option to reject the excess because it is like a weighed item. The passage is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the matn of Al-Wiqayah.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A unit of length (cubit) used in cloth sale; treated as either quantity or description depending on context.
وصف (wasf): Attribute or quality; here it refers to length as a property of the cloth, not its substance.
خيار (khiyar): Option to rescind the contract; here the buyer gets the option if the cloth is deficient in length.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 10 dirhams
B) 10.5 dirhams
C) 9.5 dirhams
D) He has the option to pay 10 or return

Key Takeaway
In a yardage sale, fractional excess is uncompensated and does not affect price; fractional deficiency gives the buyer an option to rescind but does not reduce the price.

Selling Grain in Its Ear: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on selling grain in husk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling grain in its ear and explain why the Shafi'i new opinion opposes it, using the Hanafi response and analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Selling wheat in its ear is valid because it is beneficial property; the hadith banning such sales applies only to salam.

This lesson examines a classic fiqh dispute: may a farmer sell his wheat while it is still in the ear, before threshing? The Hanafi school answers yes; Imam al-Shafi'i's later opinion says no, due to uncertainty. We will unpack the proofs on both sides and see how the Hanafis defend their position by distinguishing between spot sale and forward sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
The matn states 'صح' (it is valid) for selling wheat in its ear and other grains in their husks. Note that the ruling extends to broad beans, rice, and sesame.
The Hanafi school considers the grain as beneficial property (mal mutanaffa' bih).
2
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
The sharh mentions that Imam al-Shafi'i has two opinions; the newer (al-qawl al-jadid) holds the sale invalid because the object is concealed and its existence uncertain.
This objection is based on the principle that sales require the object to be known (ma'lum).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The text gives two proofs: (1) analogy to barley, which is unanimously permissible in its ear; (2) the hadith forbidding sale until whitening is interpreted as referring to salam (forward sale), not spot sale.
The hadith is narrated by Muslim and Ahmad; its wording 'نهى عن بيع السنبل حتى يبيض' is taken by Hanafis to mean salam.
4
Note the riba qualification
Even though the sale is valid, bartering wheat in its ear for the same kind of wheat in ear is prohibited because of potential riba (usury).
This is because wheat is a ribawi commodity; selling it like-for-like requires immediate hand-to-hand exchange without delay, and the ear introduces uncertainty in equality.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Bahr al-Raiq cites Al-Dhakhira that Abu Hanifa's view is 'asahh' (more correct), though some mashayikh prefer Muhammad al-Shaybani's view. The operative fatwa is permissibility.
This illustrates how later scholars weigh positions within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح بيع البر في سُنْبُلِهِ والباقلاء والأرز والسمسم في قشرها (وصح " بيع البرِّ في سُنْبُلِهِ والباقلاء والأرز والسمسم في قشرها) : أي بيعُ البُرِّ في سُنْبُلِهِ يجوز عندنا ، الشَّافِعِي قولان (۲) (1) وعن . (۳) وفي «البحر الرائق» نقلاً عن «الذخيرة» : «قول أبي حنيفة له أصح، ومـن المشايخ من اختار قول محمد الله وهو أعدل الأقوال» (۳). انتهى )) [١]قوله : وصح...الخ ؛ لأنه مال منتفع به، فيجوز بيعه في قشره، ولا يجوز بيعه بمثله من سنبل الحنطة ؛ لاحتمال الربا كما في «الفتح». وفي «الكفاية» نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي»: «إنّ الأصل أنه إذا باع شيئاً وهو في غلافه قبل الإزالة فإنّه لا يجوز إلا الحنطة في سنبلها ، وسائر الحبوب في سنابلها ، والذهب في ترابه، والفضة في ترابها ، بخلاف جنسه من الثمن. انتهى [٢] قوله : قولان ؛ في قوله الجديد : لا يصح ) ؛ لأنّ المعقود عليه مستور غائب عن البصر، ولا يُعْلَمُ وجوده ، فلا يجوز بيعه. ولنا : إنه حَب منتفع به، فيجوز بيعه في سنبله كالشعير، فإنّه يجوز بيعه في سنبله بالإجماع، وما رواه مسلم، وأحمد، وغيرهما أنه نهى عن بيع النخل حتى تزهو، وعن بيع السنبل حتى يبيض ، ويأمن العاهة )، المراد به السلم، يعني لا يجوز الإسلام فيه حتى يوجد بين الناس.
It is valid to sell wheat in its ear, and broad beans, rice, and sesame in their husks. (And it is valid to sell wheat in its ear, and broad beans, rice, and sesame in their husks): i.e., selling wheat in its ear is permissible in our school. For al-Shafi'i, there are two opinions. And in Al-Bahr al-Raiq, quoting from Al-Dhakhira: "The statement of Abu Hanifa is more correct, and some mashayikh chose the statement of Muhammad [al-Shaybani] and it is the most balanced of the statements." End. [1] His saying: "And it is valid..." etc.; because it is beneficial property, so its sale in its husk is permissible, and its sale for the same kind from wheat ear is not permissible due to the possibility of riba, as in Al-Fath. And in Al-Kifayah, quoting from Sharh al-Tahawi: "The principle is that if one sells something while it is in its covering before removal, it is not permissible except wheat in its ear, and other grains in their ears, and gold in its dirt, and silver in its dirt, contrary to its kind from the price." End. [2] His saying: "two opinions"; in his new opinion: it is not valid; because the object of sale is concealed, absent from sight, and its existence is not known, so its sale is not permissible. And for us: it is grain that is beneficial, so its sale in its ear is permissible like barley, for its sale in its ear is permissible by consensus. And what Muslim, Ahmad, and others narrated: that he forbade the sale of date palms until they ripen, and the sale of ears until they whiten and are safe from blight. Its intended meaning is salam (advance payment); i.e., it is not permissible to engage in salam until it exists among the people.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of selling wheat in its ear (sunbul) and other grains in their husks. The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa) is that such sales are valid because the grain is beneficial property (mal mutanaffa' bihi). The Shafi'i school has two opinions, the newer one deeming it invalid due to gharar (uncertainty) since the grain is hidden and its exact condition unknown. The Hanafi response argues by analogy to barley, which is unanimously permissible in its ear, and by interpreting the prophetic prohibition of selling ears until they whiten as referring to salam (forward sale), not spot sale. The passage also notes a qualification: selling wheat for equivalent wheat in ear is prohibited due to riba. The commentary cites Al-Bahr al-Raiq mentioning that Abu Hanifa's view is stronger, though some prefer Muhammad al-Shaybani's view. The text is a sharh expanding on the matn of Al-Wiqayah.

Key Terms
سنبل (sunbul): Ear of grain; the spike containing wheat or barley.
قشر (qishr): Husk, pod, or shell covering the grain (e.g., bean pod, rice husk).
سلم (salam): A forward sale with advanced payment for future delivery; here the hadith is interpreted as forbidding salam in unripe grain, not spot sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling wheat in its ear permissible despite a hadith prohibiting sale until it whitens?
A) The hadith is abrogated
B) The hadith refers to salam (advance payment), not spot sale
C) The hadith applies only to barley
D) The hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling grain in its ear because it is beneficial property, and the hadith prohibition is limited to salam contracts; however, bartering grain in ear for the same kind is prohibited due to riba.

Sale of Grains in Husks: The Hanafi Position and Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling with proof and Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling grains in their husks and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Grains in their husks are beneficial known property, thus their sale is valid; the prohibitory hadith refers to salam, not immediate sale.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the validity of selling grains while they are still in their ears or husks. The Hanafi school holds it valid, but the Shafi'i school disputes it. We will explore the legal reasoning and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the act: selling wheat, beans, rice, sesame in their husks. The ruling: sahih (valid). The commentary explains it is valid because it is mal muntafa' bihi (beneficial property).
The ruling is from the matn.
2
Understand the reasoning
The grain is known by its type and the ears are visible, so there is no gharar. The text analogizes to barley, which is sold by consensus in its ear.
Qiyas to barley.
3
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i (new opinion) says the sale is invalid because the object is concealed and unknown (gharar).
Gharar argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the grain is beneficial and known; the prohibitory hadith about ears is interpreted to refer to salam (forward sale), not immediate sale.
Dalīl naqlī reinterpreted.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Hanafi fiqh, one can buy wheat in the ear directly; under Shafi'i new opinion, it would be invalid, requiring threshing first.
Thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح بيع البر في سنبله والباقلاء والأرز والسمسم في قشرها (وصح " بيع البر في سنبله والباقلاء والأرز والسمسم في قشرها) : أي بيع البر في سنبله يجوز عندنا، الشافعي قولان. وفي «البحر الرائق» نقلاً عن «الذخيرة» : «قول أبي حنيفة له أصح، ومن المشايخ من اختار قول محمد وهو أعدل الأقوال». انتهى. [١]قوله : وصح...الخ ; لأنه مال منتفع به، فيجوز بيعه في قشره، ولا يجوز بيعه بمثله من سنبل الحنطة ; لاحتمال الربا كما في «الفتح». وفي «الكفاية» نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي»: «إن الأصل أنه إذا باع شيئاً وهو في غلافه قبل الإزالة فإنه لا يجوز إلا الحنطة في سنبلها، وسائر الحبوب في سنابلها، والذهب في ترابه، والفضة في ترابها، بخلاف جنسه من الثمن. انتهى. [٢] قوله : قولان ; في قوله الجديد : لا يصح ; لأن المعقود عليه مستور غائب عن البصر، ولا يعلم وجوده، فلا يجوز بيعه. ولنا : إنه حب منتفع به، فيجوز بيعه في سنبله كالشعير، فإنه يجوز بيعه في سنبله بالإجماع. وما رواه مسلم، وأحمد، وغيرهما أنه نهى عن بيع النخل حتى تزهو، وعن بيع السنبل حتى يبيض، ويأمن العاهة، المراد به السلم، يعني لا يجوز الإسلام فيه حتى يوجد بين الناس. [1] قوله : لا يجوز عنده ; ونقل صاحب ذخيرة العقبي عن «الدراية»: «له في بيع الباقلاء الأخضر وجهان، والمنصوص أنه لا يجوز، وهو ظاهر مذهبه، وقال كثير من أصحابه يجوز، وهو قولنا، وقول مالك وأحمد، والمختار عند الشافعي له جواز بيع الباقلاء الأخضر واللوز الرطب». انتهى.
The sale of wheat in its ear, and beans, rice, and sesame in their husk is valid. That is, the sale of wheat in its ear is permissible according to us. Al-Shafi'i has two opinions. In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq quoting from al-Dhakhira: 'The statement of Abu Hanifa is more correct, and some mashayikh chose the statement of Muhammad and it is the most balanced of the statements.' End. [1] His statement: 'It is valid...etc.' because it is beneficial property, so its sale in its husk is permissible, and its sale for an equivalent of wheat ear is not permissible due to the possibility of riba, as in al-Fath. In al-Kifaya quoting from Sharh al-Tahawi: 'The principle is that if one sells something while it is in its covering before removal, it is not permissible except wheat in its ear, and all grains in their ears, and gold in its dust, and silver in its dust, contrary to its own type of price.' End. [2] His statement: 'Two opinions'; in his new opinion: it is not valid, because the object of contract is concealed, hidden from sight, and its existence is not known, so its sale is not permissible. And for us: It is beneficial grain, so its sale in its ear is permissible like barley, for its sale in its ear is permissible by consensus. And what Muslim, Ahmad, and others narrated that he forbade the sale of palm trees until they ripen, and the sale of ears until they become white and are safe from blight – the intended meaning is salam, i.e., it is not permissible to enter into salam for it until it exists among the people. [1] His statement: 'It is not permissible according to him'; and the author of Dhakhirat al-‘Uqba narrated from al-Diraya: 'He has two opinions regarding the sale of fresh green beans. The prescribed opinion is that it is not permissible, and this is the apparent position of his madhhab. Many of his companions said it is permissible, and it is our statement, and the statement of Malik and Ahmad, and the chosen view according to al-Shafi'i is the permissibility of selling fresh green beans and fresh almonds.' End.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for selling grains (wheat, beans, rice, sesame) while still in their husks or ears. The Hanafi position, as stated by the author, is that such sales are valid. The passage first presents the matn (ruling) and then provides commentary. It opens by stating the rule: 'The sale of wheat in its ear, and beans, rice, and sesame in their husk is valid.' The commentator clarifies that 'according to us' refers to the Hanafi school. The passage then introduces a dispute: al-Shafi'i has two opinions. According to an excerpt from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq quoting al-Dhakhira, Abu Hanifa's opinion is considered more correct, though some scholars prefer Muhammad's (al-Shaybani's) view as the most balanced. The commentary then explains the reason for permissibility: because the grain is beneficial property (mal muntafa' bihi), so it can be sold in its husk. However, the commentator cautions that selling a similar quantity of wheat ear for wheat ear is not permissible due to the risk of riba (usury). The text then quotes from al-Kifaya via Sharh al-Tahawi, stating a general principle: selling an item while it is still in its covering is generally impermissible unless for specific exceptions like wheat in the ear, other grains in ears, gold and silver in their dust, provided the exchange is not with the same type of commodity. The passage then addresses al-Shafi'i's two opinions: in his new opinion (al-qawl al-jadid), he says it is invalid because the object is concealed and unknown. The Hanafi response is that it is beneficial grain, and by analogy to barley (which is sold by consensus in its ear), it is permissible. They also cite a hadith prohibiting the sale of palm trees until they become ripe and ears until they turn white and are safe from blight, but the commentator explains this hadith actually refers to salam (forward sale), meaning it is not permissible to make a salam contract for something that does not yet exist among people. Thus the hadith does not invalidate the immediate sale. Finally, the passage addresses a sub-ruling regarding fresh green beans (al-baqila' al-akhdar). It notes that according to some, the sale of fresh green beans is not permissible. The author of Dhakhirat al-‘Uqba reports from al-Diraya that there are two opinions within the Hanafi school: the preferred (mansus) opinion is that it is not permissible, but many companions say it is permissible, and this is also the view of Malik, Ahmad, and the chosen view of al-Shafi'i. The commentary thus highlights an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on this particular item. The overall Hanafi position is that the sale of grains in their husks is valid, but specific items like fresh green beans may have differences. The authority weight is from later commentary (mutakhkhirin), but the core ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
سنبلة (sunbula): An ear of grain (e.g., wheat ear) that contains the grains.
قشر (qishr): Husk or shell covering the grain or nut.
مال منتفع به (mal muntafa' bihi): Property that is beneficial and permissible to use, thus can be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the sale of wheat in its ear valid?
A) Because the hadith explicitly allows it.
B) Because the grain is beneficial property and known.
C) Because the ear is not considered a covering.
D) Because istihsan overrides qiyas.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the sale of grains in their husks because they are beneficial known property, and the prohibitory hadith applies only to salam.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Selling Fresh Green Beans
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on a specific grain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on selling fresh green beans and evaluate which is mufta bihi according to the text.

Core Idea
Fresh green beans are a subject of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the prescribed opinion prohibits, but many Hanafi scholars allow it.

The previous lesson established that grains in husks are generally saleable. However, the text notes an internal dispute among Hanafi scholars specifically regarding fresh green beans. This lesson explores that ikhtilaf and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific item
The text mentions al-baqila' al-akhdar (fresh green beans) as a case of internal difference.
2
State the two opinions
The prescribed opinion (mansus) is that it is not permissible. Many companions said it is permissible.
From Dhakhirat al-Uqba quoting al-Diraya.
3
Determine authority
The text states that the prescribed opinion is the apparent position of the madhhab, but many companions allowed it, and this is also the view of Malik, Ahmad, and Shafi'i's chosen view.
Cross-madhhab alignment.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The text implies the preferred opinion is the prohibition, but there is flexibility. Some later scholars may adopt the permissive view.
The commentary says 'the prescribed opinion is that it is not permissible' but adds that many allowed it.
5
Apply the legal principle
Recall the general principle from al-Kifaya: only certain items in coverings are excepted. Fresh green beans may fall under the general prohibition, but the dispute shows the principle is not absolute.
Link to the broader rule.

Sale of Nuts in Their First Shell: Walnuts, Almonds, Pistachios
Ruling on sale of nuts in their first shell with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling nuts in their first shell and explain the dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding gharar.

Core Idea
Selling nuts in their first shell is valid in Hanafi fiqh, despite al-Shafi'i's objection.

This short passage from a Hanafi matn addresses a common commercial transaction: selling nuts while they are still in their outer (first) shell. The commentator explains why the author specifically mentions the 'first shell' — it is a point of difference with the Shafi'i school. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi position on permissible uncertainty (gharar) in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity and its condition
Note that the ruling covers walnuts, almonds, and pistachios sold while still in their first (outer) shell (e.g., green hull of walnut).
The first shell is the soft, often inedible covering before the hard inner shell.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its scope
The Hanafi ruling is that such a sale is valid (sahih). There is no distinction between first and second shell in validity.
The passage highlights that the second shell is agreed upon; the first shell is the locus of dispute.
3
Identify the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i is reported to have a dispute on the first shell. The likely basis is gharar (uncertainty) because the kernel is hidden.
The commentary does not detail the Shafi'i proof, but the Hanafi response would rely on the fact that the nut is known by type and the shell is not a barrier to benefit.
4
Understand the consensus on the second shell
Both schools agree that selling nuts in their second (hard) shell is valid, as the kernel remains protected but the gharar is minimal.
This consensus reinforces the Hanafi view that the first shell does not introduce impermissible uncertainty.
5
Apply the ruling to similar products
Consider other nuts or fruits with outer coverings (e.g., peanuts, pomegranates). The principle: if the inner fruit is known and accessible after removing the shell, the sale is valid.

Sale of Fruit: Validity Before and After Ripeness
Ruling on selling fruit before or after ripeness appears

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before ripeness appears and identify the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school validates selling visible fruit even before ripeness, as it is known property, and the hadith does not require ripeness.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya addresses a common question in sales: can fruit be sold before it ripens? The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim, affirms its validity as long as the fruit is visible. Understanding this ruling clarifies how the school balances textual evidence with practical market needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What act is being ruled upon? The sale of fruit. Determine the hukm: is it valid or invalid? The passage states it is absolutely valid whether ripeness has appeared or not.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof cited. The commentator references a hadith: 'If Allah withholds the fruit, why do you consider the wealth of your brother lawful?' This hadith is from Muslim and Bukhari.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The commentator argues that the hadith implies permissibility once the fruit is safe (i.e., appears). If prohibition until ripeness were intended, the hadith would have mentioned threshing.
The hadith is used in a general sense; the Hanafi school does not add a condition of ripeness.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Is there disagreement? Yes, a minority view says sale is not permissible before ripeness appears. The commentator labels this view weaker.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Which position is mufta bihi? The first view (valid absolutely) is more correct (al-awwal aṣaḥḥ). This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع ثمرة لم يبد صلاحها أو قد بدا [2] قوله : وبيع ... الخ ; أي وصح بيع ثمرة ظاهرة ; لأن بيعها قبل الظهور لا يصح باتفاق. كذا في «المنح» ، فالتنوين في قوله : ثمرة ; ينبغي أن يكون للعهد، وهذا البيع مطلقاً صح بدا صلاحها أو لم يبد ; لأنه مال معلوم متقوم في الحال أو في المال، وقيل : لا يجوز قبل بدو صلاحها، والأول أصح. ألا ترى إلى ما رواه مسلم والبخاري بإسناده عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إذا منع الله الثمر فيم تستحل مال أخيك، فيكون حجة لنا في اشتراط وجود المسلم فيه من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، ولو أجري على إطلاقه كان حجة لنا أيضاً في هذا الموضع ; لأنه يقتضي جواز بيعه بعدما ابيض مطلقا من غير قيد بالفرك، ولو كان كما قاله لقال حتى يفرك.
And the sale of fruit whose ripeness has not appeared or has appeared. [2] His statement: 'And the sale... etc.' i.e., the sale of an apparent fruit is valid, because its sale before appearance is not valid by agreement. This is in al-Minah. So the tanwin in his words 'fruit' should be for definite reference (al-'ahd). This sale is absolutely valid whether its ripeness has appeared or not, because it is known property, valuable in the present or in property. And it is said: it is not permissible before ripeness becomes apparent, but the first is more correct. Do you not see what Muslim and al-Bukhari narrated with their chain from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'If Allah withholds the fruit, why do you consider the wealth of your brother lawful?' So it is a proof for us regarding the condition of existence of the object of salam from the time of contract until the time of delivery. And if it were taken in its absolute sense, it would be a proof for us in this place as well, because it necessitates the permissibility of selling it after it becomes white absolutely without the condition of threshing. Had it been as he said, he would have said 'until it is threshed'.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage concerns the sale of fruit, specifically whether it can be sold before or after its ripeness becomes apparent. The commentator explains that the sale of a fruit that is already visible (apparent) is valid, because selling it before it appears is not valid by consensus. The tanwin on 'thamaratan' (fruit) indicates it refers to a specific type: fruit that is already visible. The ruling is that the sale is valid regardless of whether ripeness has appeared or not, because it is known property with value either currently or potentially. There is a minority view that it is not permissible before ripeness appears, but the author says the first view (valid) is more correct. The passage then cites a hadith narrated by Muslim and al-Bukhari: 'If Allah withholds the fruit, why do you consider the wealth of your brother lawful?' The commentator uses this hadith as evidence for the requirement that the subject matter must exist at the time of contract in salam (forward sale). However, if applied generally, it also supports the permissibility of selling fruit after it becomes white (mature) without requiring threshing, because the hadith implies that once the fruit is safe from blight (i.e., appears), it is lawful to sell. The commentator argues that if the hadith were meant to prohibit until threshing, it would have said 'until it is threshed'.

Key Terms
ثمرة ظاهرة (thamara zahira): Fruit that is visible (apparent), as opposed to hidden fruit not yet appeared.
المسلم فيه (al-muslim fihi): The object of a salam (forward sale) contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, as long as the fruit is visible/apparent.
C) Only if it is sold with the tree.
D) Only if the buyer agrees to take the risk.

Key Takeaway
The sale of visible fruit is valid in the Hanafi school even before ripeness appears, based on the hadith indicating that once the fruit is safe, it is lawful to sell.

In this brief passage from the matn and its commentary, the author clarifies the ruling on a specific type of sale: nuts still in their undeveloped first peel.
