Selling Fruit Before Ripeness: The Hanafi Ruling and Proof from Hadith
Ruling on selling fruit before/after ripeness with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before full ripeness and explain how the hadith 'If Allah withholds the fruit...' supports that ruling.

Core Idea
A sale of fruit before full ripeness is valid if the fruit is deliverable; the hadith proves existence is key, not a fixed ripeness stage.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on selling fruit before or after ripeness. The matn states the permissibility with the condition of cutting. The author then cites a hadith from Muslim and Bukhari to support the requirement that the sold item must exist and be deliverable. We will unpack the proof and see how it leads to a different ruling from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Ruling
What is being sold? Fruit before or after ripeness. The Hanafi ruling: the sale is valid (sahih) if the fruit is deliverable and a condition of cutting is included.
This is a sale of a future crop or an existing crop that is not yet fully ripe.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author provides the hadith: 'If Allah withholds the fruit, how do you consider the wealth of your brother lawful?' (Bukhari and Muslim). This is an ahad hadith, so zanni al-thubut.
The hadith is used to establish the principle that the sold item must exist at the time of contract and delivery.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the hadith prove the Hanafi ruling? The hadith highlights the necessity of the fruit's existence; if the fruit is withheld (destroyed), one cannot lawfully take the buyer's money. Thus, what matters is existence, not a specific stage like rubbing. The Hanafi school infers that once the fruit appears (e.g., whitens) and is safe from blight, it can be sold with the condition of cutting.
The Shafi'i school interprets the same hadith to require full ripeness (buduww al-salah). The difference hinges on the definition of 'fruit' in the hadith.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that fruit may only be sold after full ripeness (e.g., sweetness) and requires a condition of rubbing. The Hanafi school permits sale after whitening (a sign of existence) with condition of cutting.
The text criticizes the Shafi'i view by saying: 'if it were as he said, he would have said until it is rubbed.'
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi in the school because the hadith's general implication (mutlaq) allows sale once the fruit exists, without extra conditions like rubbing. The rubbing condition is not found in the hadith text.
This is a clear example of how different usul principles (e.g., interpreting 'amm vs. requiring takhsis) lead to different fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع ثمرة لم يبد صلاحها أو قد بدا. ألا ترى إلى ما رواه مسلم والبخاري بإسناده عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إذا منع الله الثمر فبم تستحل مال أخيك» فيكون حجة لنا في اشتراط وجود المسلم فيه من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، ولو أجري على إطلاقه كان حجةً لنا أيضاً في هذا الموضع ؛ لأنه يقتضي جواز بيعه بعدما ابيض مطلقا من غير قيد بالفرك، ولو كان كما قاله لقال حتى يفرك.
And the sale of a fruit whose ripeness has not appeared or has appeared. Do you not see what Muslim and al-Bukhari narrated with their chain from the Prophet ﷺ: 'If Allah withholds the fruit, how do you consider the wealth of your brother lawful?' So it is a proof for us regarding the requirement that the sold item exists from the time of contract until the time of delivery. And if it were taken generally, it would be a proof for us in this place as well, because it necessitates the permissibility of selling it after it has become white, without restriction of rubbing. And if it were as he said, he would have said 'until it is rubbed.'
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The matn states the permissibility of selling fruit whether ripeness has appeared or not, under certain conditions (like cutting). The author then brings a hadith from Muslim and Bukhari: 'If Allah withholds the fruit...' to support the Hanafi position that the object of sale must exist and be deliverable. The hadith is used to argue that the buyer must be able to take possession. The author further argues that the hadith, taken generally, allows sale after the fruit whitens (a sign of ripeness) without the extra condition of rubbing required by the Shafi'i school. The hadith thus serves as a proof for the Hanafi view that the condition for validity is the existence of the fruit, not the full ripeness as defined by the Shafi'is.

Key Terms
بدو صلاحها (buduww salahiha): The appearance of ripeness, defined differently by each school: Hanafi defines it as safety from blight; Shafi'i defines it as the appearance of sweetness/ripeness.
فرك (fark): Rubbing; a condition required by the Shafi'i school for selling fruit, i.e., that the fruit is firm enough to be rubbed after picking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling fruit before full ripeness if the fruit exists and is deliverable, supported by the hadith that emphasizes existence over a specific ripeness stage.

Obligation to Cut Fruit After Sale and Invalid Condition
Ruling on obligation to cut and invalidity of condition to leave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on immediately cutting fruit after purchase and explain why a condition of leaving it on the tree invalidates the sale.

Core Idea
A sale of fruit requires immediate cutting; stipulating the fruit remain on the tree is a prohibited transaction within a transaction.

In Hanafi sales law, the transfer of ownership requires complete separation of the sold item from the seller's property. When fruit is sold on a tree, the buyer must cut it immediately. This lesson examines why this obligation exists and why a condition to leave the fruit on the tree renders the sale invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
State that it is obligatory (wajib) for the buyer to cut the fruit immediately after the sale.
This applies whether the sale was absolute or with a condition of cutting.
2
Recognize the invalid condition
Understand that if the buyer stipulates the fruit remain on the tree, the sale becomes fasid (invalid/defective).
Fasid means the contract is flawed but can be corrected by removing the condition.
3
Analyze the first rationale: occupation of another's property
The condition involves occupying the seller's property (the tree) without a valid right, which is not required by the sale.
The buyer's fruit on the seller's tree is akin to using another's property.
4
Analyze the second rationale: transaction within a transaction
The condition effectively combines a sale with a separate contract (ijarah or i'arah), which is prohibited as 'safqah fi safqah'.
If the buyer benefits from the tree's use (e.g., shade), it is ijarah; if no benefit, it is i'arah.
5
Conclude the practical implication
The sale is fasid; the invalid condition must be removed for the contract to become valid.
Hanafi law allows correction of fasid contracts by eliminating the defective condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب قطعها، وشرط تركها على الشجر يفسد البيع. [1] قوله : ويجب قطعها ؛ في الحال ؛ ليتفرغ ملك البائع عن ملكه، وهذا إذا اشتراه مطلقاً أو بشرط القطع. [2] قوله : يفسد البيع ؛ لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه الأخذ، وهو شغل ملك الغير، أو لأنه صفقة في صفقة ؛ لأنه إجارة في بيع إن كان للمنفعة من الثمن، أو إعارة في بيع إن لم يكن لها حصة منه. قيل في الوجه الثاني : إنّ كلاً من الإجارة والإعارة غير صحيح، فكيف يصح أن
And it is obligatory to cut it, and stipulating its remaining on the tree invalidates the sale. [1] His statement: 'And it is obligatory to cut it' means immediately, so that the seller's ownership becomes free from his ownership, and this is when he buys it absolutely or with the condition of cutting. [2] His statement: 'invalidates the sale' because it is a condition not required by the transaction, and it involves occupying another's property, or because it is a transaction within a transaction; for it is an ijarah in sale if there is benefit from the price, or an i'arah in sale if there is no share of it. It is said in the second aspect: each of ijarah and i'arah is invalid, so how can it be valid that...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn with its commentary. The matn states two rulings: (1) It is obligatory for the buyer to cut the fruit immediately after purchase, that is, remove it from the tree, so that the seller's ownership is completely separated. This applies whether the sale was absolute or with a condition of cutting. (2) If the buyer stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree, the sale is invalid (fasid). The commentary gives two reasons: first, it is a condition that does not follow from the transaction and involves occupying another's property (since the tree belongs to the seller and the buyer cannot keep his fruit on it without permission). Second, it is considered a combination of sale with a separate contract (like ijarah or i'arah), which is a prohibited 'transaction within a transaction' (safqah fi safqah). The note ends mid-sentence discussing the invalidity of ijarah and i'arah.

Key Terms
يفسد البيع (yufsid al-bay'): The sale becomes fasid (invalid/defective) due to a condition that contradicts the essence of the contract.
صفقة في صفقة (safqah fi safqah): A transaction within a transaction; a prohibited combination of two contracts in one sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a condition that leads to uncertainty (gharar).
B) Because it involves occupying the seller's property without permission.
C) Because the fruit will spoil if left on the tree.
D) Because it is a delay in delivery.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a condition that delays transfer of possession or creates a separate contract within the sale invalidates the contract.

Selling Unripe Fruit: Hanafi Position and Legal Device
Extended inter-madhhab dispute with legal device

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of ripeness (badu al-salah) and state the legal device (hila) for selling unripe fruit to avoid disagreement.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines ripeness as safety from blight, not sweetness, allowing more flexibility in selling fruit before full maturity.

The sale of fruit before it ripens is a classic point of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi school and other madhahib. This passage from al-Fath lays out the points of agreement and disagreement, focusing on the different definitions of 'ripeness' and a clever legal device (hila) to sidestep the dispute. By understanding this, you will grasp how Hanafi fiqh balances textual rulings with practical needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the points of agreement
List the four scenarios where all schools agree on the ruling: selling before appearance (impermissible), selling before ripeness with condition of leaving (impermissible), selling before ripeness with condition of cutting (permissible), and selling after ripeness (permissible).
This sets the common ground between Hanafi and others.
2
Analyze the core disagreement on the definition of 'ripeness'
Contrast the Hanafi definition (safety from blight) with the Shafi'i definition (physical sweetness/maturity). Understand that this definitional difference is the root of the dispute.
The dispute only arises when the fruit is sold before ripeness without a cutting condition.
3
Examine the Hanafi ruling on fruit with no present benefit
Consider the case where the fruit is not even useful as fodder. Note the difference among Hanafi scholars, and that the correct view permits sale because the fruit becomes beneficial 'in the next stage' (thānī al-ḥāl).
This shows the Hanafi principle of considering potential benefit.
4
Apply the legal device (hila) for early pears
The text provides a hila: sell the pear along with the leaves of the tree, making the fruit a follower of the leaves. This makes the sale permissible by agreement of all mashāyikh.
The hila leverages the principle that a subordinate (tābi') takes the ruling of its principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في «الفتح»: «لا خلاف في عدم جواز بيع الثَّمار قبل أن تظهر، ولا في عدم جوازه بعد الظهور قبل بدو الصلاح بشرط الترك، ولا في جوازه قبل بدو الصلاح بشرط القطع فيما ينتفع به، ولا في الجواز بعد بدو الصلاح. لكن بدو الصلاح عندنا أن تؤمن العاهة والفساد، وعند الشافعي له هو ظهور النضج وبدو الحلاوة، والخلافُ إنّما هو في بيعها قبل بدو الصلاح على الخلاف في معناه لا بشرط القطع، فعند الشافعي ومالك وأحمد  لا يجوز. وعندنا إن كان بحال لا ينتفع به في الأكل ولا في علف الدواب فيه خلاف بين المشايخ قيل : لا يجوز، ونسبه قاضي خان العامة مشايخنا، والصحيح أنه يجوز ؛ لأنه مال منتفع به في ثاني الحال، وإن لم يكن منتفعاً به في الحال. والحيلة في جوازه باتفاق المشايخ أن يبيع الكمثرى أوّل ما تخرج مع أوراقِ الشجر، فيجوز فيها تبعاً للأوراق، كأنه ورق كله، وإن كان بحيث ينتفع به ولو علفاً للدواب، فالبيع جائز باتفاق أهل المذهب بشرط القطع. ولو بزر بعضُها دونَ بعض لا يصح في ظاهر المذهب، وصححه السَّرَخسي مطلقاً». انتهى وأفتى الحَلْوَانِيُّ بالجواز لو الخارج أكثر.
And he said in al-Fath: 'There is no disagreement about the impermissibility of selling fruits before they appear, nor about its impermissibility after appearance before ripeness with a condition of leaving, nor about its permissibility before ripeness with a condition of cutting in what is beneficial, nor about permissibility after ripeness. But ripeness (badu al-salah) in our view is that blight and corruption are safe, while according to al-Shafi'i it is the appearance of maturity and sweetness. The disagreement is only about selling before ripeness based on the disagreement over its meaning, not with a condition of cutting. According to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, it is not permissible. According to us, if it is in a state where it is not beneficial for eating nor for animal fodder, there is disagreement among the mashayikh: some said it is not permissible, and Qadi Khan attributed it to the general body of our scholars, but the correct view is that it is permissible, because it is a beneficial property in the next stage, even if not beneficial in the present. The legal device for its permissibility by agreement of the mashayikh is that one sells the pear when it first appears with the leaves of the tree, so it becomes permissible as a follower of the leaves, as if it were entirely leaves. And if it is in a state where it is beneficial, even as fodder for animals, the sale is permissible by agreement of the school with a condition of cutting. And if some of them ripen and some not, it is not valid in the apparent school, but al-Sarakhsi corrected it absolutely.' End. And al-Halwani gave a fatwa of permissibility when the ripened ones are more.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab dispute and ruling. The author quotes al-Fath to explain the areas of agreement and disagreement regarding fruit sales. All agree on the impermissibility of selling fruit before it appears, and after appearance before ripeness if the fruit is left on the tree. All also agree on the permissibility of selling after ripeness or before ripeness with the condition of cutting. The key disagreement is over the definition of 'ripeness' (badu al-salah) and the permissibility of selling before ripeness without the condition of cutting. The Hanafi school defines ripeness as safety from blight, while the Shafi'i school defines it as physical sweetness/ripeness. The Hanafi school permits selling before ripeness without a cutting condition in certain cases, even if the fruit is not yet beneficial, because it will become beneficial soon. A legal device (hila) is then provided to avoid disagreement: sell early pears along with the leaves of the tree, making the fruit a follower of the leaves. If the fruit is already beneficial (even as fodder), sale is permissible with a cutting condition. There is also a discussion about mixed ripeness: if some fruits are ripe and some not, the sale is invalid in the apparent school, but al-Sarakhsi corrects it absolutely. Al-Halwani's fatwa permits it if the majority are ripe.

Key Terms
بدو الصلاح (buduww al-salah): The point when the fruit is safe from blight (affah) and corruption (fasad), according to Hanafis.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal device to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding disagreement, here selling pears with leaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal device (hila) for selling early pears to make the sale universally permissible?
A) Selling them with the condition of cutting.
B) Selling them along with the leaves of the tree.
C) Selling them only after they ripen completely.
D) Selling them only as fodder for animals.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling unripe fruit under conditions, notably through a hila that pairs the fruit with leaves, based on a definition of ripeness as safety from blight.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Fruits: Conditions and Ripening (Inter-Madhhab Dispute)
Inter-madhhab dispute on fruit sale validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling fruit before ripening without a cutting condition, state the opposing view, and identify the practical consequence of the differing definitions of badw al-salah.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition of ripening as safety from blight makes pre-ripening sale valid, unlike other schools' requirement of full sweetness.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute on the sale of fruit before ripening. While all schools agree on four scenarios, they diverge on selling before ripening without a cutting condition, due to differing definitions of 'badw al-salah'. The Hanafi school, focusing on the fruit's utility as wealth, permits such sales, whereas the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools prohibit them. This lesson unpacks the consensus, the contested scenario, and the Hanafi justification.

Guided Path
1
State the four agreed-upon scenarios
List the four scenarios from al-Fath to recognize the areas of consensus: selling before appearance (invalid), after appearance with leave condition (invalid), before ripening with cutting condition (valid if useful), after ripening (valid).
This ijma forms the baseline for the dispute.
2
Identify the disputed scenario
Recognize that the ikhtilaf is about selling before badw al-salah without a cutting condition, which falls outside the consensus.
The text explicitly says 'الخلاف إنما هو في بيعها قبل بدو الصلاح'.
3
Define badw al-salah per each school
Hanafi: safety from blight (تؤمن العاهة والفساد). Others: full ripeness and sweetness (ظهور النضج وبدو الحلاوة).
The definition directly determines the ruling.
4
Follow the Hanafi argument
The fruit is considered wealth (مال) because it can be used as fodder or has future utility. Therefore, the sale is valid even before ripening.
Hanafi response: مال منتفع به في ثاني الحال.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi, farmers can sell unripe fruit without requiring immediate cutting, facilitating trade. In other schools, such sales are invalid, forcing farmers to wait.
This illustrates the impact of definitional differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب قطعها، وشرط تركها على الشجر يفسد البيع. وقال في «الفتح»: «لا خلاف في عدم جواز بيع الثمار قبل أن تظهر، ولا في عدم جوازه بعد الظهور قبل بدو الصلاح بشرط الترك، ولا في جوازه قبل بدو الصلاح بشرط القطع فيما ينتفع به، ولا في الجواز بعد بدو الصلاح. لكن بدو الصلاح عندنا أن تؤمن العاهة والفساد، وعند الشافعي [؟] هو ظهور النضج وبدو الحلاوة، والخلاف إنما هو في بيعها قبل بدو الصلاح على الخلاف في معناه لا بشرط القطع، فعند الشافعي ومالك وأحمد لا يجوز. وعندنا إن كان بحال لا ينتفع به في الأكل ولا في علف الدواب فيه خلاف بين المشايخ قيل: لا يجوز، ونسبه قاضي خان [؟] العامة مشايخنا، والصحيح أنه يجوز؛ لأنه مال منتفع به في ثاني الحال، وإن لم يكن منتفعا به في الحال.
It is obligatory to cut them, and the condition of leaving them on the tree invalidates the sale. And he said in al-Fath: 'There is no disagreement regarding the impermissibility of selling fruits before they appear, nor regarding its impermissibility after appearance before the beginning of ripeness (badw al-salah) with the condition of leaving, nor regarding its permissibility before badw al-salah with the condition of cutting in what can be benefited from, nor regarding its permissibility after badw al-salah. However, badw al-salah according to us is that the blight and corruption are safe, while according to al-Shafi'i it is the appearance of ripeness and the beginning of sweetness. The disagreement is only regarding selling them before badw al-salah, based on the disagreement about its meaning, not with the condition of cutting. Thus according to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, it is not permissible. And according to us, if it is in a state where it cannot be benefited from in eating nor in fodder for animals, there is a difference among the mashayikh: some said it is not permissible – Qadi Khan attributed this to the general body of our mashayikh – but the correct view is that it is permissible, because it is wealth that can be benefited from in the second instance, even if not benefited from in the present.'
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of selling fruits before ripening (بدو الصلاح). It begins by stating two fundamental rulings: (1) when fruit is sold with the condition that the buyer must cut it immediately, the sale is valid and cutting is obligatory; (2) if the seller stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree, the sale is invalid. The text then quotes al-Fath to establish the areas of consensus: all schools agree that selling fruits before they appear is invalid, selling after appearance but before ripening with a leave condition is invalid, selling before ripening with a cutting condition is valid for useful fruits, and selling after ripening is valid. The dispute centers on the definition of بدو الصلاح. For the Hanafis, it means the fruit becomes safe from blight (تؤمن العاهة والفساد), while for Shafi'i, Malik, and Hanbal, it means the fruit is fully ripe and sweet. Consequently, the schools differ on selling before بدو الصلاح without a cutting condition: the three imams prohibit it, but the Hanafis allow it if the fruit has any utility, even as animal fodder. Among the Hanafi mashayikh, there is a difference on fruits with no utility at all; the correct position is that it is permissible because it is wealth (مال) that will be beneficial later. Thus the Hanafi school adopts a more expansive definition of ripeness, focusing on safety from damage rather than full sweetness.

Key Terms
بدو الصلاح (badw al-ṣalāḥ): The point at which fruit is safe from blight and corruption (تؤمن العاهة والفساد); the Hanafi definition of ripening for purposes of permissibility of sale.
شرط القطع (sharṭ al-qaṭʿ): A condition in the sale contract that the buyer must cut the fruit immediately; makes the sale valid.
شرط الترك (sharṭ al-tark): A condition that the fruit be left on the tree after sale; invalidates the sale because it encumbers the seller's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the definition of بدو الصلاح (beginning of ripeness) for fruit?
A) The fruit becomes sweet and fully ripe.
B) The fruit becomes safe from blight and corruption.
C) The fruit appears on the tree.
D) The fruit is large enough to be sold.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling fruit before full ripeness if the fruit has any utility, because بدو الصلاح is defined as safety from blight, not sweetness.

Selling Early Fruits with Leaves: The Hanafi Legal Trick
Legal device to permit selling early fruits by including leaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal trick for selling early fruits, explain its condition, and identify the mufta bihi position regarding mixed cutting.

Core Idea
Sell the fruit as a follower of the leaves to make the contract valid.

Early fruits that lack benefit cannot normally be sold. However, the Hanafi school provides a practical legal device: sell them together with the leaves, making the leaves the primary object. This lesson explains the trick, its conditions, and a correction by Imam al-Sarakhsi that resolves a minor disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Early fruits (e.g., pears) have no current benefit and cannot be sold independently. This creates a legal obstacle.
Benefit (manfa'ah) is a condition for sale in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Recognize the Legal Trick
Sell the fruit together with the leaves of the tree. The leaves are clearly beneficial property, and the fruit becomes a follower (taba'). The entire contract is treated as if for the leaves.
This relies on the principle that incidental items follow the primary item in sale.
3
Understand the Condition for Beneficial Fruit
If the fruit has some benefit (e.g., as animal fodder), the sale is valid only if a condition of cutting (shart al-qat') is included at the time of contract.
The cutting condition ensures the fruit is separated and becomes independent property.
4
Note the Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
If some fruit is cut and some left, the 'apparent madhhab' (zahir al-madhhab) rules the sale invalid. However, Imam al-Sarakhsi corrected this, holding the sale valid absolutely.
Al-Sarakhsi's view is considered a tashih (correction) within the school.
5
Know the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is al-Sarakhsi's absolute validity. The sale is valid whether the fruit is beneficial or not, as long as leaves are included.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحيلة في جوازه باتفاق المشايخ أن يبيع الكمثرى أول ما تخرج مع أوراق الشجر، فيجوز فيها تبعا للأوراق، كأنه ورق كله، وإن كان بحيث ينتفع به ولو علفا للدواب، فالبيع جائز باتفاق أهل المذهب بشرط القطع. ولو [؟] بعضها دون بعض لا يصح في ظاهر المذهب، وصححه السرخسي مطلقا.
And the legal trick for its permissibility, by agreement of the mashayikh, is that one sells the pear when it first emerges along with the leaves of the tree; it becomes permissible as a follower of the leaves, as if it is all leaves. And if it is such that it can be benefited from, even as fodder for animals, then the sale is permissible by agreement of the people of the madhhab with the condition of cutting. And if [؟] some of them without some, it is not valid in the apparent madhhab, but al-Sarakhsi corrected it absolutely.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides a practical legal device (حيلة) to sell fruits that have no current benefit. The example given is pears (كمثرى) when they first appear. By including the leaves of the tree, the fruit is sold as a subsidiary (تبعاً) to the leaves, which are clearly useful property (متمول). The entire sale is treated as if it is for the leaves, making it valid by consensus of the mashayikh. If the fruit later has some utility (e.g., as fodder), the sale is also permissible with the condition of cutting. The passage then discusses a scenario where some of the fruit is cut and some left: in the apparent madhhab (ظاهر المذهب), such a sale is not valid, but Imam al-Sarakhsi corrected this ruling and declared it absolutely valid. This shows the flexibility within the Hanafi school to accommodate necessary transactions.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-ḥīlah): A legal stratagem or device to achieve a permissible result that would otherwise be prohibited or invalid. Here, selling fruit with leaves to make the sale valid.
تبعا للأوراق (tabaʿan li-l-awrāq): The fruit is sold as a follower of the leaves; the primary object of sale is the leaves, and the fruit is incidental.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal trick (حيلة) for selling pears when they first emerge?
A) Sell them with a condition that they will be cut later.
B) Sell them together with the leaves of the tree.
C) Sell them only after they become fully ripe.
D) Sell them as a separate contract for each fruit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal trick allows selling early fruits by including leaves, with the fruit as a follower; al-Sarakhsi's correction makes the sale absolutely valid even in mixed cutting scenarios.
